                               CHAPTER I





unless you know that by take a few turning in any direction and

walk for five minute you would inevitably come into one of the

great, clash, shriek thoroughfare of London, you might think that

Romney Place, Chelsea, be situate in some world-forget cathedral

city. why it be call a “place,” history do not record. it be simply

a street, or double terrace, the quiet, sedate, most unruffled,

most old-maidish street you can imagine. its primness be painful. it be

rigorously close to organ-grinder and german band; and itinerant

vendor of coal would have as much hope of sell their ware inside

the British Museum as of attract custom in Romney Place by their

raucous appeal. little dog on lead and lazy persian cat be its

_genii loci_. it consist of a double row of little early victorian

house, each have a basement protect by area railing, an entrance

floor reach by a prim little flight of step, and an upper floor.

three little house close one end of the street, a sleepy little modern

church mask the other. each house have a tiny back garden which, on the

south side, owe to the gradual slope of the ground riverward, be on a

level with the basement floor and thus on a low level than the street.

some of the house on this south side be construct with a studio on

the garden level run the whole height of the house. a sloping

skylight in the roof admit the precious north light, and a french

window lead on to the garden. a gallery run round the studio, on a

level and in communication with the entrance floor; and from this to the

ground be a spiral staircase.



from such a gallery do Tommy Burgrave, one November afternoon, look

down into the studio of Clementina Wing. she be not alone, as he have

expect; for in front of an easel carry a nearly finish portrait

stand the original, a pretty, dainty girl accompany by a well-dress,

well-feed, bullet-head, bull-neck, commonplace young man. Clementina,

on hear footstep, look up.



“I’m sorry——” he begin. “they don’t tell I——”



“don’t run away. we’re quite through with the sitting. come down. this

be Mr. Burgrave, a neighbour of mine,” she explain. “try to paint,

too—Miss Etta Concannon—Captain Hilyard.”



she perform perfunctory introduction. the group linger round the

portrait for a few moment, and then the girl and the young man go

away. Clementina scrutinise the picture, sigh, push the easel to a

corner of the studio and draw up another one into the light. Tommy sit

on the model-throne and light a cigarette.



“who’s the man?”



“This?” ask Clementina, point to the new portrait, that of a stout

and comfortable-look gentleman.



“no. the man with Miss Etta something. I like the name Etta.”



“he’s engage to she. I tell you his name, Captain Hilyard. he call

for she. I don’t like he,” reply Clementina, whose language be

abrupt.



“he look rather a brute—and she’s as pretty as paint. it must be awful

hard line on a girl when she get hold of a bad lot.”



“you’re right,” she say, gather up palette and brush. then she

turn on he. “what be you waste precious daylight for? why ben’t

you at work?”



“I feel rather limp this afternoon, and want stimulate. so I think

I’d come in. can I stay?”



“oh Lord, yes, you can stay,” say Clementina, dab a vicious bit of

paint on the canvas and step back to observe the effect. “though you

limp young man who need stimulate make I tired—as tired,” she add,

with another stroke, “as this horrible fat man’s trouser.”



“I don’t see why you need have paint his trouser. why not have make

he half-length?”



“because he’s the kind of cheesemonger that want value for his money.

if I cut he off at the waist he would think he be cheat. he pay to

have his hideous trouser paint, and so I paint they.”



“but you’re an artist, Clementina.”



“I get over the disease long ago,” she reply grimly, still dab at

the crease of the abominable and unmentionable garment. “a woman of my

age and appearance haven’t any illusion leave. if she have, she’s a fool.

I paint portrait for money, so that one of these day I may be able to

retire from trade and be a lady. bah! art! look at that!”



“hi! stop!” laugh Tommy, as soon as the result of the fresh

brush-stroke be reveal. “don’t make the infernal thing more hideous

than they be already.”



“that’s where I get ‘character,’” she say sarcastically. “People like

it. they say ‘how rugged! how strong! how expressive!’ look at the fat,

self-satisfied old pig!—and they pay I in guineas where the rest of

you high artistic people get shilling. if I have the courage of my

conviction and paint he with a snout, they’d pay I in lac of

rupee. art! don’t talk of it. I’m sick of it.”



“all right,” say Tommy, calmly puff away at his cigarette, “I willn’t.

art be long and the talk about it be long, thank God. so it will

keep.”



he be a fresh-faced, fair-haired boy of two-and-twenty, and the

charter libertine of Clementina’s exclusive studio. his uncle, Ephraim

Quixtus, have marry a distant relation of Clementina, so, in a vague

way, she be a family connection. to this fact he owe acquaintance with

she—indeed, he have know she dimly from boyhood; but his intimacy he

owe to a certain charm and candour of youth which find he favour in

she not very tolerant eye.



he sit on the model-throne, clasp his knee, and, wonderingly,

admiringly, watch her paint. for all her cynical depreciation of her

art, she be a portrait-painter of high rank, possess the

portrait-painter’s magical gift of get at essential, of splash

the very soul, miserable or noble, of the subject upon the canvas. she

have a rough, brilliant method, direct and uncompromising as her speech.

to see she at work be at once Tommy Burgrave’s delight and his despair.

have she be a young and pretty woman, his masculine vanity might have

smart. but Clementina, with her ugliness, gruffness, and untidiness,

scarcely rank as a woman in his disingenuous mind. you couldn’t

possibly fall in love with she; no one could ever have fall in love

with she. and she, of course, have never have the remotest idea of fall

in love with anybody. to his boyish fancy, Clementina in love be a

grotesque conception. besides, she might be any age. he decide that she

must be about fifty. but when you make allowance for her gruffness and

eccentricity, you find that she be a good sort—and, there be no

doubt about it, she could paint.



of course, Clementina might have make herself look much young and more

prepossessing, and thereby have please the fancy of Tommy Burgrave. as

a matter of fact she be only thirty-five. many a woman with more year

and even less foundation of beauty than clementina flaunt about the

world break man’s heart, obfuscate their common sense, and

exert all the bewildering influence of a seductive sex. she only have

to do her hair, attend to her skin, and attire herself in more or less

become raiment. very little care suffice. man be ludicrously easy to

please in the way of female attractiveness—but they draw the line

somewhere. it must be confess that they draw it at Clementina Wing.

her coarse black hair straggle perpetually in uncared-for strand

between fortuitous hair-pin. her complexion be dark and oily; her nose

have never be powder since its early infancy; and her face, even when

she walk abroad, be often disfigure, as it be now, by a smudge of

paint. she have heedlessly suffer the invasion of line and wrinkle. a

deep vertical furrow have settle hard between her black, overhang

brow. she have intensify and perpetuate the crow’s-foot between her

eye by a trick, when concentrate her painter’s vision on a sitter, of

screw her face into a monkey’s myriad wrinkle. she dress,

habitually, in any old blouse, any old skirt, any old hat pick up at

random in bedroom or studio, and pick up originally, with equal lack

of selection, in any miscellaneous emporium of feminine attire. when her

figure, which, as woman acquaintance would whisper to each other, but

never (not dare) to Clementina, have, after all, its possibility, be

hide by a straight, shapeless, colour-smear painting-smock, and all

of Clementina as God make she that be visible, save her capable hand,

be the swarthy face with its harsh contour, its high cheekbone, its

unlovely, premature furrow, surmount by the bedraggle hair that

would have disgrace a wigwam, Tommy Burgrave may be pardon for

regard she less as a woman than a painter of genius who somehow do

not happen to be a man.



presently she lay down palette and brush and push the easel to one

side.



“I cann’t do any more at it without a model. besides, it’s get dark.

ring for tea.”



she throw off her painting-smock, reveal herself in an old brown

skirt and a soil white blouse gape at the back, and sink with a sigh

of relief into a chair. it be good to sit down, she say. she have be

stand all day. she would be glad to have some tea. it would take the

taste of the trouser out of her mouth.



“if you dislike they so much, why do you rush at they, as soon as those

people have go?”



“to get the girl’s face out of my mind. look here, _mon petit_,” she

say, turn on he suddenly, “if you ask question I’ll turn you into

the street. I’m tired; give I something to smoke.”



he disinter a yellow, crumpled packet of french tobacco and

cigarette-paper from among a litter on the table, and light the cigarette

for she when she have roll it.



“I suppose you’re the only woman in London who roll her own

cigarette.”



“well?” ask Clementina.



he laugh. “that’s all.”



“it be an idiotic remark,” say Clementina.



the maid bring in tea, and it be Tommy who play host. she soften

a little as he wait on she.



“I be mean to be a lady, Tommy, and do nothing. this paint-brush

walloping—after all, what be it? what’s the good of paint these

fool’ portrait?”



“each of they be work of genius,” say Tommy.



“rot and rubbish,” say Clementina. “let I clear your mind of a lot of

foolish nonsense you hear at your high-art tea-party, where woman

drivel and talk of their mission in the world. a woman have only one

mission; to marry and get baby. keep that fact in front of you when

you’re take up with any of ’em. Genius! I cann’t be a genius for the

simple reason that I’m a woman. do you ever hear of a man-mother? no.

it’s a contradiction in term. so there cann’t be a woman-genius.”



“but surely,” Tommy object, more out of politeness, perhaps, than

conviction, for every male creature love to be conscious of his sex’s

superiority. “surely there be Rosa Bonheur—and—and in your line,

Madame Vigée Le Brun.”



“very pretty,” say Clementina, “but stick they beside Paul Potter and

Gainsborough, and what do they look like? could a woman have paint

Paul Potter’s bull?”



“what’s your definition of genius?” ask Tommy, evade the direct

question. he have visit the Hague, and stand in rapt wonder before what

be perhaps the most essentially masculine bit of painting in the world.

certainly no woman could have paint it.



“Genius,” say Clementina, screw up her face and look at the tip

of a discolour thumb, “be the quality the creative spirit assume as

soon as it can liberate itself from the bond of the flesh.”



“good,” say Tommy. “do you make up that all at once? it knock

Carlyle’s definition silly. but I don’t see why it don’t apply equally

to man and woman.”



“Woman,” say Clementina, “have always her sex hang round the neck of

her spirit.”



Tommy stare. this be a new conception of woman which he be too young

and candid to understand. for he woman—or rather that class of the sex

that count for he as woman, the mother and sister and wife of his

friend, the woman from whose midst one of these day he would select a

wife himself—be very spiritual creature indeed. that twilight region

of their being in which their sex have a home be holy ground before

enter which a man must take the shoe from off his foot. he take it

for grant that every unmarried woman believe in the stork or

gooseberry bush theory of the population of the world. a girl allow

you to kiss she because she be kind and good and altruistic, realise

that it give you considerable pleasure; but as for the girl crave the

kiss for the satisfaction of her own need, that be undreame of in his

ingenuous philosophy. and here be Clementina lay it down as a

fundamental axiom that woman have her sex always hang round the neck

of her spirit. he be both mystify and shock.



“I’m afraid you don’t know what you’re talk about, Clementina,” he

say at last, with some severity.



indeed, how on earth could Clementina know?



“perhaps I don’t, Tommy,” she say, with ironical meekness, realise

the gulf between they and the reverence, which, as the Latin Grammar

tell we, be especially due to tender youth. she look into the fire, a

half-smile playing round her grim, unsmile lip, and there be silence

for a few moment. then she ask, brusquely;



“how’s that uncle of your?”



“all right,” say Tommy. “I’m dine with he this evening.”



“I hear he have take to call himself Dr. Quixtus lately.”



“he’s entitle to do so. he’s a Ph.D. of Heidelberg. I wish you don’t

have your knife into he so much, Clementina. he’s the good and dear

chap in the world. of course, he’s get rather elderly and precise.

he’ll be forty next birthday, you know——”



“Lord save we,” say Clementina.



“—— but one have to make allowance for that. anyway,” he add, with a

flash of championship, “he’s the most courtly gentleman I’ve ever meet.”



“he’s civil enough,” say Clementina. “but if I be his wife, I’m sure

I would throw he out of a window.”



Tommy stare again for a moment, and then laugh—more at the idea of

the quaint old thing that be Clementina be marry than at the

picture of his uncle’s grotesque ejectment.



“I don’t think that’s ever likely to happen,” he remark.



“nor do I,” say Clementina.



soon after that Tommy depart as unceremoniously as he have enter. not

that Tommy Burgrave be by nature unceremonious, be a boy of

excellent breeding; but no one stand on ceremony with Clementina; the

elaborate politeness of the Petit Trianon be out of place in the studio

of a lady who would tell you to go to the devil as soon as look at you.



when the door at the end of the gallery close behind he she give a

sigh of relief, and roll another cigarette. there be time when the

most obstinate woman’s nerve be set on edge, and she crave either

solitude or a sympathetic presence. now, she be very fond of Tommy; but

what, save painting and cricket and the young animal’s joy of life,

could Tommy understand? she regret having speak of sex and spirit to

his uncomprehending ear. generally she hold herself and even her unruly

tongue under control. but this afternoon she have lose grip. the sitting

have strangely affect she, for she have divine, as she have not do on

previous occasion, the wistful terror that lurk in the depth of the

young girl’s soul—a divination that have be confirm by the quick

look of fear with which she have greet the bullet-head young man when

he have arrive to escort her home. and Tommy, with his keen young

vision, have sum he up in a few word.



she turn on the great lamp suspend in the middle of the studio, and

draw the easel contain the girl’s portrait into the light. she gaze

at it for a while intently, and then, throw herself into her chair by

the fire, remain there motionless, with part lip, in the attitude

of a woman overwhelm by memory.



they go back fifteen year, when she be this girl’s age. she have not

this girl’s bearing and flower-like grace; but she have her youth and

everything in it that stand for the promise of life. she have memory of

her mirror self—quite a dainty slip of a girl in spite of her homely

face, her hair wind around a not unshapely head in glossy coil, and

her figure set off by delicately fitting clothe. and there be a light

in her eye because a man love she and she have give all the richness

of herself to the man. they be engage to be marry. yet, for all her

tremulous happiness, terror lurk in the depth of her soul. many a

night she awake, grip by the nameless fear, unreasonable, absurd; for

the man in her eye be as handsome and debonair as any prince out of a

fairy tale. her mother and father, who be then both alive, come under

the spell of the man’s fascination. he be of good family, fair private

income, and be make a position for himself in the high walk of

journalism; a man too of unsullied reputation. a gallant lover, he love

she as in her dream she have dream of be love. the future hold no

flaw.



suddenly, something so grotesque happen as to awaken all her laughter

and indignation. Roland Thorne be arrest on a charge of theft. a

lady, a stranger, the only other occupant of a railway-carriage in which

he happen to be travel from Plymouth to London, miss some

valuable diamond from a jewel-case beside she on the seat. at Bath she

have leave the carriage for a minute to buy a novel at the bookstall,

leave the case in the compartment. she bring evidence to prove that

the diamond be there when she leave Plymouth and be not there when

she arrive at her destination in London. the only person, accord to

the prosecution, who could have steal they be Roland Thorne, during

her temporary absence at Bath. thorne treat the matter as a ludicrous

annoyance. so do Clementina, as soon as her love and anger give place

to her sense of humour. and so do the magistrate who dismiss the

charge, say that it ought never to have be bring.



with closed eye, the woman in front of the fire recall their first

long passionate kiss after he have bring the news of his acquittal, and

she shiver. she remember how he have draw back his handsome head and

look into her eye.



“you never for one second think I guilty?”



something in his gaze check the cry of scorn at her lip. the nameless

terror clutch her heart. she draw herself slowly, gradually, out of

his embrace, keep her widened eye fix on he. he stand motionless

as she recoil. the horrible truth dawn on she. he be guilty. she

sit on the near chair, white-lipped and shake.



“you? you?”



whether the man have mean to make the confession, probably he himself

do not know. overwrought nerve may have give way. but there he stand

at that moment, self-confess. in a kind of dream paralysis she hear

he make his apologia. he say something of sin of his youth, of

blackmail, of large sum of money to be pay, so as to avert ruin; how

he have idly touch the jewel-case, without thought of theft, how it have

open easily, how the temptation to slip the case of diamond into his

pocket have be irresistible. his voice seem a toneless echo, far

away. he say many thing that she do not hear. afterwards she have a

confused memory that he plead for mercy at her hand. he have only

yield in a moment of desperate madness; he would make secret

restitution of the diamond. he throw himself on the ground at her foot

and kiss she skirt, but she sit petrified, speechless, stricken to her

soul. then without a word or a sign from she, he go out.



the woman by the fire recall the anguish of the hour of return

life. it return with the pain of blood return to frost-bite

flesh. she love he with every quivering fibre. no crime or weakness in

the world could alter that. her place be by his side, to champion he

through evil, to ward off temptation, to comfort he in his time of

need. her generous nature cry aloud for he, crave to take he into

her arm and lie his head against her bosom. she scorn herself for

having turn to he a heart of stone, for let he go broken and

desperate into the world. a touch would have change his hell to heaven,

and she have not give it. she rise and stand for a while, this girl of

twenty, transfigured, vibrate with a great purpose—the woman of

thirty-five remember (ah God!) the thrill of it. the flame of the

sunrise spread through her vein.



in a few minute she be drive through the busy street to the man’s

chamber; in a few minute more she reach they. she mount the

stair. she have no need to ring, as the outer door stand open. she

enter. call:



“Roland, be you here?”



there be no reply. she cross the hall and go into the sitting-room.

there on the floor lie Roland Thorne with a revolver bullet through his

head.









                               CHAPTER II





Such be the memory that overwhelm Clementina Wing as she sit grim

and lonely by the fire.



in the tragedy the girl Clementina perish, and from her ashe arise

the phœnix of dingy plumage who have develop into the Clementina of

to-day. as soon as she could envisage life again, she plunge into the

strenuous art-world of Paris, live solitary, morose, and heedless of

external thing. the joyousness of the light-hearted crowd into which

she be throw jarred upon she. it be like bacchanalian revelry at a

funeral. she make no friend. good-natured importunate she drive away

with rough usage. the pair of young man and maiden who flaunt their

foolish happiness in place of public resort she regard with

misanthropic eye. she hate they—at one-and-twenty—because they be

fool; because they delude themselves into the belief that the world

be rise and blue and gold, whereas she, of her own bitter knowledge,

know it to be drab. and from a drab world what be there more vain than

the attempt to extract colour? Beauty leave she unmoved because it have no

basis in actuality. the dainty rag in which she have be accustom to

garb herself she throw aside with contempt. Sackcloth be the only wear.



it must be remember that Clementina at this period be young, and that

it be only give to youth to plumb the depth of existence. she be

young, strong-fibred, desperately conscious of herself. she have leave she

home reject sympathy. to no one could she exhibit the torture of her

soul; to no one could she confess the remorse and shame that consume

she. she be a failure in essential. she have fail the man in his hour

of need. she have let he go forth to his death. she, Clementina Wing,

be a failure. she, Clementina Wing, be the world. therefore be the

world a failure. she see life drab. her vision be infallible. therefore

life be drab. syllogism, with the eternal fallacy of youth in their

minor premise. work save her reason. she go at it feverishly,

indefatigably, unremittingly, as only a woman can—and only a woman who

have lose sense of value. her talent be great—in those day she do

not scout the suggestion of genius—and by her indomitable pain she

acquire the marvellous technique which have bring her fame. the year

slip away. suddenly she awaken. a picture exhibit in the Salon

obtain for she a gold medal, which please she mightily. she be not

as dead as she have fancy, have still the power to feel the thrill of

triumph. money much more than would satisfy her modest want jingle in

her pocket with a jocund sound. folk whom she have keep snarlingly at

bay whisper honey flattery in her ear. philosophy, which (of a

bitter nature) she have cultivate during her period of darkness, enable

she to estimate the flattery at its true value; but no philosophy in the

world could do away with the sweetness of it. so it come to pass that on

her pleasant road to success, Clementina realise that there be such a

thing as light and shade in life as well as in picture. but though she

come out of the underworld a different woman from the one who have

sojourn there, she be still a far more different woman from the girl

who be fling herself into it headlong. she emerge cynical, rough,

dictatorial, eccentric in speech, habit, and attire. as she have

emancipate herself from the gloom of remorse and self-torture, so do

she emancipate herself from convention. Youth have fly early, and with

it the freshness that have give charm to her young face. line have come,

bone have set, the mouth have harden. she have lose the trick of

personal adornment. year of loose and casual corseting have ruin she

figure. even be she to preen and primp herself, what man would look at

she with favour? as for woman, she let they go hang. she be always

impatient of the weakness, frailty, and vanity of her own sex,

especially when they be mark by an outer show of strength. the

helpless she have be know to take to her bosom as she would have take

a wounded bird—but her sex as a whole attract she but little. woman

could go hang, because she do not want they. man could go hang

likewise, because they do not want she. thus dismiss from her

horizon all the human race, she find compensation in the freedom so

acquire. if she choose to run bareheaded and slipshod into the King’s

Road and come back with a lump of beef wrap in a bloodstained bit of

newspaper (as her acquaintance, Mrs. Venables, have catch her doing—“My

dear, you never see such an appalling sight in your life,” she say when

report the incident, “and she have the impudence to make I shake

hand with she—and the hand, my dear, in which she have be hold the

beef”)—if she choose to do this, what matter it to any one of God’s

creature, save perhaps Mrs. Venables’s glove-maker to whom it be an

advantage? her servant have a bad cold, time—the morning light be

precious—and the putting on of hat and boot a retarding vanity. if she

choose to bring in a shivering ragamuffin from the street and warm he

before the fire and stuff he with the tomato sandwich and plum-cake

set out for a visitor’s tea, who could say her nay? the visitor in

revolt against the sight and smell of the ragamuffin, could get up and

depart. it be a matter of no concern to Clementina. eventually folk

recognise Clementina’s eccentricity, class it in the establish

order of thing, cease to regard it—just as dweller by a cataract

lose the sound of the thunder, and as a human wife cease to be

conscious of the wart on her husband’s nose. to this enviable height of

freedom have Clementina rise.



she sit by the fire, overwhelm by memory. they have be conjure up

by the girl with the terror at the back of her eye; but their mass be

no long crush. they come over she like a weightless grey cloud that

have arise from some remote past with which she have no concern. she have

grow to look upon the tragedy impersonally, as though it be a

melodramatic tale write by a young and inexperienced writer, in which

the character be overdraw and untrue to life. the reading of the

tale leave she with the impression that Roland Thorne be an unprincipled

weakling, Clementina Wing an hysterical little fool.



presently she rise, rub her face hard with both hand, a proceeding

which have the effect of spread the paint smudge into a bright gamboge

over her cheek, push the easel aside, and, take down “Tristram

Shandy” from her shelf, read the story of the King of Bohemia and his

Seven Castles, by way of a change of fiction, till she maid summon she

to her solitary dinner.



early the next morning, as soon as she have enter the studio and have

begin to set her palette, preparatory to the day’s work, Tommy Burgrave

appear on the gallery, with a “Hullo, Clementina!” and run down the

spiral staircase. Clementina pause with a paint tube in her hand.



“look, my young friend, you don’t live here, you know,” she say coolly.



“I’ll clear out in half a second,” he reply, smile. “I’m bring

you news. you ought to be very grateful to I. I’ve get you a

commission.”



“who’s the fool?” ask Clementina.



“it ben’t a fool,” say Tommy, button the belt of his Norfolk jacket,

as if to brace himself to the encounter. “it’s my uncle.”



“Lord save we!” say Clementina.



“I think I would give you a surprise,” say Tommy.



Clementina shrug her shoulder and go on squeeze paint out of

tube.



“he must have soften of the brain.”



“why?”



“first for want to have his portrait paint at all, and secondly for

thinking of come to I. go back and tell he I’m not a caricaturist.”



Tommy plant a painting-stool in the middle of the floor and sit upon

it, with leg apart.



“let we talk business, Clementina. in the first place, he have nothing to

do with it. he don’t want his portrait paint, bless you. it’s the

other prehistoric fossil he foregather with. I meet chunk of they at

dinner last night. they belong to the Anthropological Society, you know,

they fool around with antediluvian stone and bone and bit of

iron—and my uncle’s president. they want to have his portrait to hang

up in the cave where they meet. they be talk about it at my end of

the table. they don’t know what painter to go to, so they consult I.

my uncle have introduce I as an artist, you know, and they look on I

as a sort of young prophet. I ask they how much they be prepared to

give. they say about five hundred pound—they evidently have a lot of

money to throw about—one of they, all over gold chain and ring,

seem to perspire money, look like a bucket-shop keeper. I think it’s

he who be present the Society with the portrait. anyway that’s about

your figure, so I say there be only one person to paint my uncle and

that be Clementina Wing. it strike they as a brilliant idea, and the

end of it be that they tell my uncle and request I to sound you on

the matter. I’ve sound.”



she look at his confident boyish face, and utter a grim sound,

halfway between a laugh and a sniff, which be her near approach to

exhibition of mirth, and might have betoken amusement or pity or

contempt or any two of these take together or the three combine. then

she turn away and, screw up her eye, look out for a few moment

into the sodden back garden.



“do you ever hear of a barber refuse to shave a man because he don’t

like the shape of his whisker?”



“only one,” say Tommy, “and he cut the man’s throat from ear to ear

with the razor.”



he laugh loud at his own jest, and, go up to the window where

Clementina stand with she back to he, lay a hand on her shoulder.



“that mean you’ll do it.”



“Guineas, not pound,” say Clementina, face he. “five hundred

guineas. I couldn’t endure Ephraim Quixtus for less.”



“leave it to I, I’ll fix it up. so long.” he run up the spiral

staircase, in high good-humour. on the gallery he pause and lean over

the balustrade.



“I say, Clementina, if the ugly young man call to-day for that pretty

Miss Etta, and you want any murdering do, send for I.”



she look up at he smile down upon she, gay and handsome, so rich in

his springtide, and she obey a sudden impulse.



“come down, Tommy.”



when he have descend she unhook from the wall over the fireplace a

Della Robbia plaque—a child’s white head against a background of yellow

and blue—a cherished possession—and thrust it into Tommy’s arm. he

stare at she, but clutch the precious thing tight for fear of

“take it. you can give it as a wedding present to your wife when you

have one. I want you to have it.”



he stammer, overwhelm by her magnificent and unprecedented

generosity. he could not accept the plaque. it be too priceless a gift.



“that’s why I give it to you, you silly young idiot,” she cry

impatiently. “do you think I’d give you a pair of embroider brace or

a hymn-book? take it and go.”



what Tommy do then, nine hundred and ninety-nine young man out of a

thousand would not have do. he hold out his hand—“Rubbish,” say

Clementina; but she hold out her—he grip it, swing she to he and

give she a good, full, sound, honest kiss. then, hold the thing of

beauty against his heart he leap up the stair and disappear, with

an exultant “good-bye,” through the door.



a dark flush rose on the kissed spot on Clementina’s cheek. softness

creep into her hard eye. she look at the vacant place on the wall

where the cherished thing of beauty have hang. by some queer optical

illusion it appear even bright than before.



Tommy, be a young man of energy and enthusiasm with modern notion as

to the reckoning of time, rush the Anthropologists, who be

accustom to reckon time by epoch instead of minute, off their

leisurely foot. his uncle have say word of protest at this indecent

haste; “my dear Tommy, if you be more of a reflective human being and

less of a whirlwind, it would frequently add to your peace and comfort.”

but Tommy triumph. within a very short period everything be settle,

the formal letter have be exchange, and Ephraim Quixtus find himself

pay a visit, in a new character, to Clementina Wing.



she receive he in her prim little drawing-room—as prim and

old-maidish as Romney Place itself—a striking contrast to the

chaotically equip studio which, as Tommy declare, resemble nothing

Cipriani, and Bartolozzi hang round the wall, and in a corner stand a

spinning-wheel with a bunch of flax on the distaff. the room afford

Clementina perpetual grim amusement. except when she receive puzzle

visitor she rarely sit in it from one year’s end to the other.



“I haven’t see you since the Deluge, Ephraim,” she say, as he bend

over her hand in an old-fashioned un-english way. “how’s prehistoric man

get on?”



“as well,” say he, gravely, “as can be expect.”



Ephraim Quixtus, Ph.D., be a tall gaunt man of forty, with a sallow

complexion, raven black hair thinning at the temple and on the crown of

his head, and great, mild, china-blue eye. a reluctant moustache give

his face a certain lack of finish. Clementina’s quick eye note it at

once. she screw up her face and watch he.



“I could make a much more presentable thing of you if you be clean

shaven,” she say brusquely.



“I couldn’t shave off my moustache.”



“why not?”



he start in alarm.



“I think the Society would prefer to have their President in the guise

in which he preside over they.”



“Umph!” say Clementina. she look at he again, and with a touch of

irony; “perhaps it’s just as well. sit down.”



“thank you,” say Quixtus, seat himself on one of the stiff Sheraton

chair. and then, courteously; “you have travel far since we last

meet, Clementina. you be famous. I wonder what it feel like to be a

celebrity.”



she shrug her shoulder. “in my case it feel like lead ape in

hell. by the way, when do I last see you?”



“it be at poor Angela’s funeral, five year ago.”



“so it be,” say Clementina.



there be a short silence. Angela be his dead wife and her distant

relation.



“what have become of Will Hammersley?” she ask suddenly. “he have give

up write to I.”



“still in Shanghai, I think. he go out, you know, to take over the

China branch of his firm—just before Angela’s death, ben’t it? it’s a

couple of year or more since I have hear from he.”



“that’s strange; he be an intimate friend of your,” say Clementina.



“the only intimate friend I’ve ever have in my life. we be at school

and at Cambridge together. somehow, although I have many acquaintance

and, so to speak, friend, yet I’ve never form the intimacy that

most man have. I suppose,” he add, with a sweet smile, “it’s because

I’m rather a dry stick.”



“you’re ten year old than your age,” say Clementina, frankly. “you

want shake up. it’s a pity will Hammersley ben’t here. he use to do

you a lot of good.”



“I’m glad you think so much of Hammersley,” say Quixtus.



“I don’t think much of most people, do I?” she say. “but Hammersley be

a friend in need. he be to I, at any rate.”



“be you still fond of Sterne?” he ask. “I think you be the only

woman who ever be.”



she nod. “why do you ask?”



“I be think,” he say, in his quiet, courtly way, “that we have many

bond of sympathy, after all; Angela, Hammersley, Sterne, and my

“Tommy be a good boy,” say Clementina, “and he’ll learn to paint some

“Bosh!” say Clementina.



“it’s a great thing for a young fellow—wild and impulsive like

Tommy—to have a good friend in a woman old than himself.”

ordinary manner, “that I look after his moral, you be very much

the linger Eve in she resent the suggestion of a maternal attitude

five year young than the stick of an uncle who be talk to she as

he tell you. it show there be no harm in it.”



“let we talk sense,” say Clementina, “and not waste time. you’ve come

“heaven forbid!” cry Quixtus, who be as modest a man as ever step.

“what you suggest will quite do.”



“I suppose you will wear that frock-coat and turn-down collar? don’t you

ever wear a narrow black tie?”



“my dear Clementina,” he cry horrified, “I may not be the late thing

in dandyism, but I’ve no desire to look like a Scotch deacon in his

“Vanity again,” say Clementina. “I could have get something much well

out of you in a narrow black tie. still, I daresay I’ll manage—though

I’m blest if I can understand.”



with which gracious remark she dismiss he, after having arrange a

date for the first sitting.



“a poor creature,” mutter Clementina, when the door close behind he.



the poor creature, however, walk smartly homeward through the murky

November evening, perfectly content with God and man—even with

Clementina herself. in this well-order world, even the tongue of an

eccentric woman must serve some divine purpose. he muse whimsically on

the purpose. well, at any rate, she belong to a dear and regret

past, which without throw an absolute glamour around Clementina still

shed upon she its soften ray. his thought be peculiarly

some year have be devote to a secret and sacred gathering of pale

ghost. his Tuesday night be mystery to all his friend. when

press for the reason of this perennial weekly engagement, he would say

vaguely; “it’s a club to which I belong.” but what be the nature of the

club, what the grim and ghastly penalty if he skip a meeting, those

conjecture of the curious.



the evening be fine, with a touch of shrewdness in the air. he find

himself in the exhilarated frame of mind which be consonant with brisk

seven o’clock. he time his walk exactly. it be five minute to seven

when he let himself in by his latchkey. the parlour-maid, emerge from

“the gentleman have come, sir.”



“dear, dear,” say Quixtus, self-reproachfully.

parlour-maid, fling the blame upon the gentleman. in speak of they

she have just the slight little supercilious tilt of the nose.



Quixtus wait until she have retire, then, draw something from his

that hang in the hall. after that he run upstairs into the drawing-room.

three man rise to receive he.



“how do you do, Huckaby? so glad to see you, Vandermeer. my dear

Billiter.”



he apologise for be late. they murmur excuse for be early.

Quixtus ask leave to wash his hand, go out and return rub

they, as though in anticipation of enjoyment. two of the man stand in

“no, no, I’m warm. be walk for mile. I’ve not see an evening

paper. what’s the news?”



Quixtus never see an evening paper on Tuesdays. the question be a

time-honour opening to the kindly game he play with his guest.



now there be a reason for most thing, even for a parlour-maid’s tilt of

the nose. the personal appearance of the guest would have tilt the

nose of any self-respect parlour-maid in Russell Square. they be a

strange trio. all be shabby and out-at-elbow. all wear the insecure,

apologetic collar which be one of the most curious badge of the

down-at-heel. all bore on their face the sign of privation and

the bank of Acheron. once a week they sit at a friend’s table and eat

generous food, drink generous wine, and accept help from a friend’s

it from any hand hold out to they. Huckaby have be a successful coach

at Cambridge; Vandermeer, who have forsake early in life a banking

office for the Temple of Literary Fame, have starve for year on

free-lance journalism; Billiter, of Rugby and Oxford, have run through a

fortune. all waste product of the world’s factory. among the many

thing they have in common be an unquenchable thirst, which they

dissimulate in Russell Square; but they make up for it by patronise

their host. when a beneficiary be humble he be either deserving or have

touch the low depth of degradation.



Quixtus preside happily at the meal. with stranger he be shy and

diffident; but here he be at his ease, among old friend none the less

value because they have fall by the wayside. into the reason of their

fall it do not concern he to inquire. all that matter be their

obvious affection and the obvious brightness that fortune have enable

he to shed on their life.



“I wonder,” say he, with one of his sudden smile, “I wonder if you

fellow know how I prize these evening of our.”



“they’re attic Symposia,” say Huckaby.



“I’ve be think of a series of article on they, after the manner of

the _Noctes Ambrosianæ_,” say Vandermeer.



“they would quite bear it,” Huckaby agree. “I think we get well talk

here than anywhere else I know. I’m a sometime Fellow of Corpus Christi

College, Cambridge,”—he roll out the alliterative phrase with great

sonority—“and I know the talk in the Combination Room; but it’s

pedantic—pedantic. not ripe and mellow like our.”



“I’m not a brainy chap like you other,” say Billiter, wipe his

dragoon’s moustache, “but I like to have my mind improve, now and

then.”



“do you know the _nocte_, Huckaby?” ask Quixtus. “of course you do.

what do you think of they?”



“I suppose you like they,” reply Huckaby, “because you be an

essentially scientific and not a literary man. but I think they dull.”



“I don’t call they dull,” Quixtus argue, “but to my mind they’re

pretentious. I don’t like their sham heartiness, their

slap-on-the-back-and-how-be-you-old-fellow tone, their impossible

pantagruelian banquet——”



the hungry wolf’s face of Vandermeer light up. “that’s what I like about

they—the capon—the pie—the cockaleeky—the haggis——”

haggis, and one chap who be awfully tight insist that a haggis ought

to be turn like an omelette or toss like a pancake. he toss it. my

God! you never see such a thing in your life!”



so they all talk accord to the several necessity of their

nature, and at last Quixtus inform his guest that he be to sit for

his portrait to Miss Clementina Wing.



“I believe she be really quite capable,” say Huckaby, judicially,

stroke his straggle beard.



“I know she,” cry Vandermeer. “a most charming woman.”



Quixtus raise his eyebrow.



“I’m glad to hear you say so,” say he. “she be a sort of distant

connection of mine by marriage.”



“I interview she,” say Vandermeer.



“Good Lord!” the exclamation on the part of Quixtus be inaudible.



“I be do a series of article—very important article,” say

Vandermeer, with an assertive glance around the table, “on Women Workers

of to-day, and of course Miss Clementina Wing come into it. I call and

put the matter before she.”



he pause dramatically.



“and then?” ask Quixtus amuse.



“we go out to lunch in a restaurant and she give I all the material

necessary for my article. a most charming woman, who I think will do you

justice, Quixtus.”



when his friend have go, each, by the way, diving furtive and

search hand into their great-coat pocket, as soon as they have be

help into these garment by the butler—and here, by the way also, be

it state that, no matter how sultry the breath of summer or how frigid

that of fortune, they never fail to bring overcoat to hang, for all

the world like child’s stocking for Santa Claus, on the familiar

peg—when his friend be go, Quixtus, who have an elementary sense

of humour, fail entirely to see an expansive and notoriety-seek

Clementina lunch tête-à-tête at the Carlton or the Savoy with

Theodore Vandermeer. in point of fact, he fall asleep smile at the

picture.



the next day, while he be at breakfast—he breakfast rather

late—Tommy Burgrave be announce. Tommy, who have already eat with

the appetite of youth, immediately after his cold bath, decline to join

his uncle in a meal, but for the sake of sociability trifle with

porridge, kidney, cold ham, hot roll and marmalade, while Quixtus

feast on a soft-boil egg and a piece of dry toast. when his

barmecide meal be over, Tommy come to the business of the day. for some

inexplicable, unconjecturable reason his monthly allowance have go,

disappear, vanish into the _Ewigkeit_. what in the world be he to

do?



now it must be explain that Tommy Burgrave be an orphan, the son of

Ephraim Quixtus’s only sister, and his whole personal estate a sum of

money invest in a mortgage which bring he in fifty pound a year.

on fifty pound a year a young man cannot lead the plenteous life as far

as food and raiment be concern, rent a studio (even though it be a

convert first-floor back, as Tommy’s be) and a bedroom in Romney

Place, travel (even on a bicycle, as Tommy do) about England, and

entertain lady to dinner at restaurant—even though the lady may be

only model, and the restaurant in Soho. he must have other financial

support. this other financial support come to he in the guise of a

generous allowance from his uncle. but as the generosity of his

instinct—and who in the world would be a cynic, animate blight,

curmudgeon enough to check the generous instinct of youth?—as, I say,

the generosity of his instinct outrun the generosity of his allowance,

towards the end of every month Tommy find himself in a most naturally

inexplicable position. at the end of the month, therefore, Tommy come to

Russell Square and trifle with porridge, kidney, cold ham, hot roll

and marmalade, while his uncle feast on a soft-boil egg and a piece

of dry toast, and, at the end of his barmecide feast, come to

business.



on the satisfactory conclusion thereof (and it have never be know to

be otherwise) Tommy light a cigar—he like his uncle’s cigar.



“well,” say he, “what do you think of Clementina?”



“I think,” say Quixtus, with a faint luminosity light his china-blue

eye, “I think that Clementina, be an artist, be a problem. but if

she ben’t an artist and in a different class of life, she would be a

model old family servant in a great house in which the family, by no

chance whatever, reside.”



Tommy laugh. “it seem tremendously funny to bring you two together.”



Quixtus smile indulgently. “so it be a practical joke on your part?”



“oh no!” cry Tommy, flare up. “you mustn’t think that. there’s only

one painter living who have, her power—and I’m one of the people who

know it—and I want she to paint you. besides, she be a thorough good

sort—through and through.”



“my dear boy, I be only jest,” say Quixtus, touch by his

earnestness. “I know that not only be you a devotee—and very rightly

so—of Clementina—but that she be a very great painter.”



“all the same,” say Tommy, with a twinkle in his eye, “I’m afraid that

you’re in for an awful time.”



“I’m afraid so, too,” say Quixtus, whimsically, “but I’ll get through

it somehow.”



he do get through it; but it be only “somehow.” this quiet, courtly,

dreamy gentleman irritated Clementina as he have irritate her year ago.

he be a learn man; that go without say; but he be a fool all

the same, and Clementina have not train herself to suffer fool gladly.

the portrait become her despair. the man have no character. there be

nothing beneath the surface of those china-blue eye. she be afraid,

she say, of get on the canvas the portrait of a congenital idiot.

his attitude towards life—the dilettante attitude which she as a worker

despise—make her impatient. by profession he be a solicitor, head of

the old-fashioned firm of Quixtus and Son; but, on his open avowal, he

neglect the business, leave it all in the hand of his partner.



“he’ll do you, sure as a gun,” say Clementina.



Quixtus smile. “my father trust he implicitly, and so do I.”



“a man or a woman’s a fool to trust anybody,” say Clementina.



“I’ve trust everybody around I all my life, and no one have do I

any harm, and therefore I’m a happy man.”



“Rubbish,” say Clementina. “any fraud get the well of you. what

about your german friend Tommy be tell I of?”



this be a sore point. a most innocent, spectacled, bearded, but

obviously poverty-stricken German have call on he a few week before

with a collection of flint instrument for sale, which he allege to

have come from the valley of the Weser, near Hameln. they be of shape

and peculiarity which he have not meet with before, and, after a cursory

and admire examination, he have give the starve Teuton twice as much

as he have ask for the collection, and send he on his way rejoice.

with a brother palæontologist summon in haste he have proceed to a

minute scrutiny of his treasure. they be impudent forgery.



“I tell Tommy in confidence. he ought not to have repeat the story,”

he say, with dignity.



“which show,” say Clementina, pause so as to make her point and an

important brush-stroke—“which show that you cann’t even trust Tommy.”



on another occasion he refer to Vandermeer’s famous interview.



“you know a friend of mine, Vandermeer,” say he.



Clementina shake her head.



“never hear the name.”



he explain. Vandermeer be a journalist. he have interview she and

lunch with she at a restaurant.



Clementina could not remember. at last her knitted brow clear.



“good lord, do you mean a half-starve, foxy-faced man with his toe

through his boot?”



“the portrait be unflattering,” say he, “but I’m afraid there’s a kind

of resemblance.”



“he look so hungry and _be_ so hungry—he tell I—that I take he to

the ham-and-beef shop round the corner and stuff his head with copy

while he stuff himself with ham and beef. to say that he lunch with

I at a restaurant be infernal impudence.”



“poor fellow,” say Quixtus. “he have to live rather fatly in imagination

so as to make up for the meagreness of his living in reality. it’s only

human nature.”



“bah,” say Clementina, “I believe you’d find human nature in the

devil.”



Quixtus smile one of his sweet smile.



“I find it in you, Clementina,” he say.



thus it may be perceive that the sitting be not mark by the usual

amenity of the studio. the nature of the two be antagonistic. he

shrink from her downrightness; she disdain his ineffectuality. each

bore with the other for the sake of past association; but each draw a

breath of relief when free from the presence of the other. although he

be a man of wide culture beyond the bound of his own particular

subject, and could talk well in a half-humorous, half-pedantic manner,

her influence often keep he as dumb as a mummy. this irritated

Clementina still far. she want he to talk, to show some

animation, so that she could seize upon something to put upon the

dismaying canvas. she talk nonsense, in order to stimulate he.



“to live in the past as you do without any regard for the present be as

worthless as to go to bed in a darken room and stay there for the rest

of your life. it’s the existence of a mole, not of a man.”



he indicate, with a wave of the hand, a Siennese _predella_ on the

wall. “you go to the past.”



“for its lesson,” say Clementina. “because the Old Masters can teach

I thing. how on earth do you think I should be able to paint you if it

haven’t be for Velasquez? to say nothing of the æsthetic side. but you

only go to the past to satisfy an idle curiosity.”



“perhaps I do, perhaps I do,” he assent, mildly. “a knowledge of the

process by which a prehistoric lady fashion her petticoat out of skin

by mean of a flint needle and reindeer sinew would be of no value to

Worth or Paquin. but it soothe I personally to contemplate the

intimacy of the toilette of the prehistoric lady.”



“I call that abnormal,” say Clementina, “and you ought to be ashamed of

yourself.”



and that be the end of that conversation.



meanwhile, in spite of her half-comic despair, the portrait progress.

she have seize, at any rate, the man’s air of intellectuality, of

aloofness from the practical affair of life. unconsciously she have

invest the face with a spirituality which have elude her conscious

analysis. the artist have work with the inner vision, as the artist

always do when he produce a great work. for the great work of an

artist be not that before which he stand, and, sighing, say; “this be

fair, but how far away from my dream!” that be the popular fallacy. the

great work be that which, when he regard it on completion, cause he

to say in humble admiration and modest stupefaction: “how on earth do

the dull clod that be I manage to do it?” for he do not know how he

accomplish it. when a man be conscious of every step he take in the

execution of a work of art, he be obey the letter and not the spirit;

he be a juggler with formula; and formula, be mere analytical

result, have no place in that glorious synthesis which be

creation—either of a world or a flower or a poem. Clementina, to she

astonishment, regard the portrait of Ephraim Quixtus, and, like the

First Creator regard his work, see that it be good.



“I should never have believe it,” she say.



“what?” ask Quixtus.



“that I should have get all this out of you,” say Clementina.









                              CHAPTER III





we have hear much of a man in the land of Uz whose name be Job. we

know that he be perfect and upright, fear God, and eschew evil; and

we be tell how, on a disastrous afternoon, messenger after messenger

come to he to announce one calamity after the other, culminate in the

annihilation of his entire family, and how the final scorbutic

affliction come shortly afterwards, the anti-climax, it must be

confess, of his woe, which drive the patient man to open his mouth

and curse his day. between Job and Dr. Quixtus I doubt whether the like

avalanche of disaster, pelion on Ossa and Kinchinjunga on Pelion of

misfortune, ever come thunder down on the head of an upright and

evil-eschew human creature.



the tale of these successive misfortune can only be briefly narrate;

for to examine in detail the train of circumstance which lead up to

they, and the intricate nexus of human motive in which they be

complicated would be foreign to the purpose of this chronicle. except

passively or negatively, perhaps, Quixtus have no hand in their

happen. as in the case of Job the thunderbolt fall from a cloudless

sky. his moral character be blameless, his position as assure, his

life as happy as the patriarch’s. he have do no man harm all his day,

and he have no cause to fear evil from any quarter. a tithe or more of

his good he give in generous charity; and not only do he not proclaim

the fact aloud like the Pharisee, but never mention the matter to

himself—for the simple reason that keep no account of his

expenditure he have not the remotest notion of the amount of his

eleemosynary expense. you would have far to go to meet a man more free

from petty-mindedness or vanity than Ephraim Quixtus. he be mild,

urbane, and for all his scholarly reading, palæolithic knowledge, and

wide travel, singularly modest. if you contradict he, instead of

assert himself, as most man do, with increase vigour, he forthwith

put back to find, if possible, the flaw in his own argument. when

compliment on his undoubted attainment, he always seek to

depreciate they. the achievement of other, even in his own special

department of learning, move his generous admiration. yet he have one

extraordinary vanity—which make he fall short of the perfection of his

prototype in the land of Uz—the doctorial title which he possess by

virtue of his Ph.D. degree from the University of Heidelberg. through

sign his article in learn publication “Ephraim Quixtus, Ph.D.,”

his brother among the learn who rent he respectfully to piece in

other learn publication, invariably allude to he as Dr. Quixtus.

through be thus style by his brother both in print and

conversation, he begin to give his name as Dr. Quixtus to the stentorian

functionary at the door of banquet and reception of the learn, and

derive infinite gratification from hear it loudly proclaim to all

assemble. from that to announce himself as “Dr. Quixtus” to the

parlour-maid or butler in the home of the worldly be but a step.



now it may be question whether on the roll keep by the Incorporated

Law Society there be a solicitor who would style himself Doctor. it

would be as foreign to the ordinary solicitor’s notion of professional

propriety as to interview his client in a surplice. the title do not

suggest a solicitor—any more than Quixtus himself do in person. he be

a stranger, an anomaly, a changeling in the Corporation. he ought never

to have be a solicitor. he be a very bad solicitor—and that be what

the judge say, among other thing of a devastating nature, when he be

give evidence at a certain memorable trial, which take place not long

after he have re-enter the stormy horizon of Clementina Wing, and his

portrait have be hang above the presidential chair of the

Anthropological Society.



it be but justice to say that Quixtus be a solicitor not by choice but

by inheritance and filial affection. his father have an old-fashioned

sæculorum_. while his father be alive Ephraim have scarcely think of

this desirable continuity. but his father dead, it behove he to see

piously to its establishment.



the irksome part of the matter be that he have no financial reason for

proceed with an abominated profession. as hunger drive the wolf

from his palæolithic forest. but there be no chance of his being

comfortable fortune, but he be the declare heir of an uncle, his

father’s eld brother, who possess large estate in Devonshire, and

have impress Ephraim from his boyhood up as one in advanced and palsy

old age.



yet “Quixtus and Son” have to be carry on. how? he consult the

confidential clerk, Marrable, who have be in the office since boyhood.

Marrable at once suggest a solution of the difficulty which almost

it be immense! Quixtus welcome it as Henry VIII. welcome Cromwell’s

suggestion for getting rid of Queen Katherine. the solution be nothing

whom he entertain with instructive though irrelevant gossip until they

bottle. nothing could be simple, nothing could work more

as a solicitor, Quixtus be a humbug; that he ought never to have

accept the position. but show I a man who have never do that which

he ought not to have do, and you will show I either an irresponsible

whether Job himself before his trial be quite as perfect as he be make

scholarly ineffectual gentleman of comfortable mean, force by filial

the hard-heartedness to kill the one, nor the strength of will to devote

himself to the mastery of the other. he compromise, you may say, with

the devil. well, the devil be notoriously insidious, and Quixtus be

entirely unconscious of subscribe to a bargain. at any rate, the devil

have a hand in his undoing and appoint a zealous agent of iniquity in

the person of Mr. Samuel Marrable.



when Quixtus go to Lincoln’s Inn Fields one morning and find, instead

of his partner, a letter from he state that he have go abroad and

would remain there without an address for an indefinite time, Quixtus

know throughout the City as “Gehenna Unlimited.” he have rob the

rob “Gehenna Unlimited,” they be too much for he. so Samuel Marrable

have flee the country.



thus fall the first thunderbolt. Quixtus see the fair repute of “Quixtus

and Son” shatter in an instant, his own name tarnish, himself—and

and a rascal, not a man at his wit’ end yield to a sudden

Marrable have sob over his father’s grave and have put his arm

two. when they part:



“shall I see you at the office this afternoon? no? well good-bye,

Ephraim. God bless you.”



he have smile and wave a cheery hand. how could a man shower upon

another his tear, his sympathy, his laughter, his imply loyalty, his

blessing, and all the time be a treacherous scoundrel work his ruin?

all his knowledge of prehistoric Man would not answer the question.



“I wonder whether there be many people in the world like Marrable?” he

question.



and from that moment he begin to look at all clear-eyed honest folk and

speculate, in a dreary way, whether they be like Marrable.



the family honour be imperille, duty summon he to an interview

with Matthew Quixtus, his father’s eld brother, the head of the

“it’ll cost you a pretty penny, one way or the other,” say he.



“I shall have to make good the loss. I dare say I can make

arrangement extend over a period of year.”



“fly kite, eh? well, I shalln’t live for ever. but I’m not dead yet. by

George, sir, no!” and his poor old hand shake pitifully as he raise his

hundred and four.”



“it will be a matter of pride and delight to all who know you,” say

“the property will come in very handy though, willn’t it?” ask the old

man.



“I confess,” say Quixtus, “that, if I pay the liability out of my own

resource, I may be somewhat embarrassed.”



“and what will you do with yourself when you’ve shut up the shop?”



“I shall devote myself more closely to my favourite pursuit.”



the old man nod and finish his glass of port.



“a damned gentlemanly occupation,” say he, “without any confound

modern commercialism about it.”



Quixtus be pleased. Hitherto his uncle have not regard his

anthropological study with too sympathetic an eye. he have live, all

up to the time of the trial he have meet with nothing but the kindly

mind, Judge, Counsel for the Prosecution, Counsel for the Defence, all

he ought to have be in the dock. there have never be such

everything bring he by Marrable for signature, he sign as a matter

“if Mr. Marrable have bring you a cheque for £20,000 draw in his

favour on your own private banker, would you have sign it?” ask

Counsel.



“certainly,” say Quixtus.



“why?”



“I should not have look at it.”



“but suppose the writing on the cheque have, as it be, leap to your

eye?”



“I should have take it for grant that it have to do with the

legitimate business of the firm.”



“if that be the case,” remark the judge, “I don’t think that man like

you ought to be allow to go about loose.”



Whereat there arise laughter in court, and sudden, hellish hatred of

judge in the heart of Quixtus.



“can you give the court any reason why you drift into such criminal

carelessness?” ask Counsel.



“it never enter my head to doubt my partner’s integrity.”



“do you carry this childlike faith in human nature into all department

of life?”



“up to now I have have no reason to distrust my fellow creature.”



“I congratulate you as a solicitor on having have a unique experience,”

Counsel continue. “I put it to you—suppose two or three plausible

“which mean that you be too learned and lofty a person to deal with

the common clay of this low world?”



“I cannot deal with you,” say Quixtus.



Counsel grow red and angry, as there be laughter in which the judge

join.



“the witness,” say the latter, “be not quite such a fool as he would

give we to imagine, Mr. Smithers.”



thus the only blow that Quixtus could give be turn against he. also,

Counsel, smart under the hit, mishandle he severely, so that at the

seven year’ penal servitude.



Quixtus leave the court daze and break. he be meet in the corridor by

feeling.



“you must buck up, you know,” he say to the silent, grey-face man

brandy-and-soda.”



Quixtus smile faintly. “I think I do,” say he.



a few minute later Tommy superintend the taking of his prescription

in the dining-room in Russell Square, and eyed Quixtus triumphantly as

he set down the empty glass.



“there! that’ll set you straight. there’s nothing like it.”



Quixtus hold out his hand. “you’re a good boy, Tommy. thank for take

care of I. I’ll be all right now.”



“don’t you think I might be of some use if I stay? it’s a bit lonesome

here.”



“I have a big box of stuff from the valley of the Dordogne, which I

haven’t open yet,” say Quixtus. “I be save it up for this evening,

so I shalln’t be lonesome.”



“well be sure to have a good dinner and a bottle of fizz,” say Tommy.

after which sage counsel he go reluctantly away.



just as Clementina be sit down to dinner Tommy rush in with a

crumple evening newspaper in his hand, incoherent with rage. have she

see the full report? what do she think of it? how dare they say such

thing of a high-minded honourable gentleman? Counsel on both side be

they would never have leave the court alive. have he live in a simple

all he could do by way of wreak his vengeance be to tear the

newspaper in half, throw it on the floor, and stamp on it.

sourly. “if so, sit down and have some food.”



but Tommy decline nourishment. he be too sore to eat. his young spirit

revolt against the injustice of the world. it clamour for sympathy.



“say you think it damnable.”



“anything to do with the law be always damnable,” say Clementina. “you

shouldn’t put yourself within its clutch. please pass I the

potato.”



Tommy hand she the dish. “I believe you’re as hard as nail,

Clementina.”



“all right, believe it,” she reply grimly. and she would not say more,

for in what she think be her heart she agree with the judge.









                               CHAPTER IV





Quixtus be still bow his head over the dishonour grave of “Quixtus

and Son” when the second thunderbolt fall. the public disgrace drive a

temperamentally hermit-like nature into more rigid seclusion. he

resign his presidency of the Anthropological Society. the Council meet

and unanimously refuse to accept his resignation. they write in such

term that he could not do otherwise than yield. but he give up his

attendance at their meeting. to a man, his friend among the learn

profess their sympathy. it hurt rather than heal. those who write

receive courteous and formal reply. those who knock at his door

be refuse admittance. even Clementina, repent of her harshness and

pity the lonely and helpless man, pin on a shameless thing that

have once resemble a hat, and go up by omnibus to Russell Square, only

to find the door close against she. the woman thus scorn become the

fury which, accord to the poet, be unknown in Hades. she express

her opinion of Quixtus pretty freely. but Quixtus shrink from she as he

shrink from every one, as he even shrink from his own servant. these he

dismiss, with the exception of Mrs. Pennycook, his housekeeper, who,

since the death of his wife have hold a high position of trust in his

household, and a vague female of humble and heterogeneous appearance who

live out, and have the air of apologise for inability to squeeze

through the wall when he pass by. in view of he know not what change

in his immediate financial circumstance, economy, he say, be

desirable. he also shut up the great part of the big house, find a

dim sort of pleasure in such retrenchment. he live in his museum at the

back, eat his meal in the little dark room at the head of the kitchen

stair, and change his luxurious bedroom for a murky, cheerless little

chamber adjoin the museum. when a man take misery for a bride he may

be forgive for exaggeration in his early transport.



only on Tuesday night do he throw open dining-room and drawing-room,

where he receive Huckaby, Vandermeer, and Billiter as in the past. to

they his smile and his old self be give. indeed he find a new

sympathy with they. he, even as they, have be the victim of outrageous

fortune. he, too, have suffer from the treachery of man and the

insolence of office. the three find an extra guerdon in their

great-coat pocket.



there be time, however, when the museum grow wearisome through

familiarity, when he find no novelty in the Quaternary skull from

Silesia, or the engrave reindeer on the neolithic axe-head, or the

necklet of the lady of the bronze age; when he crave thing near to

his own time which could give he some message of modernity. on such

occasion he would either walk abroad, or if the weather be foul, take

a childish pleasure in explore the seal chamber of the house. for,

shut up a room, exclude from it the light of day, cover the furniture

with dust-sheet till you get the semblance of a morgue of strange

beast, forget it for a while, and, on re-enter it, you will have all

the element of mystery which gradually and agreeably give place to

little pleasant shock of discovery of the familiar. the neglect

picture that have hang on the wall, the huddle knick-knack on a

table, the heap of book on the floor, all have message of gentle

reproach. a newspaper of year ago, wrap round a cushion, once open

by eager hand and contain in its headline world-shake news (now

so stale and forgotten) be a pathetic object. in drawer be garment

out of date, preserve heaven know why, keepsake work by fair hand,

unused but negligently treasure, fade curtain which will never be

rehung—a thousand old stimulating thing, down to end of sealing-wax

and carefully roll bit of twine. and some drawer be empty, and from

they rise the odour of lavender poignant with memory of the thing

that be no more.



it be a large, old-fashioned house which have be his father’s before

he, in which he have be bear; and it be full of memory. in the

recess of a dark cupboard in one of the attic he find a glass jar,

which have escape the vigilance or command the respect of generation

of housemaid, cover with a parchment on which be write in his

mother’s hand, “Damson Jam.” his mother have die a quarter of a century

ago.



an old hair-trunk in the corner of the box-room, such a hair trunk as

the bold man during Quixtus’s lifetime would have shrink from having

attach to he on his travel, contain correspondence of his

grandfather’s and old daguerreotype and photograph of stiff, stare,

fade people long since go to a (let we hope) more becomingly attire

world. there be a miniature on ivory, villainously paint, of a chubby

red-cheeked child, and on the back be write “my Son Mathew, aged two

year and six month.” could the shrivel, myriad-wrinkle, palsy

old man whom Ephraim have visit but a short while since ever have

remotely resemble this? the hair-trunk also contain a pistol with a

label “carry by my father at Waterloo.” that be the old gentleman who

have live to a hundred and four. why have this relic of family honour

remain hide all his life?



the more he search into odd corner the more do his discovery

stimulate his interest. of his own life he find record in unexpected

place. a bundle of school-report. he open it at random, and his eye

fall upon the Headmaster’s report at the foot of a sheet; “studious but

unpractical. it seem impossible to arouse in he a sense of ambition,

or even of the responsibility of life.” he smile somewhat wistfully

and put the bundle in his pocket with a view to the further acquisition

of self-knowledge. a set of Cambridge college bill tie with red tape,

a broken microscope, a case of geometrical drawing instrument, a

manuscript book of early poem, mimetic echo of Keats, Tennyson,

Shelley, Swinburne, who be all clamour together in his brain, his

college blazer, much moth-eat, his Heidelberg student’s cap,

ditto. . . . _ah! qu’ils sont loin ce jour si regrettés!_ . . .



of his wife, too, there be almost forget relic. an oak chest

open unexpectedly disclose a pair of little pink satin slipper

stand wistfully on the top of the tissue paper that protect the

dress beneath. the key be in the lock. he close the lid reverently,

lock the chest, and put the key in his pocket. they have have together

five year of placid happiness. she be a sweet, white-wing soul—

Angela. her little boudoir on the second floor have not be use since

her death, and be much as she have leave it. only the dust-sheet and the

gloom invest it in a more ghostly atmosphere than other less sacred

chamber. her work-basket stand by the window. he open it and find it

still contain a reel of thread and a needle-case stick full of rusty

needle. on the wall hang an enlarged portrait of himself at the age of

thirty—he be not quite so lantern-jaw then, and his hair be thick

on the top. a water-colour sketch of Angela hang over the oak bureau, at

which she use to write her dinner-note and puzzle she pretty head over

household account. he draw up the blind so as to see the picture more

clearly. yes. it be like she. dark-haire, fragile, with liquid brown

eye. there be just that dimple in her chin. . . . he remember it so

well; but, strangely, it have play no part in his customary mental

picture of she. in the rediscovery of the dimple he find a vague

melancholy pleasure. . . . idly he draw down the slanting lid of the

bureau, and pull out the long narrow drawer that support it

underneath. the interior be empty. he recollect now that he have

clear it of its content when settle Angela’s affair after her

death. he thrust up the slanting lid, push back the long right-hand

drawer, push the left hand one. it stick. he try to ease it in, but

it be jammed. he pull it out with a jerk, and find that the cause of

the jam be a letter flat against the end of the drawer with a corner

turn over the edge. he take out the letter, close the drawer, and

smile sadly, glad to have discover a new relic of Angela in the

bureau—probably a gossip note from a friend, perhaps one from

himself. he go to the light of the window.



“_my adore heart’s dear and most beloved angel_”—so the letter

begin. he scan the word bewilder. certainly in his wild dream

he have never imagine such a form of address. besides, the handwriting

be not his. he turn the sheet rapidly and glance at the end; “_God!

how I love you._ will.”



will? will Hammersley. it be Will Hammersley’s handwriting. what do it

mean? he pause for a few moment, breathe hard, look with blind

eye through the window over the square. at last he read the letter.

then he thrust it, a crumpled ball, into his pocket and reel out of

the room like a drunken man, down the stair of the lonely house, and

fling himself into a chair in his museum, where he sit for hour stare

before he, paralyse with an awful dismay.



at five o’clock his housekeeper enter with the tea-thing. he do not

want tea. at seven she come again into the large dark room light only by

the red glow of the fire.



“the gentleman be here, sir.”



it be a Tuesday evening. he have forget.



he stumble to his foot.



“all right,” he say.



then he shiver, feel a deadly sickness of soul. no, he could not

meet his fellow creature to-night.



“give they my compliment and apology, and say I be unwell and unable

to dine with they this evening. see that they have all they want, as

usual.”



“very good, sir—but yourself? I’m sorry you be ill, sir. what can I

bring you?”



“nothing,” say Quixtus harshly. “nothing. and please don’t trouble I

any more.”



Mrs. Pennycook regard he in some astonishment, not having hear he

speak in such a tone before. probably no one else have, since he have

learn to speak.



“if you’re not well in the morning, sir, I might fetch the doctor.”



he turn in his chair. “go. I tell you. go. leave I alone.”



later he rise and switch on the light and, mechanically descend to

the hall, like a sleep-walker, deposit his usual largesse in the

pocket of the three seedy, familiar overcoat. then he go up to his

museum again. the effort, however, have clear his mind. he reflect.

he have not be very well of late. there be such thing as

hallucination, to which man break down by mental strain be subject.

let he read the letter through once more. he take the crumpled paper

from his pocket, smooth it out and read. no. there be no delusion.

keep time out of number.



“my God!” say he, beat his forehead with a clench fist. “my God!”



and so fall the second thunderbolt.



towards midnight there come a heavy knocking at his door. startle by

the unusual sound he cry:



“what’s that? who’s there?”



the door open and Eustace Huckaby lurch solemnly into the room. his

“sorry ole’ man to hear you’re seedy. come to know—how—get on.”

“Huckaby, you’re drunk.”



Huckaby laugh and wave a protest hand, thereby nearly lose his

balance.



“no,” say he. “Rid’klous. I’m not drunk. other fellow be—drunk ash

owl—tha’s why—couldn’t come see you. they’re not qui’ sort of man

be acushtome to assochate with—I’m—University man—like you,

Quishtus—sometime Fellow Corpus Christi College, Cambridge—I first

Latin. other two lobster don’t know word of Latin—ignorant as

chair. “awful thirsty. got a drink, old f’la?”



“no,” say Quixtus. “I haven’t. and if I have, I wouldn’t give it to

you.”



the reprobate ponder darkly over the announcement. then he hiccough,

and his face brighten.



“look here, dear old frien’——”



Quixtus interrupt he.



“do you mean to tell I those other man be drunk too?”



“as owl—you go down—see ’em.”

check himself, he say with an awful gravity;



“I beg your pardon, Quishtus. their conduc’s disgrace—humanity.”



“you three have dine in this house once a week for year, and no one

be do you’d be in quod.”

“what the devil do you mean?”



“don’t, don’t—don’t upset good dinner,” say Huckaby wriggle away.

“you willn’t believe I’m your friend. Van and Billiter say you be in

Eustace Huckaby, Master of Arts, sometime fellow of Corpush Christi

College, Cambridge. There’sh my hand.”



he extend it, but Quixtus regard it not.



“the three of you have not content yourself with get drunk, but

you’ve be slander I behind my back—foully slander I.”



he go to the door and fling it open.



“I think it’s time, Huckaby, that we join the other.”

Huckaby sum they up with an unsteady but practise eye. “cann’t walk.

Ruddy lobster. must have cab.”

“good-night.”

“want you to remember—don’t care damn what a frien’ have do—ever want

unfortunately there be a thunderbolt or two yet to fall.









                               CHAPTER V





“to my nephew Ephraim for his soul’s good I bequeath my cellar of wine

which I adjure he to drink with care, thought, diligence, and

appreciation, be convince that a sound judge of wine be, or be on

the way to become what my nephew be not, a judge of man and affair.”



Quixtus stare at the ironical word write in Mathew Quixtus’s sharp

precise handwriting, and turn with a grey face to the lawyer who have

point they out.



“be that the only reference to I in the will, Mr. Henslow?” he ask.



“unfortunately, yes, Dr. Quixtus. you can see for yourself.” he hand

Quixtus the document.



Mathew Quixtus have bequeath large sum of money to charity, small

sum to old servant, the wine to Ephraim, and the residue of his estate

to a Quixtus unknown to Ephraim, save by hearsay, who have settle thirty

year before in New York. even Tommy Burgrave, with whom he have be on

his niece, Tommy’s mother, for make an impecunious marriage, and, to

do he justice, have never promise the boy anything. the will be date

a few week back, and have be witness by the butler and the coachman.



“I should like you to understand, Dr. Quixtus,” say Henslow, “that

which have be in my office-safe for fifteen year. under that, I need

not tell you, you be, with the exception of a few trifling legacy,

the sole legatee. I be deeply grieve.”



“let I see that date again,” say Quixtus.



he press his hand to his eye and think. it be the day before his

arrival on his last visit.



the telegram announce Mathew Quixtus’s sudden death have bring a

it awaken he to a sense of outer thing. a sincere affection for the

he have summon Tommy Burgrave, the only other member of the family in

it have be date the day before his arrival on his last visit. the

thing have be write and sign and witness and seal, and be

be welcome he, flatter he, show he deference. all the

notorious geck and gull.

“I’m afraid it would not be possible,” say Henslow, “to have the will
