“it be a heartless jest,” the lawyer agree.



“if you will do I a service, Mr. Henslow, you might be kind enough to

“there’s not a decent train for two or three hour.”

he walk out of the room and out of the house, and after wait at the

in his corner of the railway carriage the much-afflict man sit

Billiter, Mathew Quixtus? if the same hell-streak run through the soul

of these, surely it must run through the soul of all the son and

daughter of Adam. now here come the great puzzle. why should he,

Ephraim Quixtus, (as far as he could tell) vary from the unkindly race

of man? why hitherto have baseness, treachery, and cruelty be as

homicide? why have he be unequipped with these quality which appear

to serve mortal as weapon wherewith to fight the common battle of

that he have go to the wall through lack of they be obvious, too.

instead of the dagger of baseness, the sword of cruelty, the shield of

he should have be, he would have out-manœuvre Marrable at his own

his uncle into the belief that he be a devil of a fellow, and now be

dunghill of a world. but he have be hopelessly outmatch. whoever have

take upon himself the responsibility of equip he for the battle of

but it would be idiotic as well as blasphemous to call on he to make

one bad. how, then, be the essential quality of baseness, treachery,

towards Macathiel—the name suggest a merciless, bowelless,

the train arrive an hour late, as slow train be apt to do, and it be

even a man wallow in the deep abyss of spiritual misery need

he stride down the passage to the head of the kitchen stair and open

it be then that a constricting thread snap in Quixtus’s brain. it

just as the boil make Job, who have bear the annihilation of his family

the glorious thrill of a remorseless, relentless malignity.



he throw up his hand and laugh aloud, peal of unearthly laughter

Hyde, like Caligula, be also the man for he. don’t he once throw a

and chilly.



this be a degenerate age. a couple of century ago Quixtus could have

man a ship with cut-throat, hoist the skull and cross-bone, and

become the Terror of the Seas. or, at a more recent date, if he have be

a Corsican he could have take his gun and go into the maquis and

declare war on the island. if he have live in the fourteenth century he

could have become a condottiere after the fashion of the gentle Duke

Guarnieri, who, wear on his breast a silver badge with the

have take a queerly curve, businesslike knife and run amuck, to his

nor be this all. owe to some flaw in our system of education, Quixtus

have not be train to deed of violence; no one have even set before

the stone-axe of the quaternary age; we have the subtler vengeance of

chicane, and of the elementary principle of financial dishonesty that

guide the nefariousness of folk like “Gehenna, unlimited.”



it must be admit, therefore, that Quixtus enter on his career of

the grey light of a hopeless May dawn be just begin to outline the

above a whisper.



“for Gord’s sake, guv’nor, spare a poor man a copper or two. I’ve not

taste food for twenty-four hour.”



Quixtus stop, his instinctive finger dive into his pence-pocket.

suddenly an idea strike he.



“you must have lead a very evil life,” say he, “to have come to this

stage of destitution.”



“Whatcher gettin’ at?” growl the applicant, one eye fix suspiciously

“I’m not go to preach to you—far from it,” say Quixtus; “but I

should like to know. you must have see a great deal of wickedness in

“if you arsk I,” opine the man, “there’s nothing but wickedness in

this blankety blank world.”



he do not say “blankety blank,” but use other and more lurid epithet

which, though they be not exactly the one that Quixtus himself would

have choose, at least show he that his companion and himself be

agree on their fundamental conception of the universe.



“if you will tell I where I can find some,” he say, “I will give you

half a crown.”



a glimmer of astonished interest light up the man’s dull eye. “Whatcher

want to know for?”



“that’s my business,” say Quixtus.



the cabman, suddenly awaken, see the possibility of a fare. he

clamber out of the vehicle.



“Cab, sir?” he call across the road.



“yes,” say Quixtus.



“‘arf a crown?” say the battered man.



“certainly,” say Quixtus.



“then I’ll tell yer, guv’nor. I’ve be a bookie’s tout, see? not a

slap-up bookie in the ring—but an outside one—one what do a bit of

welshing when he could, see?—and what I say be, that I seed more

wickedness there than anywhere else. if you want to see blankety blank

wickedness you go on the turf.” he clear his throat, but his whisper

“you look very ill,” say he.



“Consumpshon,” breathe the man.



Quixtus shiver. the cabman, who have hastily dispossess the dejected

“I thank you very much for your information,” say Quixtus. “here’s half

a sovereign.”



Voicelessness and wonder provoke an inarticulate wheeze like the

“what a fool! what a monumental fool I’ve be!” he cry.



he have just realise that the devil have offer he as pretty a little

without pay the half-crown he have promise. to have let the very

first opportunity slip through his finger! he would have to wear a

badge like that of the gentle Duke Guarnieri to keep his wit from

wandering.



when he reach home he look for a moment into the little room at the

head of the kitchen stair. the Blissful One still sleep, a happy smile

on her face, and the paper pin to her apron.



there be surely some chance of wickedness here. Quixtus _furen_

scratch an inventive head. suppose he carry she outside and set she

on the doorstep. he regard she critically. she be buxom—about twelve

stone. he be a spare and unathletic man. a great yawn interrupt his

speculation, and turn off the light he stumble off sleepily and

wearily to bed.









                               CHAPTER VI





the Blissful one carry out her master’s write injunction. he do not

near butcher, buy a fibrous organic substance which he assert to

“I give it to you for yourself, my good woman,” say he, pride himself

on his murderous intent. “I’ll get lunch elsewhere.”



he back to his club, for the first time for many day. and this mark

his reappearance in the great world.

“my dear Quixtus. how good it be to see you again.”



he be a bald, pink-faced little man, wear great round gold

“the monkey’s tail peep out between their coat tail?” Quixtus ask

“no. no. it’s only Griffiths.” griffith be the Vice-President. “he

“we know how Primitive Man in most of the epoch slay his enemy,

cook his food, and adorn or disfigure his person; but of the subtle

working of his malignant mind we be hopelessly ignorant.”



“I don’t suppose his mind be more essentially malignant than yours or

“quite so,” Quixtus agree. “but we can study the malignancy, the

“but, my dear man,” smile Wonnacott, “you be get into the province

“it’s the only science worth study,” say Quixtus. then, after a

“sometimes—yes,” laugh the other, startle at the unexpectedness of

“possibly,” reply Wonnacott, apologetically, “but I’ve never see any

myself.”



Quixtus musingly butter a piece of biscuit. “that’s a pity. a great

pity. I be think of go on the turf. I be tell that nowhere else

one or two of Wonnacott’s smile drop, as it be, from his face and

he drink the remainder of his glass of white wine, and throw his napkin

“let we go and smoke,” say he.



in the smoking-room, Wonnacott, still observe he narrowly, ask he

“I tell you I be go to take up the study of criminology. it’s a

Wonnacott explain, and for the comfortable half-hour of coffee and

“I must have be mistake after all.”

either avoid he altogether, or hit he over the head out of

on the other hand, the newly bear idea of the study of criminology

thus he enter upon a new phase of life. he begin to mingle again with

one afternoon, after deep and dark excogitation, he proceed to Romney

manifestly there be something wrong in his psychological economy which

need a guide in the Whole Art and Practice of Wickedness?



he walk up and down his museum in anxious thought. at last a smile light

up his gaunt feature. he sit down and write note of invitation to

Huckaby, Vandermeer, and Billiter to dinner on the following Tuesday.









                              CHAPTER VII





Quixtus receive they in the museum, a long room mainly furnish with

speciman case whose glass top form a double incline plane, diagram

of geological formation, and bookcase full of palæontological

literature—a cold, inhuman, inhospitable place. the three look more

dilapidated than ever. Huckaby’s straggle whisker have grow deeply

into his cheek; Vandermeer’s face have become foxy, Billiter’s more

pallid and puffy. no overcoat hang on the accustomed peg, for the

cessation of the eleemosynary deposit have lead, among other misfortune,

to the pawning of these once indispensable article of attire. the three

wear, therefore, the dismally apologetic appearance of the man who have

no wedding garment. the only one of they who put on a simulated

heartiness of address be Billiter. he thrust out a shaky hand—



“my dear Quixtus, how delightful——”



but the sight of his host’s unwelcoming face chill his enthusiasm.

Quixtus bow slightly and motion they, with his grave courtesy, to

comfortless seat. he command the situation. so might a scholar prince

of the school of Machiavelli have receive his chief poisoner,

strangler, and confidential abductor. they go down to dinner. it be

not an hilarious meal. the conversation which use to flow now fall in

spattering drop amid a dead silence.



“it’s a fine day,” say Quixtus.



“very,” say Huckaby.



“Finer than yesterday,” say Vandermeer.



“it promise well for to-morrow,” say Billiter.



“it always break its promise,” say Quixtus.



“H’m,” say Huckaby.



they make up for the lack feast of reason by material voracity. a

microscopic uplifting of Spriggs the butler’s eyebrow betoken wonder

at their gargantuan helping. Vandermeer, sit at the foot of the

table opposite to Quixtus, bend his foxy face downwards till the

circumference of the plate become the horizon of his universe. Billiter

eat with stolid cynicism; Huckaby, with a faint air of bravado. once he

say:



“I’m afraid Quixtus we get a bit merry the last time.”



“it’s to the memory of that,” reply Quixtus; “that I owe the pleasure

of your company to-night.”



“I’m beastly sorry—” begin Billiter.



“pray don’t mention it,” Quixtus interrupt blandly. “I hope the quail

be to your liking.”



“fine,” say Vandermeer, without raise his eye from his plate.



once more reign the spell of silence which oppress even the three

outcast man; but Quixtus, harden by his fix idea, feel curiously at

his ease. he sit in his chair with the same sense of security and

confidence as he have do before deliver his Presidential Address at

the meeting of the Anthropological Society, while the secretary go

through the preliminary formal business. the preliminary business here

be the meal. as soon, however, as the port have be send round and

Spriggs have retire, Quixtus address his guest.



“gentleman,” say he, and meet in turn the three pair of question

eye. “you may wonder perhaps why I have invite you to dinner to-night,

and why, you be thus invite, the meal have not be warm by its

accustomed glow of geniality. it be my duty and my pleasure now to tell

you. Hitherto at these dinner we have—let we say—wear the comic mask.

beneath its rosy and smile exterior we have dissimulate our own

individual sentiment. we have be actor, without realise it, in an

oft-repeat comedy. only on the occasion of our last meeting do we put

aside the mask and show to each other what we be.”



“I’ve already apologise,” murmur Billiter.



“my dear fellow,” say Quixtus, raise his long thin hand, “that’s the

last thing I want you to do. in this world of fraud and deceit no man

ought to regret having bare his soul honestly to the world. now,

gentleman, I have not ask you here to insult you at my own table. I

have gather you around I because I need your counsel and your

service for which I hope adequately to remunerate you.”



a quiver of animation pass over the three face. “Remunerate” be a

magic word; the master-word of an incantation. it mean money, and money

mean food and drink—especially alcoholic drink.



“I know I be speak for my two friend,” say Huckaby, “when I say

that our heart be always at your service.”



“the heart,” reply Quixtus, “be a physiological organ and a

sentimental delusion. there be no heart in that sense. you know as

well as I do, my dear fellow, that there be no such thing as love,

affection, honour, loyalty in the world. self-interest and

self-indulgence be the guide principle of conduct. govern by a

morbid and futile tradition, we refuse to regard the world in the

malevolent light of day, but see it artificially through the

hypocritical coloured glass of benevolence.”



Huckaby and Vandermeer, who retain the rudiment of an intellect,

look at their once simple-minded and tender-hearted host in blank

bewilderment. they hardly know whether to wince under a highly educate

gentleman’s cutting irony, or to accept these remarkable proposition as

honest statement of opinion. but the ironical note be not perceptible.

Quixtus speak in the same gentle tone of assurance as he would have use

when enter on a dissertation upon the dolichocephalic skull in his

collection which have be find in a long barrow in Yorkshire. he be

the master of a subject lay down incontrovertible fact. so Huckaby

and Vandermeer, marvel greatly, stare at he out of speculative

eye. Billiter, before whom the incautious decanter of port have halt,

lose the drift of his host’s philosophic utterance.



“the time have now come,” continue Quixtus, relighte (unsophisticate

soul!) the cigar which he have allow to go out—“the time have now come

for we four to be honest with one another. up to a recent date I be a

slave to this optical delusion of tradition. but thing have happen to

clear my eye, and to make I frankly confess myself no well than

yourself—an entirely unscrupulous man.”



“pray remember that I’m a sometime fellow—” begin Huckaby.



“I’m a gentleman of good family—” begin Billiter, who have understand

the last sentence.



“yes. yes,” reply Quixtus, interrupt they. “I know. that’s why your

assistance will be valuable. I need the counsel of man of breed and

education. I find from my reading that the vulgar criminal would be

useless for my purpose. now, you all have trust man who have fail

you. so have I. you have feel the cowardly blow of Fortune. so have I.

you have no vestige of faith in your fellow man—you even believe I to

be a party to my late partner’s fraud—you can have, I say, no faith

leave in humanity. neither have I. you be ishmael, your hand against

every man. so be I. you would like to be revenge upon your fellow

rather than good. you, in a word, be entirely unscrupulous. if you will

acknowledge this we can proceed to business. if not; we will part

finally as soon as this agreeable evening be at an end. gentleman what

do you say?”



Billiter, look upon the wine while it be red—there be not much

leave to show the colour—laugh wheezily and shortly.



“I suppose we’re wrong ‘un,” say he. “at least I be. I own up.”



Vandermeer say bitterly: “when a man be hunt by poverty he cann’t run

straight, for at the end of the straight path be death.”



“and you, Huckaby?”



“I also have bolt into a drain or two in my time.”



“good,” say Quixtus. “now we understand one another.”



“you may understand we,” say Huckaby, tug at his untidy beard, “but

I’m hang, draw, and quarter if we understand you.”



“I think I have make myself particularly clear,” say Quixtus.



“for my part,” say Billiter, “I cann’t make out what you’re get at

except to make we confess that we’re wrong ‘un.”



“dear, dear,” say Quixtus.



“I cann’t understand it,” say Vandermeer, look intently at he across

the table out of his little sharp eye. “I cann’t understand it, unless

it be that you have some big scoop on and want we to come into it, so as

to do the dirty work. if that’s so I’m on, so long as it’s safe. but

I’ve steer clear of the law up to now and have no desire to run the

risk of penal servitude.”



“oh Lord no!” cry Billiter with a shiver.



Quixtus press the burn stump of his cigar against his plate and

look up with a smile.



“please make your mind easy on that score. I have be read

criminology lately with considerable interest, and I have go through a

volume or two of ‘the Newgate calendar,’ and the result of my reading be

the conviction that crime be folly. it be a disease. it be also vulgar.

no, I have no desire to increase my personal possession in any way;

neither do I contemplate the commission of act of violence against the

person or the destruction of property. anything therefore that come

within the category of crime may be dismiss from our consideration.”



“then in the name of Gehenna,” exclaim Huckaby, “what be it that you

want we to do?”



“it be very simple,” say Quixtus. “I may plot out an attractive scheme

of wickedness, but the circumstance of my early training have leave I

without the power to execute it. I should like to call on any one of you

for guidance, perhaps practical assistance. I may want to see and hear

of wickedness go on around I. I would count on you to gratify my

curiosity. lastly, not have an inventive mind, it be rather

analytic than synthetic, I should welcome any suggestion that you might

bring I.”



“it’s a rum go,” say Billiter, “but I’m on, so long as there’s money in

it.”



“there will be money in it,” say Quixtus.



“then I’m on too,” say Vandermeer.



“you will find we, my dear Quixtus,” say Huckaby, “your very devoted

familiar—your Oliviers le Daim, your eminence gris, your _âme

damnées_. we’ll be your minister evil spirit, your genius from Eblis.

it’s a new occupation for a Fellow of Corpus Christi College, Cambridge,

but it’s not unallure. and now, as Billiter have finish the decant,

may I take the liberty of ask for another bottle, so that Vandermeer

and I can drink to the health of our chief.”



“with all the pleasure in life,” say Quixtus.



                 *        *        *        *        *



as soon as the three newly constitute Evil Genii be out of earshot of

the house, they stop on the pavement with one accord and burst into

unseemly laughter.



“do you ever hear anything like it?” cry Billiter.



“he’s as mad as Bedlam,” say Vandermeer.



“a sort of invert Knight of the Round Table,” say Huckaby. “he yearn

to ride abroad commit human wrong.”



“be we to call for order every day like the butcher, the baker, and

the greengrocer?” say Vandermeer.



“he be so sane at first,” say Vandermeer, “that I really think he

have some definite scoop in view. but it all turn out to be utter

moonshine.”



“if he don’t want to thieve or murder or paint the town red,” say

Billiter, “what the blaze in the way of wickedness be leave for he to

do?”



“it’s moonshine,” repeat Vandermeer.



“if it ben’t,” say Huckaby, “none of we would touch it. we cann’t take

the matter seriously. we’re just lend ourselves to a farce, that’s

all.”



“naturally,” Billiter agree. “we must humour he.”



they walk on slowly, discuss the unprecedented situation. they be

unanimous in the opinion that the poor gentleman have go distraught.

they have all notice sign of his affliction on the last occasion of

their dining at his table. if he have be in his right sense then, he

would surely not have behave with such discourtesy. they agree to

forgive he for turn they out of door.



“it’s lucky for he,” say Huckaby, “that he have three old friend like

ourselves. he might have get into other hand, and then—God help he.

my only reason for fall in with his mood be in order to protect he

from himself—and from shark and blood-sucker.”



Billiter and Vandermeer declare that they, too, have act only out of a

sense of loyalty to their old and distracted friend. they protest so

hard that their tongue clave to the roof of their mouth, and each

acknowledge his thirst. they turn into the bar-parlour of the first

public-house, where they call for whisky, and, each man having find a

hat as good a substitute for the sack of Joseph’s brother as an

overcoat, they continue to call for whisky, and to drink it until the

tavern close for the night. by that time they glow with conscious

virtue. Huckaby swear that he would permit no ruddy lobster to dig

their claw into Quixtus’s sacred person.



“here’s poor dear old chap’s health, drunk in very last drop,” cry

Billiter, enthusiastically drain his last glass.



the tragedy of Quixtus’s loss of reason reduce Vandermeer to tear. he

be sorrowful in his cup. he, Vandermeer, have no one to love he; but

Quixtus should never find himself in that desolate predicament, as he,

Vandermeer, would love he like a friend, a brother, like a

silver-haired maiden aunt.



“I’ve have a silver-haired maiden aunt myself,” he wail.



while Billiter comfort he, Huckaby again warn they against ruddy

lobster. if they would swear to join he in a league to defend their

patron and benefactor, he would accept their comradeship. if they

prefer to be ruddy lobster, he would wash his hand of they. they

repudiate the crustacean suggestion. they be more Quixtus’s friend

than he. a quarrel nearly break out, each claim to be the most loyal

and disintereste friend Quixtus ever have in his life. finally they be

reconcile and wrung each other warmly by the hand. the barman call

closing time and push they gently into the street. they stagger

deviously to their several garret and go to bed, each certain that he

have convince the two other of his beauty and nobility of soul.



                 *        *        *        *        *



Vandermeer be the first of the Evil Genii to be summon. Quixtus lay

before he the case of Tommy and the failure of his diabolical project.

Vandermeer listen attentively. there be method after all in his

patron’s madness. he wish to do some hurt to his nephew for the sheer

sake of evil-doing. as far as the intention go he be seriously try

to carry out his malevolent principle. it be not all moonshine.

Vandermeer think quickly. he be the craftiest of the three, and that

perhaps be why Quixtus have instinctively choose he for the first

adventure. he see profit in humour the misanthrope, though he smile

inwardly at the simplicity of his idea.



“there’s nothing particularly diabolical in tell a young fellow with

a brilliant career before he that you’re go to cut he out of your

will.”



“ben’t there?” say Quixtus, with an air of disappointment. “what then

would you suggest?”



“first,” answer Vandermeer, “what do you think would be a fair price

for a suggestion?” he regard he with greedy eye. “would twenty

pound be out of the way?”



“I’ll give you twenty pound,” say Quixtus.



Vandermeer draw in his breath quickly, as a man do who win a bet at

long odd.



“there be all sort of thing you can do. the obvious one would be to

stop his allowance. but I take it you want something more artistic and

subtle. wait—let I think—” he cover his eye with his hand for a

moment. “look. how will this do? it strike I as infernally wicked. you

say he be devoted to his art. well, make he give it up——”



“excellent! excellent!” cry Quixtus. “but how?”



“can you get he into any business office in the City?”



“yes. my friend Griffiths of the Anthropological Society be secretary of

the Star Assurance Coy. a word from I would get the boy into the

office.”



“good. then tell he that unless he accept this position within a month

and promise never to touch a paint-brush again, he will not receive a

penny from you either during your lifetime or after your death. in this

way you will bring he up against an infernal temptation, and whichever

way he decide he’ll be wretche. I call that a pretty scheme.”



“it’s an inspiration of genius,” exclaim Quixtus excitedly. “I’ll

write the cheque now.” he sit down to his desk and pull out his

cheque-book. “and you will go at once to my nephew—I’ll give you a card

of introduction—and acquaint he with my decision.”



“what?” cry Vandermeer.



Quixtus calmly repeat the last sentence. Vandermeer’s face go a

shade paler. he wring his hand, which be naturally damp, until they

grow as bloodless as putty. he have never do any wanton harm in his

life. all the meanness and sharp-dealing he have practise be but a

poor devil’s shift to fill an empty belly. Quixtus’s behest cover he

with dismay. it be unexpected. it be one thing to suggest to a crazy

and unpractical patron a theoretical fantasia of wickedness, and another

to be command to put it oneself into execution. it be less moonshine

than ever.



“don’t you want to do it?” ask Quixtus, unwittingly balance

temptation, in the form of a fat cheque-book, in his hand.



Vandermeer fall. what wolf-eyed son of Hagar would have resist?



“I think,” say he, with a catch in his throat, “that if the suggestion

alone be worth twenty pound, the carrying out of it be worth—say—ten

more?”



“very well,” say Quixtus; “but,” he add drily, “the next time I hope

you’ll give an estimate to cover the whole operation.”



the second of the three to receive a summon from the Master be

Billiter.



“you know something about horse-racing,” remark Quixtus.



“what I don’t ben’t worth know. I’ve chuck away a fortune in

acquire the knowledge.”



“I want you to accompany I to race-meeting and show I the wickedness

of the turf,” say Quixtus.



“so that’s my little job be it?”



“that’s your little job.”



“I think I can give you a run for your money,” remark Billiter, a pale

sunshine of intelligence overspread his puffy feature. “but—” he

pause.



“but what?”



“I cann’t go race with you in this kit.”



“I will provide you,” say Quixtus, “with whatever costume you think

necessary for the purpose.”



Billiter go his way exult and spend the remainder of the afternoon

in track a man down from his office in Soho, his house in Peckham,

several tavern on the Surrey side of the river, to a quiet café in

Regent Street. the man be a red-faced, thick-necked, hard, fishy-eye

villain with a mouth like the slit of a letter-box, and go by the name

(which he wear inscribe on his hat at race-meeting) of Old Joe Jenks.

Billiter draw he into a corner and whisper gleeful tiding into his

ear. after which Old Joe Jenks draw Billiter to a table and fill he

up with the most seductive drink the café could provide.



before the lesson in horse-racing under Billiter’s auspex begin—for

gorgeous raiment, appropriate to Sandown and Kempton, like Rome, be not

build in a day—Quixtus send for the remain evil Genius.



“what have you to suggest?” he ask after some preliminary and

explanatory conversation.



a humorous twinkle come into Huckaby’s eye, and a smile play round his

lip beneath the straggling brushwood of hair.



“I have a great idea,” he say.



“what be it?”



“break a woman’s heart,” say Huckaby.



Quixtus reflect gravely. it would indeed be a charming, entice piece

of wickedness.



“I shouldn’t have to marry she?” he ask in some concern.



“Heaven forbid.”



“I like it,” say Quixtus, lean back in his chair and smooth his

scrappy moustache with his lean finger. “I like it very much. the only

difficulty be: where can I find the woman whose heart I can break?”



“take a tour abroad,” say Huckaby. “on the Continent of Europe there

be thousand of english woman only wait to have their heart

break.”



“that may be true,” say Quixtus; “but how shall I obtain the necessary

introduction?”



“I,” cry Huckaby raise a bony hand that protrude through a very

frayed and dirty shirt-cuff. “I, Eustace Huckaby, will reassume my air

of academical distinction and will accompany you into the _pay du

tendre_ and introduce you to any woman you like. in other word, my dear

Quixtus, although I may not look like a Lothario at the present moment,

I have have considerable experience in amatory adventure—and I’m sure

you would find my assistance valuable.”



Quixtus reflect again. aware of his limitation he recognise the

futility of go alone on a heart-break expedition among strange

even though expectant female. but would Huckaby be an ideal companion?

Huckaby be self-assertive, not to say impudent, in manner; and Huckaby

have certain shocking habit. on the other hand, perhaps the impudence

be the very quality need in the quest; and as for the habit—he

decide.



“very well. I accept your proposal—on one condition. what that be you

doubtless can guess.”



“I can,” say Huckaby. “I give you my word of honour that you will never

see I otherwise than sober.”



an undertaking which would not preclude he from take a bottle of

whisky to bed whenever he feel so inclined.



“we have well start at once,” say Huckaby, after some necessary

discussion of the question of wardrobe.



“I must wait,” reply Quixtus, “until I’ve attend some race-meeting

with Billiter.”



Huckaby frown. he be not aware that to Billiter have already be

assign a sphere of action.



“I don’t want to say anything unfriendly. but if I be you I shouldn’t

trust Billiter too implicitly. he’s a—” he pause—be sober and

serious he reject the scarlet epithet which, when use in allusion to

his friend, have give colour to his gayer speech—“He’s a man who know

too much of the game.”



“my dear Huckaby,” say Quixtus. “I shall never trust another human

be as long as I live.”



that evening, somewhat wonder that he have hear no news of Tommy or

of Vandermeer, he unlock the iron safe in his museum and take out his

will. he light a candle and set it by the hearth. now be the time to

destroy the benevolent document. he put it near the flame; then draw it

back. a new thought occur to he. to practise on his nephew the same

trick as his uncle have play upon he be mere unintelligent

plagiarism. he feel a sudden disdain for the merely mimetic in

wickedness.



“I will be original,” say he. “yes, original.” he repeat the word as

a formula both of consolation and incentive, and blow out the candle,

put the will back into the safe.









                              CHAPTER VIII





“Lord have mercy upon we!” cry Clementina.



the pious ejaculation be in the nature of a reply to Miss Etta

Concannon, the fragile slip of a girl whose portrait she have paint and

in whose cornflower-blue eye she have catch the haunt fear. there

be no fear, however, in the eye to-day. they be bright, direct, and

abnormally serious. she have just announce her intention of become a

hospital nurse. Whereupon Clementina have cry: “Lord have mercy upon

we!”



now it must be state that Etta Concannon have bestow on an embarrassed

Clementina her young and ardent affection; secretly, during the sitting

for the portrait which her father have commission Clementina to paint

as a wedding present, and openly; when the sitting be end and she

call upon Clementina as a friend. in the first flush of this avow

adoration she would send shy little note, ask whether she might come

to the studio to tea. as she live quite close by, the missive be

despatch by hand. Clementina, disturb in the midst of her painting,

would tear a ragged corner from the first bit of paper her eye fall

upon—note-paper, brown-paper, cartridge-paper—once it be

sand-paper—scribble “yes” on it with a bit of charcoal and send it out

to the wait messenger. at last she be drive to desperation.



“my good child,” she say, “cann’t you drop in to tea without put I

to this elaborate correspondence?”



Etta Concannon think she could, and thence-forward come to tea

unheralded, and, eventually such be her power of seduction that she

entice Clementina to her own little den in her father’s house in Cheyne

Walk—a fairy den all water-colour and gossamer very much like herself,

in which Clementina give the impression of an ogress who have blunder

in by mistake. it be on the first visit that Clementina repudiate the

name of Miss Wing. she hate and loathe it. on Etta’s lip it suggest

a prim, starch governess—the conventional french caricature of the

English Old Maid with long tooth and sharp elbow. she might be an old

maid, but she ben’t a prim governess. everybody call her Clementina.

upon which, to her profess discomfort, Etta throw her arm round her

neck and kiss she and call she a darling. why Clementina waste she

time over this chit of a girl she be at a loss to conjecture. she be

about as much use in the world as a rainbow. yet for some fool reason

(her own expression) Clementina encourage she, and feel less grim in

her company. the odd part of their intercourse be that the one thing

under heaven they do not talk about be the bullet-head, bull-neck

young man to whom Etta be engage—not until one day when, in response

to the follow epistle, Clementina brusquely dismiss her sitter,

skewer on a battered hat, and rush round to Cheyne Walk.



“my dearest, dearest Clementina,—Do come to I. I be in abject misery.

the very bad have happen. I would come to you, but I’m not fit to be

see.



                                                  your own unhappy

                                                               “Etta.”



“my poor child,” cry Clementina, as she enter the bower and beheld a

very dim and watery fairy sob on a couch. “who have be do this

to you?”



“it’s r-Raymond,” say Etta, chokingly.



to her astonishment Clementina find herself sit on the couch with

her arm round the girl. now and then she do the most idiotic thing

without know in the least why she do they. in this position she

listen to Etta’s heartrende story. it be much involve, here and

there incoherent, tell with singular disregard of chronological

sequence. when properly piece together and shorn of irrelevance, this

be what it amount to:



certain doing of the bullet-head young man, doing not at all

creditable—mean and brutal doing indeed—have reach the ear of

Etta’s father. now Etta’s father be a retired admiral, and Etta the

beloved child of his old age. the report of Captain Hilyard’s doing have

wound he in his weak spot. in a fine fury he telephonically

command the alleged wrongdoer to wait upon he without delay. captain

Hilyard obey. the scene of the interview be a private room in the

service club to which Admiral Concannon belong. Admiral Concannon go

straight to the point—it be an uncomfortable characteristic of british

admiral. the bullet-head young man not be able to deny the charge

bring against he, Admiral Concannon express himself in such term

as be only polished to their bright perfection on the quarter-deck

of a man-of-war. the young man show resentment—amazing impudence,

accord to the Admiral—whereupon the Admiral consign he to the

devil and charge he never to let he (the Admiral) catch he (the

bullet-head young man) lift his scoundrelly eye again to an

innocent young girl. Admiral Concannon come home and tell his daughter

as much of the tale of turpitude as be meet for her ear. captain

Hilyard repair forthwith in unrighteous wrath to his quarter and

pack off Etta’s letter, with a cover note in which he insinuate

that he be not sorry to have see the last of her amiable family. it

have all happen that day.



hence the tear.



“I think you write I that the bad have happen,” say Clementina.



“well, haven’t it?”



“Good Lord!” cry Clementina. “it’s the very good thing that ever

happen to you in all your bear day.”



in the course of a week Clementina bring the sorrow damsel round to

her own way of thinking.



“do you know,” say Etta, “I use to be rather afraid of he.”



“any fool could see that,” say Clementina.



“do you guess?” this with wide-open cornflower eye.



“look at your portrait and you’ll see,” say Clementina, mindful of the

avalanche of memory which the portrait of Tommy Burgrave’s

rough-and-ready criticism of the bullet-head young man have start on

its overwhelming career. “have you ever look at it?”



“of course I have.”



“to look at a thing and to see it,” remark Clementina, “be two

entirely different proposition. for instance, you look at that young

man, but you don’t see he. yet your soul see he and be afraid. your

father too—I cann’t understand what he be about when he consent to

the engagement.”



“Captain Hilyard’s father and he be old mess-mate,” say Etta.



“old messmaker!” snap Clementina. “and what make you accept he?”



Etta look mournful. “I don’t know.”



“the next time you engage yourself to a young man, just be sure that you

do know. I suppose this one say, ‘dilly, dilly, come and be kill,’

and you go like the foolish little geese in the nursery rhyme.”



“they be duck, dear,” laugh Etta, take Clementina’s grim face

between her dainty hand. “duck like you.”



Clementina suffer the caress with a wry mouth.



“I think you’re get well,” she say. “and I’m jolly glad of it. to

have one young idiot on my hand ill with congestion of the lung and

another ill with congestion of the heart—both at the same time—be more

than I bargain for. I suppose you think I’m a sort of Sister of

Charity. why don’t you do as your father tell you and go down to your

Aunt What’s-she-name in Somersetshire?”



Etta make a grimace. “Aunt Elmira would drive I crazy. you’re much more

wholesome for I. and as for father”—she toss her pretty head—“he

have to do what he’s tell.”



so Etta remain in town, her convalescence synchronise with that of

Tommy Burgrave. Clementina begin to find time to breathe and to make up

arrear of work. as soon as Tommy be able to take his walk abroad, and

Etta to seek distraction in the society of her acquaintance, Clementina

shut herself up in her studio, forbid the young people to come near

she, and for a week paint the livelong day. at last, one morning two

piteous letter be smuggle almost simultaneously into the studio.



“. . . I haven’t see you for month and month. do let I come to

dinner to-night. . . . Tommy.”



“. . . oh darling, do come to tea this afternoon. . . . Etta.”



“I shall go and paint in the Sahara,” cry Clementina. but she seize

two dirty scrap of paper and scrawl on they:



“Lord, yes, child, come to dinner.”



“Lord, yes, child, I’ll come to tea.” and having fold they crookedly

despatch they to her young friend.



it be during this visit of Clementina to the fairy bower in Cheyne Walk

that Etta inform she of her intention of become a hospital nurse.



“Lord have mercy upon we!” cry Clementina.



“I don’t see why I shouldn’t,” say Etta.



“the idea be preposterous,” reply Clementina. “what need have you to

work for your living?”



“I want to do something useful in the world.”



“you’ll do much well by remain ornamental,” say Clementina. “it’s

only when a woman be as ugly as sin and as poor as charity that she need

be useful; that’s to say while she’s unmarried. when she’s married she

have get as much as she can do to keep her husband and child in order.

a girl like you with plenty of money and the devil’s own prettiness have

get to stay at home and fulfil her destiny.”



Etta, sit on the window seat, look at the Thames, see in patch

of silver through the fresh greenery of the Embankment tree.



“I know what you’re think of, dear,” she say, with the indulgent

solemnity of the Reverend Mother of a Convent, “but I shall never

marry.”



“Rubbish,” say Clementina.



“I’ve make up my mind, quite make up my mind.”



Clementina sigh. youth be so solemn, so futile, so like the youth of

all the generation that have pass away. the child be suffer from

one of the natural sequelæ of a ruptured engagement. once maiden in her

predicament gat they into nunnery and become nun and that be the end

of they. whether they regret their rash act or not, who can say?

nowadays they rush into philanthropic or political activity, contrive

happy evening for costermonger or unhappy afternoon for Cabinet

Ministers. the impulse drive they to nunnery, Whitechapel, or Caxton

Hall have always be merely a reaction of sex; and the duration of the

period of reaction be proportionate to the degree of brokenness of the

heart. as soon as the heart be mend, sex have her triumphant way again

and leap in response to the eternal foolishness that the maiden blush

to read in the eye of a comely creature in trouser. this Clementina

know, as all those—and only those—whose youth be behind they know it;

and so, when Etta with an air of cold finality say that she have make up

her mind, Clementina sigh. it be so ludicrously pathetic. Etta’s

heart have not even be break; it have not sustain the wee-est,

tiniest fracture; it have be roughly handle; that be all. in a

month’s time she would no more yearn to become a hospital nurse than to

follow the profession of a chimney-sweep. in a month’s time she would be

flirt with merry, whole-hearted outrageousness. in a month’s time, if

the True Prince come along, she might be in love. really in love. what a

wonderful gift to a man would be the love of this fragrant wisp of

womanhood!



“I’ve quite make up my mind, dear,” she repeat.



“then there’s nothing more to be say,” reply Clementina.



a shade of disappointment spread over the girl’s face, like a little

cloud over a May morning. she jump from the window-seat and slide to a

stool by Clementina’s chair.



“but there’s lot to be say. lot. it’s a tremendously important

decision in life.”



“tremendous,” say Clementina.



“it mean that I’ll die an old maid.”



“like I,” say Clementina.



“if I’m like you I willn’t care a bit!”



“Lord save we,” say Clementina.



the girl actually take it for grant that she enjoy be an old

maid.



“I’ll have a little house in the country all cover with honeysuckle,

and a pony-trap and a dog and a cat and you’ll come and stay with I.”



“I think you be go to be a hospital nurse,” say Clementina.



“so I be; but I’ll live in the house when I’m off duty.”



Clementina roll a cigarette. Etta kneel bolt upright and offer a

light match. now when a lissom-figure girl kneel bolt upright, with

a shapely head throw ever so little back, and stretch out her arm,

there be few thing more adorable in this world of beauty. Clementina

look at she for full ten second with the eye of a Moses on Mount

Nebo—suppose (a bewilder hypothesis) that Moses have be an artist

and a woman—and then, disregard cigarette and light match, she

lay her hand on the girl’s shoulder and shake she gently so that she

sink back on her heel, and the match go out.



“oh, you dear, delightful, silly, silly child.”



she rise abruptly and go to the mantelpiece and light the cigarette for

herself. Etta laugh in blushing confusion.



“but darling, nurse do have time off now and then.”



“I ben’t think about nurse at all,” say Clementina.



“then what be you think of?” ask Etta; still sit on her heel

and crane her head round.



“never mind,” say Clementina. “but what will you want an old frump like

I in your house for?”



“to listen to my trouble,” say the girl.



Clementina walk home through the soft May sunshine, a smile twinkle

in her little beady eye and the corner of her lip twist into an

expression of deep melancholy. if she have be ten year young there

would have be no smile in her eye. if she have be ten year old a

corroborative smile would have play about her lip. but at thirty-five

a woman in Clementina’s plight often do not know whether to laugh or

to cry, and if she be a woman with a sense of humour she do both at

once. the eternal promise, the eternal message vibrate through the air.

the woman of five-and-thirty hear it instinctively and reject it

intellectually. she hurry her pace and grip she

umbrella—Clementina always carry a great, untidy, bulge

“see how fresh and young we be.” a very young plane tree look

impudently green; in its dainty fragility it suggest Etta.

eye to the immature impertinence of the spring. but outside the window

splash all over with their crimson heart’ blood, flare like the

sunset flame of a tropical summer. as a hungry tomtit fly straight to

a shred of meat, she go in and buy they.



when she reach her house in Romney Place she peep for the last (and

the hundredth) time into the open mouth of the twisted white paper

cornet.



“they’ll make a nice bit of colour on the dinner-table for Tommy,” she

say to herself.



o Clementina! o woman! what in the name of Astarte have the gold and

crimson reprobate to do with Tommy?



she let herself in with her key, traverse her Sheraton drawing-room,

and open the door lead on to the studio gallery. Tommy be below,

walk up and down like a young wild beast in a cage. his usually tidy

Clementina call out:



“you ask if you could come to dinner. six o’clock ben’t dinner-time.”



“I know,” he cry up at she. “I’ve be here for an hour.”



she go down the spiral staircase and confront he.



“what have you be do to your hair? it’s like Ferdinand’s in _the

Tempest_. and;” notice a new note of violence in the customary

“my uncle have go stick, stark, staring, raving, lunatic mad,” say

he turn on his heel and strode to the other end of the studio.

Clementina throw the parrot-tulip on a chair and draw off her left-hand

old cotton glove, which she cast on the tulip. then for a while, during

Tommy’s retreat and approach, she gaze thoughtfully at the thumb-tip

which protrude from the right-hand glove.



“I’m not at all surprised,” she say, when Tommy join she.

“account for what?”



“what he have do. listen. a week ago he come to see I, as jolly as

could be. you be there——”



“about as jolly as a slug,” say Clementina.



“anyway he be all right. I tell the dear old chap I have unaccountably

exceed my allowance—and he send I a cheque next day, just as he

Vandermeer.’”



“what name?” ask Clementina, prick her ear.



“Vandermeer.”



“go on.”



“I tell the servant to show he in—and in come a dilapidated devil

look like a mangy fox——”



“that’s the man.”



“do you know he?”



“all right. go on.”

Clementina work the thumb-tip through the hole in the right hand glove

until the entire thumb be visible.

quite worthy young man in City office.”



“I would soon go into a stoke-hole,” cry Tommy. “oh, it’s

phantasmagorical!”



he sit down on the platform of the throne and bury his head in his

“cheer up,” say Clementina. “the world haven’t come to an end yet and we

she open a door at the back of the studio that communicate with the

“go to the grocer’s and fetch a bottle of champagne for dinner.”



“yes, ma’am,” say the voice, come nearer. “what kind of champagne?”



“I don’t know,” say Clementina. “but tell he to send the good bottle

he have get.”



“what a good sort you be,” say Tommy.



neither be alarm by the prospective quality of this vaguely select

pass through terror without recognise they.

the young be apt to exaggerate the defect of those who have not gain

“the devil you do,” say Tommy.



Clementina relate the episode and her subsequent conversation with

Quixtus.

“if he’s really off his head, it’s tragic. he be the good and dear

so do Thrones and Dominations, I imagine, speak of the mundane

avocation of a mere Angel.



“if you refuse, you’ll be give up three hundred a year now and heaven

know how much afterwards,” say Clementina.

she respond in her grim way, and bid he amuse himself while she go

at dinner, Tommy, inspire by more than three-fourth of the grocer’s

good bottle of champagne talk glowingly of his prospect in the event

of his uncle’s craziness not be a transitory disorder. after all, the

world be his oyster, and he know the trick of open it. Most people

would sleep in the pig-sty.



for the second time that day she ask:



“what will you want an old frump like I in the house for?”

Clementina sniff. “I think as much,” she say. “really, the callous

Tommy, accustom to her sharp saying, only laugh boyishly. how be

you. it mean nothing.”



“that’s just why I want to sit in it,” say Clementina. “you can go to

the studio, if you like.”



“that wouldn’t be polite,” say Tommy.



she shrug her shoulder and sit down at the piano and play scrap

“have I say or do anything wrong, Clementina? if so, I’m dreadfully

sorry.”



she shut the piano with a bang.



“you poor, motherless babe,” she cry. “whom would you go to with your

trouble, if you haven’t get I?”



Tommy smile vaguely.



“deuce know,” say he.



“then let we go down to the studio and talk about they,” say

Clementina.









                               CHAPTER IX





after leave Clementina, Tommy go for a long brisk walk in order to

clear his mind, and on his homeward way along the Embankment, branch

off to the middle of old Chelsea Bridge in order to admire the moonlight

view; he also take off his hat in order to get cool. the treacherous May

wind cool he effectually and send he to bed for three day with a

chill.



Clementina sit by his rueful bedside and rate he soundly. the idea of

one just recover from pneumonia set his blood boil hot and

then cool himself on a bridge at midnight in the bitter north-east

wind! he be about as sane as his uncle. they be a pretty and

well-match pair. both ought to be place under restraint. a dark house

and a whip would have be their portion in the good old time.



“I’ve get ’em both now,” say Tommy, grin. “this confound bedroom

be my dark house and your tongue be the whip.”



“I hope it hurt like the devil,” say Clementina.



Tommy write from his sick bed a dignified and manly letter to his uncle,

and, like Brutus, pause for a reply. none come. Quixtus read it, and

his warped vision see ingratitude and hypocrisy in every line. he have

already speak to Griffiths about the office-stool in the Star Insurance

Company. Tommy’s emphatic refusal to sit on it place he in an awkward

position with regard to Griffiths. opening in a large insurance office

be not as common as those for hop-picker in August. griffith, a

sour-temper man at time, would be annoy. Quixtus, encourage by

Vandermeer, regard himself as an ill-use uncle, and not only miss

all the thrill of his deed of wickedness, but accept Tommy’s decision

as a rebuff to his purely benevolent intention. he therefore add the

unfortunate Tommy to the list of those whom he have try and find

want. he have a grievance against Tommy. such be the topsyturvydom of

man after a little thread have snap in his brain.



now, it so happen that, on the selfsame day that Tommy crawl again

into the open air, Clementina, stand before her easel and painfully

paint drapery from the lay figure, suddenly feel the whole studio

gyrate in a whirling maelstrom into whose vortex of unconsciousness she

be swiftly suck. she fall in a heap on the floor, and remain there

until she come to with a splitting headache and a sensation of carry

masse of bruise pulp at various corner of her body instead of limb.

her maid, Eliza, find she lie white and ill on the couch to which

she have drag herself, administer water—there be no such thing as

smell-salt in Clementina’s house—and, on her own responsibility,

summon the near doctor. the result of his examination be a

diagnosis of overwork. Clementina jeer. only idler suffer from

overwork. besides, she be as strong as a horse. the doctor remind she

that she be a woman, with a woman’s delicately adjust nervous system.

she also have her sex’s lack of restraint. a man, find that he be

lose sleep, appetite, control of temper and artistic grip, would

abandon work and plunge utterly unashamed into hoggish idleness. a woman

always feel that by fight against weakness she be uphold the

honour of her sex, and struggle on insanely till she drop.



“I’m glad you realise I’m a woman,” say Clementina.



“why?”



“because you’re the first man who have do so for many year.”



the doctor, a youngish man, very earnest, of the modern neuropathic

school, miss the note of irony. this be the first time he have see

Clementina.



“you’re one of the most highly string woman I’ve ever come across,” say

he, gravely. “I want you to appreciate the fact and not to strain the

tension to breaking-point.”



“you wrap it up very nicely,” say Clementina, “but, to put it brutally,

your honest opinion be that I’m just a silly, unreasonable, excitable,

sex-ride fool of a female like a million other. ben’t that so?”



the young doctor bear the scrutiny of those glitter, ironical point

of eye with commendable professional stolidity.



“it be,” say he, and in say it he have the young practitioner’s

horrible conviction that he have lose an influential new patient. but

Clementina stretch out her hand. he take it very gladly.



“I like you,” she say, “because you’re not afraid to talk sense. now

I’ll do whatever you tell I.”



“go away for a complete change—anywhere will do—and don’t think of

work for a month at the very least.”



“all right,” say Clementina.



when Tommy, look very much the bad for his relapse, come in the

next day to report himself in robust health once more, Clementina

acquaint he with her own bodily infirmity. it be absurd, she

declare, that she should break down, but absurdity be the guide

principle of this comic planet. holiday be ordain. she have spend a

sleepless night think how she should make it. Dawn have bring

solution of the problem. why not make it in fantastic fashion,

harmonise with the absurd scheme of thing?



“what be you go to do?” ask Tommy. “spend a frolicsome month in

Whitechapel, or put on male attire and go for a soldier?”



“I shall hire an automobile and motor about France.”



“it’s sport enough,” say Tommy, judicially, “but I should hardly

call it fantastic.”



“wait till you’ve hear the rest,” say Clementina. “I have originally

intend to take Etta Concannon with I; but since you’ve come here

look like three-ha’porth of misery, I’ve decide to take you.”



“I?” cry Tommy. “my dear Clementina, that’s absurd.”



“I think you would agree with I,” say Clementina, “but I’m go to

do it. wouldn’t you like to come?”



“I should think so!” he exclaim, boyishly. “it would be gorgeous.

but——”



“but what?”



“how can I afford to go motor abroad?”



“you wouldn’t have to afford it. you would be my guest.”



“it’s delightful of you, Clementina, to think of it—but it’s

impossible.”



Whereupon an argument arise such as have often arise between man and

woman.



“I’m old enough to be your grandmother, or at least you think so, which

come to the same thing,” say Clementina.



Tommy’s young pride would not allow he to accept largesse from feminine

hand, however elderly and unromantic.



“if I have a country house and host of servant and several motor-car

and ask you to stay, you’d come without hesitation.”



“that would be different. don’t you see for yourself?”



Clementina choose not to see for herself. here be a dolorous baby of a

boy disinherit by a lunatic uncle, emaciate by illness and unable to

work, refuse a help hand just because it be a woman’s. it be

preposterous. Clementina grow angry. Tommy hold firm.



“it’s merely selfish of you. don’t you see I want a companion?”



Tommy point out the companionable quality of Etta Concannon. but she

would not hear of Etta. the sight of Tommy’s wan face have decide she,

and she be a woman who be accustom to carry out her decision. she

be somewhat dictatorial, somewhat hectoring. she have take it into her

head to play fairy godmother to Tommy Burgrave, and she resent his

repudiation of her godmotherdom. besides, there be purely selfish

reason for choose Tommy rather than Etta, which she acknowledge with

inward candour. Tommy be a man who would fetch and carry and keep the

chauffeur up to the mark, and inspire gendarme and custom-house

official and maitre-d’hotel with respect, and, although Clementina

fear neither man nor devil, she be aware of the value of a suit of

clothe fill with a male entity as a travel adjunct to a lone

woman. with Etta the case would be different. Etta would fetch her

motor-veil and carry her glove with the most adoringly submissive grace

in the world; but all the real fetching and carry for the two of they

would have to be do by Clementina herself. therein lie the difference

between Clementina and the type generally know as the emancipate

woman. she have no exaggerated notion of the equality of the sex,

which in feminine logic generally mean the high superiority of woman.

Circumstance have emancipate she from dependence upon the other sex, but

on the circumstance and the emancipation she cast not too favourable an

eye. she have a crystal clear idea of the substantial usefulness of man

in this rough and not always ready cosmic scheme. therefore, for

purpose of utility, she want Tommy. in her usual blunt manner she

tell he so.



“you run in here at all hour of the day and night, and it’s Clementina

this and Clementina that until I cann’t call my soul my own—and now, the

first time I ask you to do I a service you fall back on your silly

little prejudice and vanity and pride, and say you cann’t do it.”



“I’m very sorry,” say Tommy, humbly.



“I tell you what it be,” say Clementina, with a curiously vicious

feminine stroke, “you’d come if I be a smart-look woman with fine

clothe who could be a credit to you—but you willn’t face go about

with an animate rag-and-bone shop like I.”



Tommy flush as pink as only a fair youth can flush; he spring forward

and seize her wrist and, unwittingly, hurt she in his strong and

indignant grip.



“what you’re say be abominable and you ought to be ashamed of

yourself. if I think anything like that I’d be the most infernal cur

that ever trod the earth. I’d like to shake you for dare to say such

thing about I.”



he fling away her hand and stalk off to the other end of the studio,

leave she with tingle wrist and unfindable retort.



“if you really think I can be of service to you,” he say, in a

dignified way, having complete the return journey, “I shall be most

happy to come.”



“I don’t want you to make a martyr of yourself,” she snap.



Tommy consider within himself for a moment or two, then break into his

boyish laugh.



“I’m an ungrateful pig, and I’ll follow you all over the world. dear old

Clementina,” he add, more seriously, put his hand on her shoulder,

“forgive I.”



Clementina gently remove his hand. she prefer the grip on the wrist

that hurt. but, mollify, she forgive he.



so in a few day they start on their travel.



                 *        *        *        *        *



the thirty-five horse-power car whirl they, a happy pair, through the

heart of summer. above the blue sky blaze, and beneath the white road

gleam a shivering streak. the exhilarating wind of their motion fill

their lung and set their tired pulse throb. now and then, for

mile, the great plane tree on each side of the way form the

never-end nave of an infinite cathedral, the roof a miracle of green

tracery. through quiet, sun-bake village they pass, at a snail’s

pace, hoot child and dog from before their path—and because they

proceed slowly and Tommy be goodly to look upon, the woman smile

from their doorway, or from the run laundry stream where they kneel

and beat the wet clothe, or from the fountain in the cool, flag

little square jutting out like a tiny transept from the aisle of the

street. baby stare stolidly. here and there a bunch of little girl,

their hair tie in demure pigtail, the blue sarrau over their loud

check frock; would laugh and whisper, and one more daring than the rest

would wave an audacious hand, and when Tommy blow she a kiss from his

finger there come the little slut’s gracious response, amid mirth and

delight unspeakable. man would look up from their dusty, bare, uneven

bowl-alley beneath the tree and watch they as they go by. an

automobile, in spite of its frequency, be always an event in a french

village. if it race mercilessly through; there be reasonable

opportunity to curse which always gladden the heart of man. if it

proceed slowly and show deference to the inhabitant, it be an event

rare enough to command their admiration. instead of shut their eye

against a sort of hell-chariot in a whirlwind; they can observe the

gracefully build car and its stranger though human occupant, which be

something deserve a note in the record of an eventless day. if they

stop and quit the car so as to glance at leisure at old church or

quaint fountain—and in many an out-of-the-way village in France the

water of the community gush forth from a beautiful work of art—all

the idler of the sunny place cluster round the car, while the british

chauffeur stand by the radiator, impeccably vesture and unembarrasse

as a fate. at noon come the break for déjeuner; preferably in some

little world-forget townlet, where, after the hor-d’œuvre, omelette,

cutlet, chicken, and fruit—and where be the sad, plague-stricken hamlet

of France that cannot, in the twinkling of an eye, provide such a meal

for the hungry wayfarer?—they love to take their coffee beneath the

awning of a café on the shady side of the great, sleepy square, and

absorb the sleepy, sunny, prosperous spirit of the place; the unpainted

bandstand in the centre, the low-lie house with sleepy little shop

and cafés—Heavens! how many cafés!—around it, the modern, model-build

Hôtel de Ville, the fine avenue of plane tree without which no Grande

Place in France could exist, and, above the roof of the house, the

weather-beat, crumble Gothic tower of the church surmount by its

extinguisher-shape leaden belfry alive with vivid yellow and olive.

and then the road again past the rapidly become familiar object; the

slow ox-cart; the herd of wayside goat in charge of a dirty,

tow-head child; the squad of canvas-suit soldier; the great

lumber waggon draw by a string of three gaudily and elaborately

yoked horse, the driver fast asleep on the top of his mountainous load;

the mongrel dog that seek, and happily find not, euthanasia beneath

the wheel of the modern car of Juggernaut; the sober-vest peasant

woman bend beneath their burden with the calm unexpressive face of

caryatide grow old and wither. towards the late afternoon be

reach the large town where they would halt for the night: first come

the eternal, but grateful, outer boulevard cool with foliage, run

between newly build, perky house and shop and then lead into the

heart of the old city, grey, narrow-streete, picturesque. as the

automobile clatter through the great gateway of the hotel into the

paved courtyard, out come the decent landlord and smile landlady,

welcome their guest, summon unshaven man in green-baize apron—who,

at dinner, be to appear in the decorous garb of waiter, and in the

morning, by a subtle modification of costume (dingy white apron instead

of green-baize) be to do uncomplaine work as housemaid—to take

down the luggage, and show the traveller to their clean, bare room.

after the summary removal of the journey’s dust come the delicious

saunter through the strange old town; the stimulus of the sudden burst

into view of the west front of a cathedral, with its deeply recess and

sculpture doorway, and its great, flamboyant window strike by the

westering sun; the quick, indrawn breath of delight when, in a narrow,

evil-smell, cobble-paved street, they come unexpectedly upon some

marvel of an early Renaissance façade, with its refined riot of

ornament, its unerre proportion, its laugh dignity—laugh all

the more and with all the more dignity, as become its mocking,

aristocratic soul, because the ground floor be give up to a dingy

tinsmith and its upper storey to the same class of easy-go,

slatternly folk who sit at the window of the other unconsidered house

in the sallow and homely street; the gay relief of emerge from such

unsavoury and foot-massacre by-way into the quarter of the town on

which the Syndicat d’Initiative pride itself—the wide, well-keep

thoroughfare or _place_ with its inevitable greenery, its flourishing

cafés thick with decorous folk beneath the awning, its proud and

prosperous shop, its Municipal Theatre, Bourse, Hôtel de Ville, its

generously spout fountain, its statue of the great son—poet, artist,

soldier—of the locality; its crowd of well-feed saunterer—fat and

greasy citizen, the supercilious aristocrat and the wolf-eyed anarchist

might perhaps join together in call they—but still God’s very worthy

creature; its general expression, not of the joy of life, for a

provincial town be, as a whole, govern by condition which affect only

                               CHAPTER X

                               CHAPTER XI

                              CHAPTER XII





while Clementina, in her own fashion, be shatter an idyll to piece,

Quixtus under the tutelage of Billiter pursue the most distasteful

occupation in which he have ever engage. have some Rhadamanthine Arbiter

of his destiny compel he, under penalty of death, to choose between

horse-race and laborious practice as a solicitor, he would

unhesitatingly have choose the latter. course and stand and paddock and

ring, the whole machinery of the sport, weary he to exasperation.

just as there be some man to whom, as the saying go, music be the

most expensive form of noise, so be there other to whom the racing of

horse be merely the most extravagantly cumbersome form of gambling. why

train valuable animal, they ask; to run round a field, when the same

end could be attain by make little leaden horse gyrate mechanically

round a disk, at a millionth part of the cost? of the delight of

study pedigree, of follow form, of catch the precious trickle

of information that percolate through the litter of stable, of back

their judgment thus misguide they have no notion. they cannot even feel

a thrill of excitement at the sight of the far-off speck of gallop

horse. they wonder at the futility of it all as the quadruped scrabble

down the straight. an automobile, they plead, can go ten time as fast.

that such purblind folk exist be sad; but after all they be God’s

creature, just the same as jockeys and professional tipster.



at first there be one feature of the race-course which fascinate

Quixtus—the ring. then he imagine he have come into contact with

incarnate evil. those coarse animal face, swell with the effort of

bawl the odd, those hard greedy eye bulge from purple cheek,

those voice raucous, inhuman, suggest to his mild fancy a peculiarly

depraved corner of Tophet. but what practical evil result from this

Masque of Hades be not quite apparent. nobody seem any the bad. the

bookmaker smile widely on those who win, and those who lose smile on

the world with undaunted cheerfulness. so, in the course of time,

Quixtus begin to regard the bookmaker with feeling of disappointment,

which give place after a while to indifference, and eventually to

weariness and irritation.



even Old Joe Jenks, thick-neck, fishy-eyed villain, to whom Billiter

personally introduce he, prove himself, in all his dealing, to be a

scrupulously honest man. the turf, in spite of its depressing ugliness,

appear but a manœuvring ground for the dull virtue. where be its

wickedness? he complain, at length, to Billiter.



Billiter seem for the moment to be in a bad humour. he tug at his

heavy moustache.



“I don’t see what fault you can find with racing. you’re make a very

good thing out of it.”



which be true. Fortune, who have play he such scurvy trick, be now

turn on he her sunniest smile. he be win prodigiously,

fantastically. Billiter select the horse which he be to back, he

back they to the amount advise by Billiter, and in most instance the

horse win.



“if you think the mere gaining of money give I any pleasure, my dear

“if that’s the matter, you can turn they over to I,” say Billiter, “I

don’t get much out of the business.”



they be walk about the paddock, between the race. Quixtus halt

and regard his morose companion with cold inquiry.

be.”



Billiter curse himself for an incautious fool.



“only now and then,” say he, “and for small stake. how can I afford to

plunge like you?”

Quixtus look at his card.



“Punchinello. forty-five to one. dead cert.”



“then,” say Quixtus, “here be five pound. put they on Punchinello and

if he win you will have two hundred and twenty-five.”



Billiter leave he, make his way out of the paddock to that part of the

Old Joe Jenks jump from his stool and take Billiter aside.



“look here, old friend,” say he, “chuck it. come off it. I’m not

stretch out the odd—to oblige you. and you’ve damn well land I.

it’s get monotonous. see? I’m tired.”



“it’s not my fault, Joe,” say Billiter, humbly. “look. just an extra

fiver on Punchinello. he’s get no earthly—you know that as well as I

“do I?” growl the bookmaker angrily, convince that Billiter be

little do I expect he to win that I’ve not ask a penny commission.”



“then ask it now, and be hang to you,” cry the angry bookmaker, and

leap back to his stool, he resume his brazen-throate trade.

stake money? not one. he could lay his hand on his honest heart and

if the devil have get into the horse so that every shocking outsider,

nothing. except have a drink. in the circumstance it be the only thing

his moral tone restore, he determine to live in poverty no more for

seem good to he.



the bell ring, the course be clear, the number put up; the horse

the start be make. for a long time the horse run in a bunch. then

“Race. of course it be. _the_ race. don’t you see it?”

thus, however, end Quixtus’s career on the Turf. to stand about

“in one way, yes,” reply Quixtus, musingly; “but I loathe touch the

in spite of his confessed belief in the altruistic purity of the turf,

he would be as diabolically and defiledly wicked as you please, but the

intermediary pitch he would not touch.



that be his attitude towards all the suggestion for wickedness lay

heart break.



Vandermeer, unemployed since his embassy to Tommy Burgrave, unsuccessful

in his suggestion and envious of Billiter and Huckaby, at last hit upon

an ingenious idea. he bring Quixtus a dirty letter. it run:



“dear Mr. Vandermeer,—You, who be an old friend of my husband’s in

our well day and know how valiantly I have struggle to keep the home

together, cann’t you help I now? I be ill in bed, my child be

starve. the little one be lie now even too weak to cry out for

bread. it would break a wolf’s heart to see they. if you cann’t help I,

for I know how thing be with you, cann’t you bring my case before your

rich friend, Mr. Quixtus, of whose kindness and generosity you have so

often speak? . . .



                                                 “your sincerely,

                                                     “Emily Wellgood.”



it bear the address “2, Transiter Street, Clerkenwell Road, N.W.”



“what do you bring I this for?” ask Quixtus as soon as he have read

it.



“I be satisfy my own conscience as far as Mrs. Wellgood be

Quixtus lean back in his chair and give the matter his consideration.

prospect of a whole family perishing of hunger by an act; as it be, of

his will, please his fancy.



“very good. very good, Vandermeer. let they starve,” say he. “let they

starve,” he murmur to himself, as he take up his pen.



Vandermeer, hang about, hint at payment for the service render.

Quixtus meet his crafty eye with equal cunning.



“you would be too soft-hearted—you would give they some of the money.

wait till some of they be dead.” he roll the last word delectably

round his tongue. “and now, my dear Vandermeer, I’m very busy. many

thank and good-bye.”



Vandermeer leave reluctantly and Quixtus resume his work.



“the bizygomatic transverse diameter,” he write, put down the

beginning of the sentence that be in his head when Vandermeer be

announce. he pause. he have lose the thread of his idea. it be a

subtle argument depend on the comparative measurement of newly

gentlemanly language anathematise Vandermeer. he attack the

bizygomatic transverse diameter again; but the starve family occupy

his thought. presently he abandon work for the morning and give

“I will _not_ be a fool,” he assert, as the taxi-cab enter the

Clerkenwell Road.



the taxi-cab driver sign that he wish to communicate with his fare.

Quixtus lean forward over the door.



“do you know where Transiter Street be, Sir?”



Quixtus do not. do any easy London gentleman know the mean street in

Quixtus drive home in an angry mood. once more he have be deceive.

“my dear Vandermeer,—I be send Mrs. Wellgood the burial expense. I

you.



                                                 “your sincerely,

                                                    “Ephraim Quixtus.”



he post the letter himself on his way to lunch at the club where

Wonnacott remark on his high good humour.



since the discontinuance of the Tuesday dinner (for they be not

an ugly look overspread Vandermeer’s foxy face.

but Billiter have turn his broad back on he and do not catch the

                              CHAPTER XIII





not long after this Quixtus announce to Huckaby his intention of go

to Paris to attend a small Congress of the Anthropological Societies of

the North-west of France, to which he, as president of the

Anthropological Society of London, have be invite. he have gradually,

in spite of his preoccupation, resume his interest in his favourite

pursuit, and, though he know his learn friend to be villain at

human society have begin to attract he again. it afford he saturnine

wear the mask himself. when he smile in his grave and benevolent

occupy all his time and attention.



“quite so,” say Huckaby. “while you’re busy with prehistoric man, I’ll

the flesh.”

“to the spirit then—the Evil Spirit,” say Huckaby, unabashed. “that

be, if we discover a subject. we’re bind to try various experiment

before we finally succeed.”

Quixtus, musingly.



here be something happen which Huckaby dread. Quixtus be

Huckaby glance at he swiftly. beyond the iniquity of Marrable, he be

“she betray you?”

“a man’s foe,” he quote in a murmur, “be ever of his own household.”



Quixtus nod again three or four time, with part lip.

in spite of his suspicion, Huckaby be astounded at the inadvertent

“Hammersley,” he say, aloud.

“no time like the present.”



“you’re right,” say Quixtus. “we’ll go to Paris together.”

for the first few day in Paris Quixtus have little time to devote to the

“Eustace Huckaby, can that possibly be you—or be it your ghost?”

“it’s year and year since we meet. how many?”

“I too. and in its vastness I feel the most lonesome widow woman that

a waiter bring the tray with tea which she have order before the

“with pleasure,” say Huckaby.

“just a day or two long—till the end of my Congress.”

“_Voilà_,” say Mrs. Fontaine. “the warm June night, the Bois in the

she embroider her thesis very gracefully, clothe the woman of the

“I should have think that bath of innocence be in itself the fountain

of Youth,” say Huckaby.



the least suspicion of a frown pass over Mrs. Fontaine’s candid brow.

but she reply with a smile:



“on the contrary, my friend. that be a penitential dipping in the water

of the past.”



“why penitential?” ask Quixtus.



“ben’t it wholesome discipline to give oneself pain sometimes?” her face

grow wistful. “to re-visit scene where one have be happy—and sharpen

the knife of memory?”



“it be the instinct of the ascetic,” smile Quixtus.



“I suppose I have a bit of it,” she reply, demurely. then her face

brighten. “I don’t wear a hair shirt—I’ve get to appear in an evening

gown sometimes—but I find an odd little satisfaction in do penance.

if I be a roman Catholic I would embarrass my confessor.”



Huckaby’s lip twitch in a smile beneath his moustache. if all the

tale that Billiter tell of Lena Fontaine be true, a confessor would

be exceedingly embarrassed. he regard she with admiration. she be an

entirely different woman from the hard and contemptuous partner in

iniquity to whom Billiter have introduce he before he leave London. it

nothing more. Huckaby, descend her stair with Billiter, have feel as

now have the satisfaction of see Quixtus catch immediately in the

maintain her relation with so undesirable a person as Billiter, for

whom he himself entertain a profound contempt. billiter be unusually

in the Dragoon Guardsman’s set before run through his money, and

that he have accidentally do she a service in later year. what that

the more likely influence keep together a well-receive woman of

remember that Billiter have confess to a mysterious source of income.

what more natural an explanation thereof than the fact that, having once

surprised a woman’s secret and hold her reputation in his hand, he

should have be accept by she, in desperation, as her pay doer of

unavowable office? he know that a woman of Lena Fontaine’s type, with

an assured social position in the great world, do not descend into the

half-world without a desperate struggle. her back be against the wall,

and she use any weapon to hand. hence her use of Billiter. at all

event, in the present case there have be no pretence of friendship. to

be anxious to conclude as soon as possible. one condition she

rigorously exact; that her acquaintance with Billiter should not be

reveal to Quixtus. she be not proud of Billiter. Huckaby take what

comfort he could from the thought.



Mrs. Fontaine sit talk to the two man until the tea-drinking and

chattering crowd have melt away. then she rise, thank they prettily

for waste their science-fill time on an irresponsible woman’s

loneliness, and express to Huckaby the hope that she would see he

again before he leave Paris.



“I trust I, too, may have the pleasure,” say Quixtus.



“you might lead we to the Fountain of Youth one of these evening,” say

Huckaby.



“it would be delightful,” say the lady, with a questioning glance at

Quixtus.



“I could dream of nothing more pleasant,” he reply, bow in his

old-fashioned way.



when she have go, the man resume their seat. Quixtus light a cigarette.



“a very charming woman.”



Huckaby agree. “it have be one of my great regret of the past few

year that I have not be able to keep up our old friendship. we move

in different world.” he pause, as if think sorrowfully of his

misspent life. “I hope you don’t mind my suggest the little

dinner-party,” he say, after a while. “my position be a delicate one.”



“it be a very good idea,” say Quixtus.



Huckaby say little more, prefer to leave well alone. the plot, up

to this point, have succeed. Quixtus give complete credence to the
