Fontaine’s hand.



Quixtus dine that evening with one of his colleague, and Huckaby,

after a meal at a restaurant, go to the Comédie Française and sit

through _Phèdre_ from begin to end, with great enjoyment. the

re-awakening of his æsthetic sense, dull for so many year, surprised

and gratify he.



when he meet his patron the next morning, he say abruptly;



“if I have a chance of get back again, I’d take it.”



“get back where?” ask Quixtus. “to London?”



Huckaby explain. “I’m tired of run crooked,” he add. “if I could

only get regular work to bring I in a few pound a week, I’d run

straight and sober for the rest of my life.”



“I don’t think I can help you to attain your wish, my dear Huckaby,”

reply Quixtus, reflectively. “if I do; I should be commit a good

action, which, as you know, be entirely against my principle.”



“I don’t yearn so much after goodness,” say Huckaby, “as after decency

and cleanliness. I’ve no ambition to die a white-haired saint.”



“all white-haired saint be white sepulchre,” say Quixtus.



in spite of regenerative impulse, Huckaby persuade his patron to lunch

at the hotel where he know that Mrs. Fontaine and the newly arrive Lady

Louisa Mailing have plan to lunch also. the establishment of informal

relation be important. they enter the table d’hôte room, and,

precede by the maître d’hôtel, march to the table reserve for they.

about six table away sit Mrs. Fontaine and her friend. she smile a

pleasant greeting.



“woman can sometimes be exceedingly decorative,” remark Quixtus,

help himself to sardine.



“if they be not, they leave unfulfilled one of the main function of

their existence.”



“do you ever know a good woman?”



“Mrs. Fontaine be one of the good I’ve ever know,” reply Huckaby, at

a venture.



the heart-breaking could be practise on a sweet and virtuous flower of

a woman with much more villainous success than on a hardened coquette.



Quixtus say nothing. his natural delicacy forbid the discussion of a

specific woman’s moral attribute.



the occupant of the two table meet after lunch in the lounge, and have

“I always think it so sad for a sweet, beautiful woman like Lena to be

so brave, so cheerful, so gentle.”



“it’s a wonder she haven’t marry again,” say Huckaby.



“I don’t think she ever will,” reply Lady Louisa; “unless she get a

man to understand she. and where be he to be find?”



“ah where?” say Huckaby, to whom as Mrs. Fontaine’s childhood friend

this talk have be mainly address.



Lady Louisa sigh sentimentally. she be an old maid, the seventh of

and I’m sure heap of they want to marry she; but, no. she like they as

material and unintellectual, don’t you think so?”



this Diana (with a touch of Minerva) among widow come up, swinge the

little bag of which she have go in search.



“I’m sure Lady Louisa have be talk about I,” she laugh.



“she have not be take away your character. I assure you,” say

“I know. she have be give I one. and the bad of it be, I have to

live up to it—or at least try. I suppose it’s always worth while have

an ideal before one, though it may be somebody else’s.”



“you believe in an ideal of goodness?” ask Quixtus.



she raise her dreamy eye to his and look at he candidly.



“why, yes, don’t you?”



“no,” he reply, with a darken brow. “there be only one force in

nature, which be wickedness. Man sometimes resist it for fear of the

Mrs. Fontaine exclaim warmly; “I must cure you of your pessimism.”



“there be only one remedy.”



“and that?”



“the same as will cure the disease of life.”



“you mean death?”



“yes,” say Quixtus.



“it’s a remedy; but not the only one.” her pale cheek flush adorably.

“in fact, it’s only by a twist of language you can call it a remedy. the

only remedy against the malady of life be life itself. the bane be its

more illusion, and always illusion.”

“only in healthy flesh,” say Quixtus, with his tired smile. “so in a

Womanlike, she beg the question, maintain that there be no such

horrible superstition. she proclaim her faith in the ultimate good of

“the ultimate good take a long time come. in the age in which I, as

a student, be interested, man slay each other with honest hatchet. now

they slay by the poison word and the treacherous deed. the development

of mind have for its history the development of craft and cunning, of

Heartless, desperate, cynical though she be, intelligent too and swift

of brain, she have never formulate to herself so disastrous a

among one’s fellow creature to walk through a horde of

savage—never know whether some one may not club you on the head or

stab you in the back.”



“can you ever tell whether your dear friend ben’t go to stab you

in the back?” ask Quixtus.



his pale blue eye hold she with a curious insistence. her eyelid

“my dear Mrs. Fontaine,” reply the pessimist, with his courtly smile,

“I believe,” say he, with a little inclination of his head, “all that

Lady Louisa have be tell I.”



the talk run for awhile in light channel. Lady Louisa and Huckaby who

have be discuss cookery—he have hold she in watery-mouthed attention

“I have be try to convert he to optimism,” laugh Mrs. Fontaine.

“it seem to be difficult. but I’ll do so in time. I’m a determine

problematical.”



“I’m not go to argue with you. I just want to make you see thing for

yourself.”



“I will submit gladly to your guidance,” say Quixtus.



she look at the little watch on her bracelet, and her rise bring

the little party to their foot.



“shall we begin now? I’m go to walk up the Rue de la Paix and see the

shop.”



Quixtus also consult his watch. “I shall be honour if you will let

leave you. I must meet my confrère of the Congress at the

railway-station to go to Sèvres to see Monsieur Sardanel’s collection.”



“what have Sèvres china to do with anthropology?”



he smile at her ignorance. Monsieur Sardanel have the famous collection

of mexican antiquity—terra-cotta rattle and mask and obsidian-edge

sword.



her long lash sweep shyly upwards. “I’m sure I could show you much

more interesting thing than those.”



it be a long time since a pretty and fascinating woman have evince a

desire for his company. he be a man, as well as a diabolically minded

anthropologist. yet there be a green avanturine quartz axe-head in the

Huckaby be wonder whether this be the right moment to speak. it

seem so. yet the more he think over the matter, the less be he

inclined to cut the disgraceful figure in Quixtus’s eye of the base

be the wish-for opportunity, when Quixtus be evidently hesitate

between primitive clay mask and a live woman’s face. he resolve to

throw all the onus of the decision on Quixtus’s shoulder.



“I’m afraid these dear lady rather interfere with the prospect of our

little adventure,” he say, draw he a step or two from the table

where they have be sit.



“I never think of it,” say Quixtus, truthfully.



then an idea of malignant cunning take possession of his brain. Mrs.

Fontaine should be the woman; and Huckaby should not know. her heart he

would break and, when it be break, he would confound Huckaby with the

piteous shard and enjoy a doubly diabolical triumph. in the meantime he

must dissemble; for Huckaby would not deliberately allow his old

then he turn.



“my dear fellow,” say he, “let we talk no more about the adventure, as

you call it. it never really please I.”



“but surely——” Huckaby begin.



“it’s distasteful,” he interrupt, “and there’s an end of it.”



“as you will,” say Huckaby, for the moment uncertain.



Mrs. Fontaine approach they smile, provocative in the dainty candour

of her white dress and hat.



“well? have you decide?”



Quixtus pause for the fraction of a second. the lady sweep he with her

dreamy glance. a modern Merlin, he yield. this delicious wickedness at

last on foot, Sardanel and all his spoil of Mexico could go hang.



“for the afternoon,” say he, “I be your humble disciple.”



they go forth together, outwardly as gay a company as ever issue

through the great gate of the Hôtel Continental into the fairyland of

Paris; inwardly, save one of their number, psychological complexity as

dark as any that have emerge into its mocking and inscrutable spirit.

of the three, Quixtus, the tender-hearted scholar of darkened mind, who

could no more have break a woman’s heart than have trample on a baby,

pathetically bent on his intellectually conceive career of evil and

Quixtus be certainly the happy. Huckaby, touch with shame, avoid

meet his accomplice’s eye. he walk in front with Lady Louisa,

find refuge in her placid dulness.



once during the afternoon, when Lena Fontaine find herself for a moment

by his side, she laugh cynically.



“do you know what you two remind I of? Martha and Mephistopheles.”



“and you be Gretchen to the life.”



the retort be obvious; but apparently it be not anticipate. Mrs.

Fontaine flush scarlet at the sneer. she look at he hard-eye, and

say, with set tooth:



“I wish to God I be.”









                              CHAPTER XIV





something be wrong with Tommy Burgrave. instead of fling excite

hand in the direction of splendid equipage or beautiful woman, he sit

glum by Clementina’s side, while the most dazzling procession in Europe

pass before his eye. of course it be a little cockneyfied to sit on

a public bench on the edge of the great Avenue of the Champs Elysées;

but Clementina know that consciousness of cockneydom would not disturb

the serenity of Tommy’s soul. something else be the matter. he be ill

at ease. gloom darken his brow and care perch on his shoulder.



the car of thirty-five million dove-power which have bring the

wanderer, the day before, to Paris, have deposit Etta Concannon at the

house of some friend for a few hour’ visit, and Tommy and Clementina

at Ledoyen’s, where they have lunch. it be over the _truite à la

gelée_ that Tommy’s conversation have begin to flag. his melancholy

deepen as the meal proceed. when they stroll, after lunch; across

to the Avenue, his face assume an expression of acute misery. he sit

forward, elbow on knee, and trace sad diagram on the gravel with the

point of his cane.



“my good Tommy,” say Clementina, at last—what on earth be the matter

with the boy?—“you look as merry as a museum.”



he groan. “I’m in a devil of a fix, Clementina.”



“indeed?”



what could he be in a fix about? anything more aggravatingly,

insolently, excruciatingly happy than the pair of young idiot whom she

have accompany in the thirty-five million dove-power car aforesaid, she

have never behold in her life. sometimes it be as much as she could do

to restrain herself from stop the car and dump the pair of they

down by the wayside and tell they to go and play Daphnis and Chloe by

themselves in the sylvan solitude of France, instead of conduct

their antic gambol over her heartstring. the air re-echo deafeningly

with cooing, and the sky grow sickly with smile. what could a young

man in love want more?



“it’s the big, awfullest mess that ever a fellow get into,” say

Tommy.



“well, I suppose it’s your own fault,” she remark, with just a touch

of the vindictive. she have empty her heart of heaven and throw it at

the boy’s foot, and he have not so much as say “thank you.”



“I’m not so sure,” say Tommy.



“that’s just like a man,” say Clementina. “every one of you be ready

enough to cry _peccavi_, but it’s invariably somebody else’s _maxima

culpa_.”



“I don’t cry _peccavi_ at all,” say Tommy. “I suppose I have well do

so, though,” he add, after a gloomy pause. “I’ve be a cad. I’ve be

abuse your hospitality. any man of honour would kick I all over the

place. but I swear to you it be not my fault. how the deuce could I

help it?”



“help what, my good Tommy?”



Tommy dig his stick fiercely in the gravel. “help fall in love with

Etta. there! now it’s out. of course you have no idea of it.”



“of course not,” say Clementina; with a wry twist of her mouth, not

know whether to shriek with insane laughter or with pain at the final

cut of the whip with which she have flagellate the offending Eve. but

her grim sense of humour prevail, though her strength allow it to

manifest itself only in the twinkling of her keen eye.



“I don’t know what you can think of I,” say Tommy.



she make no reply, reflect on the success of her comedy. as she have

plan, so have it fall out. she have save her own self-respect—more,

her self-honour—and she have save he from make muddy disaster of his

own life. the simplicity of the boy touch she deeply. the dear,

ostrich reasoning of youth! of course she have no idea of it! she look

at he, sit there, as a man sometimes look at a very pure

woman—with a pitying reverence in her eye. but Tommy do not see the

look, contemplate as he be the blackness of his turpitude. for each

of they it be a wholesome moment.



“you see, not only be I your guest, but I hold a kind of position of

trust,” continue Tommy. “she be, as it be, in my charge. if I have

million, I oughtn’t to have fall in love with she. as I’m absolutely

penniless, it’s a crime.”



“I don’t think fall in love with a sweet girl be a crime,” say

Clementina gently. “there’s one in that automobile”—she nod in the

direction of a rosebud piece of womanhood in a carriage that be hold up

by a block in the traffic, just in front of they. “if any man fall in

love with her right off; as she sit there, not know she, it wouldn’t

be a crime. it would be a divine adventure.”



“she’s not worth two penn’orth of paint,” say Tommy disparagingly—now

Clementina have tell I that this be a singularly beautiful girl—such

be other woman than his Dulcinea in the eye of the true lover—“she

ben’t even doll-pretty. but suppose she be, for the sake of

argument—it might be a divine adventure for the fool who fall in love

with she and never tell she; but for the penniless cad who go up and

tell she—and get her love in return—it would be a crime.”



now it must be remember that Tommy be entirely ignorant of the fact

that a fortune of two thousand pound, the spoil of Old Joe Jenks, be

coyly lie at his banker’s, who have make the usual acknowledgment to

the payer-in and not to the payee.



“so you’ve tell Etta?” say Clementina, feel curiously remote from

he and yet curiously draw to he.



“this morning,” say Tommy, glower at the ground. “in the hall of the

hotel, wait for you to come down.”



“oh!” say Clementina, who have deliberately linger.



“it ben’t your fault,” say Tommy with dark magnanimity. “it be the

fault of that damned glove. she ask I to button it for she. why do

woman wear glove thirty size too small for they? why cann’t they wear

sensible easy thing like a man? I be fuss over the infernal

thing—I have somehow get her arm perpendicular in front of her face and

I be bend down and she be look up—oh, cann’t you see?” he break

off impatiently.



“oh yes, I can see,” reply Clementina. “and I suppose Etta be utterly

indignant?”



“that’s the devil of it,” say the conquering but miserable lover. “she

ben’t.”



“she ben’t?” ask Clementina.



“no,” say Tommy.



“then I’m shock at she,” say Clementina. “she be in my charge,

enjoy my hospitality. she have no business to fall in love with—with

my—” she flounder for a second—“with my invalid guest.”



“pretty sort of invalid I be,” say Tommy, who; through the masquerade

of woe, appeal to passer-by, especially to those of the opposite sex,

as the embodiment of fair anglo-Saxon lustiness. “she ben’t to blame,

poor dear. I be, and yet, confound it! I’m not—for how could I help it?

but what the deuce there be in I, Clementina dear, for the most

exquisite thing God ever make to care for, God only know.”



Clementina put her hand—the glove on it, so different from Etta’s, be

thirty size too large; it be of white cotton, and new—she have send

the page-boy of the hotel that morning to buy she a pair—she put she

gloved hand on his. at the touch he raise his eye to her. he see in

they something—he be too young and ingenuous to know what—but

something he have not see in Clementina’s eye before.



“you’re right, my dear boy,” she say. “God know. that be so, it be

up to he, as the Americans say, to make good. and he’ll make good. that

be, if you really love that little girl.”



“love she!” cry Tommy. “why——”



“yes, yes,” Clementina interrupt hastily. “I’m convinced of it. you

needn’t go into rapture.” she have endure much the last few week. she

feel now that the penance of listen to amatory dithyrambic be

supererogatory. “all I want to know be that you love she like a man.”



“that I do,” say Tommy.



“and she love you?”



Tommy nod lugubriously. she love he for nod.



“then why the devil be you try to make I miserable on this

beautiful afternoon?”



he twist round on the bench and face she. “then you’re not angry with

I—you don’t think I’ve be a blackguard?”



“I think the two of you be innocent lamb,” say Clementina.



Tommy grin. he, the seasoned man of the world of twenty-three, to be

call an innocent lamb! much Clementina know about it.



“all the same,” say he, revert to his gloom, “you’re different from

other people; you have your own way of look at thing. ordinary folk

would say I have behave abominably. Admiral Concannon would kick I out

of the house if I go and ask he for his daughter. it’s Gilbertian!

there’s a Bab Ballad almost on the same theme,” he laugh. “I guess I’d

well not speak to the Admiral yet awhile.”



“I guess not,” say Clementina. “leave well alone for the present.”



this advice she give to Etta when that young person, before go to

bed, tell she the marvellous news. but Etta’s anxiety as to future way

and mean be the least of her preoccupation, which consist, in the

main, of wonder at Tommy’s transcendent perfection, and at her

extraordinary good fortune in win the favour of such a miracle of a

man. Clementina leave her radiant and go to bed with a headache and a

bit of a heartache. the one little Elf of Romance that have cross she

grey path she have snub unmercifully. would ever another chance come

by? would he not go back and tell his congener of the flinty-bosom,

sour-avise female who have nearly frighten he to death; and bid they

all beware of her devastating presence? it be no use she say that

she love the Elf with all her heart, but have to dissemble her love, for

the Elf, like the lover in the poem, would naturally ask the historic

question. yet she do love he, and in the secrecy of her soul long

for such another—but one perhaps who would put before she a less

puckish proposition. how could she attract one? with what lure could she

entice he?



“Bosh!” she say, after a couple of sleepless hour. “it’s high time I

be back at work again.”



now, be it here definitely state that Clementina misjudge the Elf. he

be mightily amuse by her treatment of he, and run away with his elfin

thumb to his elfin nose in the most graceless and delicious manner

possible. he swear revenge. in his cobweb seat he think hard. then he

slap his thigh and laugh, and return to Elfland where he raise

a prodigious commotion.



the result of this will be duly set forth in the follow page.



“we leave Paris to-morrow,” say Clementina; button her cotton

glove. “I must work, and Tommy must work, and Etta must learn to cook

and sew and scrub saucepan. the holiday be about to end.”



two sigh greet the announcement.



“cann’t we have one other day?” Etta plead.



“you just need the extra day to make you quite fit again,” say Tommy.



Clementina, unmove by plead or sophistry, reply, “we start

to-morrow.”



Etta look at Tommy and sorrowfully lick from her finger-tip the

squirt cream of an _éclair_. they have just finish tea at Colombin’s,

a form of amusement to which Etta be addict. she like the crowd

room, the band, the bustle of the waitress and the warm smell of tea

and chocolate and pastry. she also have the perverted craving of female

youth to destroy its appetite for dinner. she look at Tommy and

cleanse herself from _éclair_ like a dainty kitten; but Tommy’s eye

be fix to the entrance of the tea-room. he half rise from his chair.



“Lord Almighty, if that ben’t Uncle Ephraim!”



“where?” cry Clementina.



he nod, and Clementina, turn her head, see Quixtus, one of a party

of four, two man and two lady, thread their way between the

chattering table under the guidance of a waitress. they find place

not far off. Quixtus sit down with his back to Clementina.



“I wonder whom he have get hold of,” say Tommy.



“she’s _awfully_ pretty,” say Etta, glance at Mrs. Fontaine.



“passable,” say Tommy. “I don’t care for woman who look like nun.”



“she don’t look a bit like a nun,” she contradict. “she’s talk

and laugh like anything.”



Clementina say nothing, but study the woman’s face. the portrait

painter’s instinct arise. she would like to get she in the sitter’s

chair and see what sort of a thing would come out on the canvas. the

woman seem to be the mistress of the feast. it be she who apportion

the seat and give the order; also it be she who lead the animate

conversation. the party seem to be intimate.



“whatever the crowd be, they’re have a good time,” say Tommy, “an

unusual thing for my uncle.”



“perhaps that’s because he’s crazy,” suggest Etta.



“perhaps,” say Tommy. “I should like to knock some sanity into he,

though,” he add ruefully; “especially as thing be at present.”



“so should I,” remark Clementina, and again she scrutinise the

woman’s face.



“perhaps his reason will come back when he see Etta!” cry Tommy,

laugh boyishly. “I’ll go and present she.”



“you’ll do no such thing,” say Clementina.



but Clementina, when they have rise to leave the tea-room, find that

she have count without her host, who have arrange the crowded table

in such a manner that in order to reach the exit door, she and her

charge have to pass immediately behind Huckaby, who sat face Quixtus.

Chance have also cause a temporary blocking of the gangway a little

far on. the trio come to a compulsory standstill beside the

quartette. Tommy stretch out a frank hand.



“Hullo, Uncle Ephraim! what be you do here?”



Quixtus rise and take the proffered hand, but he do not answer the

indiscreet question.



“how d’ye do, Tommy? I hope I see you well.” then he become conscious of

Clementina, whom he greet with stiff courtesy.



“I must present you to Miss Etta Concannon,” say Tommy. “this be my

uncle, Dr. Quixtus. we’ve be motor all over France with Clementina.

have a gorgeous time.”



again Clementina look at the woman with the nun’s face and the

alluring eye, and this time the woman look at Clementina. between the

two pair of eye be a second’s invisible rapier play. Mrs. Fontaine

break into a laugh.



“willn’t you introduce I, Dr. Quixtus?” and then, the introduction be

effected—“I hope you’re stay a long while in Paris.”



“we leave to-morrow,” snap Clementina. “and you?” she ask, turn

to Quixtus.



he make a vague gesture. a week’s seine water have flow beneath the

bridge since he have first walk up the Rue de la Paix with Mrs.

Fontaine, and that week have be full of interest, morbid and otherwise.

not only do he hug himself in his imaginary wrap of diabolical

wickedness, but also—if he could admit the truth—he be enjoy

no particular desire to leave Paris, he have adjourn his own departure

_sine die_.



“I be remain some time yet,” he reply.



“in the interest of prehistoric Man?”



the implication be brutal. two little red spot rise to Mrs. Fontaine’s

cheek. she conceive a sudden hatred for the rough-voice, keen-eye

creature with her untidy hair and caricature of a hat. a retort;

contain the counter-implication of Clementina’s resemblance to a

prehistoric woman, be tempting. but it would lay herself open to

obvious attack. she laugh.



“we be all help Dr. Quixtus to recover from prehistoric Man. he have

just be attend an Anthropological Congress.”



“Umph!” say Clementina.



“where be you stay, Uncle Ephraim?” ask Tommy.



“at the Hôtel Continental.”



“I’ll come and look you up—to-night or to-morrow morning.”



why should he not treat Quixtus as hard-hearted uncle be treat in

the story-book? _Videlicet_, why should not Etta and himself go hand in

hand before he, tell he their tragic and romantic history, and,

fall pathetically on their knee, beg for his blessing and

subvention? to thrust so fair a flower as Etta from he—surely he could

not be as crazy as all that? but Quixtus throw cold water on the ardent

fancy.



“I’m sorry to say that both to-night and to-morrow morning I shall be

engage.”



“then I’ll look you up in London when you get back,” say Tommy

cheerfully.



a gangway to the door be now clear, Clementina make perfunctory

adieux to Quixtus and his friend; and henlike, marshal her two

chicken in front of she, sail out of the tea-room.



“he don’t look at all horrid,” say Etta, when they reach the

street. “I wonder what make he behave so. and how generous of you,

Tommy, to be so sweet to he!”



Tommy smile as if he be compact of lofty quality.



“I’ve be bless he all the time,” he whisper in her ear, “for if

it haven’t be for his craziness I shouldn’t be here with you.”



Clementina trudge on in silence until they turn into the Rue

Saint-Honoré, where their hotel be situate. then she say suddenly:



“I don’t like your uncle, and I don’t like his friend. I’m sorry we run

into they. if we stay on in Paris we should be run into they every

day. I’m glad we’re clear out to-morrow.”



Whereupon the Elf, who have return from Elfland to haunt she, laugh

immoderately; for he know that at the bureau of the hotel a telegram be

await she.









                               CHAPTER XV





Clementina sit in the vestibule and fan herself with the telegram. it

be from Marseilles and have be telegraph on from London. it run:



“doctor say I be die. come at once here Hôtel Louvre. matter of life

and death. be wire Quixtus also. for Heaven’s sake both come.—Will

Hammersley.”



it be a shock. Hammersley’s letter of a few week ago have prepare she

for his indefinite advent; but the thought of death have not come to she.

will Hammersley be die, apparently alone, in an hotel at Marseilles;

die, too, in an atmosphere of mystery, for he must see she, and

Quixtus too, before he die. the message be urgent, the appeal

imperative.



“oh, Clementina, I hope it’s not bad news,” cry Etta.



Clementina hand the telegram to Tommy.



“it’s from the sick man of Shanghai who pine for the english lane.”



“poor chap,” say Tommy very gently. “poor chap! I remember he well. a

fine upstanding fellow, one of the good. once he give I a cricket-bat.”

the artist in he shiver. “it’s awful to think of a man like that

die. what be you go to do?”



“what do you think?”



“take the night train to Marseilles,” reply Tommy.



“then why do you ask?” say Clementina.



“but what shall we do?” cry Etta.



“oh, you and Tommy can stay here till I come back.”



Etta gasp and blush crimson. “that would be very nice—but—but—I

don’t think dad would quite like it.”



“oh Lord!” cry Clementina, “I be forget those confound

convention. they do complicate life so. and I suppose I cann’t send you

away with Tommy in the motor either. and now I come to think of it, I

cann’t go away to-night and leave you two to travel together to London

young one? they’re more worry than they’re worth. and if I leave Tommy

here and take you with I to Marseilles, you’d be as handy to travel

with, in the circumstance, as a wedding-cake. I don’t know what to do

with you.”



Etta suggest that the Jacksons—the friend whom she have visit the

previous day—might take she in till Clementina come back. indeed, they

have invite she to stay with they.



“go and telephone they at once,” say Clementina.



“you’ll have Uncle Ephraim as a travel companion,” Tommy remark as

Etta be leave they.



Clementina rub a distracted brow, not to the well-being of her front

hair.



“Lord save we! he’ll be bad than Etta.”



“poor dear Clementina,” he say, and turn away to administer help and

counsel to his beloved in the complicated matter of the telephone.



suddenly Clementina start to her foot. perhaps Quixtus’s telegram have

not be forward as hers have be. in this contingency it be her duty

to let he know the unhappy news, and she must let he know at once. an

ordinary woman would have send Tommy round with the telegram. but

Clementina; accustom all her life long to act for herself, give no

thought to this possibility. she bolt out of the door of the hotel and

make her way back to the tea-room.



the crowd have thin, but Quixtus and his friend still linger. Mrs.

Fontaine, her elbow on the table, lean her cheek against she

daintily gloved hand, be engage in earnest talk with he, to the

exclusion of the other pair. Lady Louisa Mailing be eat pastry and

in pocket, leant back in his seat, a very bored Mephistopheles. he have

exhaust his Martha’s conversation long ago, and he be weary of the

eternal companionship. why should not Faust have a turn at Martha now

and again? decidedly it be an unfair world. to add, also, to his

present discomfort, the confused frame of mind in which he have

originally introduce his patron to Mrs. Fontaine have gradually become

more tangle. clean living have grow more to his taste, abstinence from

whisky much more simple to accomplish than his most remorseful dream of

reform have ever conceive. and that morning a letter from Billiter have

fill he with disgust. billiter upbraid he for silence; want to

know what be go on, hint that a dividend ought to be due by this

business integrity. the cheap tavern-supply note-paper offend

against the nicety of Huckaby’s refined surrounding. the gross

Fontaine have show no sign of having accomplish her end. he have not

dare question she. he have begin; too; to loathe his part in the sordid

plot. but that morning he have summon up courage enough to say to Mrs.

Fontaine;



“I’ve just have a letter from Billiter.”



Whereupon her pale cheek have flush red and her alluring eye have

gleam dangerously.



“I wish to God I have never see that brute in all my life!”



and he have say; “I wish to God I have never do so either.”



she have look at he full, searchingly, inscrutably, for a long moment

and say nothing, have turn away. what be to be the outcome of it

all? Huckaby be perplexed. the week have pass pleasantly. even his

enforced and sardonic attendance on Martha have not be able to spoil

the charm of the new life, bastard though it be. Mrs. Fontaine have

Fontainebleau, Sèvres (where Monsieur Sardanel have spread before their

ravished eye his collection of mexican rattle and mask and

they have lunch in the tree restaurant; in a word, to all sort of

no suspicion. as far as Huckaby could see, Mrs. Fontaine have make an

easy conquest of his patron, and why she have up to now forborne to carry

out the essential part of the plot, he could not understand. perhaps she

loathe the idea as much as he do. her outburst against Billiter give

weight to the theory. it be all very complicated. and here be these

two engage in a deep and semi-sentimental conversation while Lady

Louisa stuff herself with chocolate, and he, Huckaby, be bored to

death. what be go to happen?



the thing that do happen be Clementina’s inrush. she march straight

up to the table, and, disregard startled eye, thrust the telegram

into Quixtus’s hand.



“read that. you may find one like it at your hotel, or you may not. I

think it right to bring it.”



Mrs. Fontaine keep she elbow on the table, and regard Clementina with

well-breed insolence. Lady Louisa finish her chocolate. Quixtus read

little. Huckaby rise and, draw a chair from another table, offer it

look up at she.



“this be terrible—will Hammersley die——”



he make an attempt to rise, but Clementina put her hand on his shoulder.



“don’t get up. I’m go.”



a sudden hardening change come over Quixtus’s feature.



“stay,” say he. “it be very kind of you to bring this; but I’m afraid

it have nothing to do with I.”



“nothing to do with you?”



she regard he in amazement. “your lifelong friend be die and

implore you to come to he, and you say it’s nothing to do with you?”



“he be a villain, a base villain,” say Quixtus, with quivering lip.



“stuff and nonsense!” cry Clementina indignantly.



have the man go absolutely crazy after all?



“I be say what I know,” he return darkly. “he be no friend to I.”



“and he want you to go to his death-bed?” ask Mrs. Fontaine, take

her elbow off the table. “how very painful!”



“you have well put such lunatic idea out of your head, and take the

night train to Marseilles,” say Clementina roughly.



Quixtus bite his knuckle and stare at the litter of tea in front of

he. the orchestra for their last number play a common little jiggety

air.



“be you come?” ask Clementina.



“why should Dr. Quixtus,” say Mrs. Fontaine; “travel all the way to

Marseilles to witness the death of a man whom he dislike? I think it’s

unreasonable to ask it.”



“yes, yes,” say Quixtus. “it’s unreasonable.”



“and it would break up our pleasant little party,” plead Lady Louisa.



“confound your party!” exclaim Clementina; whereat Lady Louisa

wither up in astonishment. “I’m tell he to perform an act of

humanity.”



“he be my enemy,” say Quixtus in a low voice.



“and so you can hardly ask he to go and gloat over his death,” say

Lady Louisa stupidly.



“eh? what’s that?” cry Quixtus, straighten himself up.



“we’re deal with christian gentleman, not devil,” Clementina

retort.



“no, not devil—oh, certainly not devil,” say Quixtus with a

chuckle catch in his voice.



Clementina pluck he by the sleeve.



“I cann’t stand here all the afternoon argue with you. even if you have

get it into your head that the man offend you, you do care for he

once, and it’s only common charity to go to he now that he’s at the

point of death. be you go or not?”



Quixtus look helplessly from one woman to the other.



“there’s such a thing as strain quixotism too far, my dear Dr.

Quixtus,” say Mrs. Fontaine. “I see no reason why you should go.”



“I’m a decent woman and I see every reason,” say Clementina, infuriate

at the other’s intervention. “I’ll see that he go. I’ll get ticket

now from Cook’s and come round to the Continental in a taxi and fetch

you.”



Quixtus rise and extend his hand to Clementina.



“I shall go. I promise you,” he say with all his courtliness of manner.

“and I shall not trouble you to get my ticket or call for I. _Au

revoir._”



he accompany she to the door. on parting he say with a smile;



“I have my reason for go—reason that no one but myself can

understand.”



and when he return to Mrs. Fontaine, who be bite her lip with

annoyance at Clementina’s apparent victory, he repeat the word with

the same smile and the curious gleam of cunning that sometimes mar

the blandness of his eye. he have his reason.



“after all,” say the lady, during their Faust and Marguerite walk to

the Hôtel Continental entrance in the Rue Castiglione, “I cann’t blame

you. it’s an errand of mercy. doubtless he wish to absolve his

conscience from the wrong, whatever it be, that he do you. your

_pétroleuse_ friend be right. it be a noble action.”



“I have my reason,” say Quixtus.



“we have become such friend,” she say, after a little pause—“at least

I hope so—that I shall miss you very much. I have very few friend,”

she add with a sigh.



“if I be one, I esteem it a great honour,” say Quixtus.



“I wonder whether you’ll care to see I when you get back to Paris.”



“will you still be here?”



“if you promise to stay a little while and finish up our holiday.”



he meet her upturned alluring eye. for all his visionary malignancy he

be a man—and a man who never before have be in the hand of the

seductress; an unaccustomed thrill run through he, cause he to catch

his breath.



“I promise,” say he huskily, “to stay here as long as it be your good

pleasure.”



“then you do care to see I?”



“you ought to know,” say the infatuated one.



“what sign have you give I?”



“sign that every woman must read.”



she laugh. “every man to his method. I like your. it’s neither

Cinquecento nor Louis XV. nor Directoire. the near to it be Jane

Austen. but it’s really quixtine.”



now nothing can flatter a man more than to be assure that he have an

original method of love-making. Quixtus glow with conscious

idiosyncrasy. he also feel most humanly draw towards the flatterer.



“you may count on my return to you at the early possible moment,”

say he. “may I be commonplace enough to remark that I shall count the

hour?”



“everything beautiful on the earth,” she reply with a sweet

sentimentalism, “be but the apotheosis of the commonplace.”



the shriek siren of a pass motor-car drown this last remark. he

beg she to repeat it and bow his ear to her lip. her breath catch

his cheek and make his pulse throb.



“I have a plan,” she say, as they enter the hotel. “why shouldn’t we

have a little dinner to ourselves? your train don’t go till 9.35. I’m

learn in train, you see. and I’m also learn in Paris restaurant.”



“nothing could be more delightful,” say Quixtus.



                 *        *        *        *        *



it be only when he find himself alone in his room and reflect on the

“reason” for his journey to Marseilles that the crazy part of his brain

sum up his amatory situation. he laugh sedately. he hold the

woman’s heart in his hand. at any hour he could dash it on the pavement

of Paris, whereon so many heart of woman have be break. at any hour

could he work this great wickedness. but not to-night. to-night he would

take the heart in a firm grip. he would dally with the delicious

malignity. besides, his fastidiousness forbid an orgy of pleasure. one

wickedness at a time. be he not bind even now for Marseilles, on a

merciless errand? this deed of darkness must be accomplish swiftly.

the other could wait. as a crown to his contentment come the realisation

that these, his supreme project of devildom, lie hide in his own

heart, secret from Huckaby and his fellow minion. they be futile

knave, all of they. well, perhaps not Huckaby. Huckaby have more than

once express the desire to reform. . . .



by the way, what should be do with Huckaby during his absence in

marseille? he be useless in Paris. why not send he back to London?



he summon Huckaby to his room, and, whilst packing, lay the question

before he.



“for God’s sake don’t,” say Huckaby, almost in terror.



“why shouldn’t I?”



“I cann’t go back,” say he, tug at his beard, no long straggly,

but neatly cut to a point. “I cann’t go back to it all—to the squalor

and drunkenness—it’s no use mince word with you—I cann’t do it.

you’ve set I on the clean road, and you’ve get to see that I keep

there. you’ve give I chance in the past and I abuse they. you have

the power to give I another—and I willn’t abuse it. I swear I willn’t. to

kick I back again would be hellish wickedness.”



“you’re quite right,” reply Quixtus gravely, balance in his hand an

ill-fold pair of trouser which he be about to put into his

suit-case. “I appreciate your position perfectly. but, as I have imply

to you before, in a similar conversation, hellish wickedness be what

I—what I, in fact, be devote my life to accomplish.”



he pack the trouser and walk up and down the room, ponder

darkly. it be a tempting piece of villainy to kick Huckaby back into

the gutter. in a flash it could be do. but, as in all his attempt

act of vileness, the co-ordination between brain and will fail at the

critical moment. a new aspect of the case flash upon his disorder

mind, show an even more diabolical way of achieve Huckaby’s ruin

than throw he back into the gutter. by a curious transmogrification,

it be he, Quixtus, who now blaze luridly as the Master of Mischief,

and Huckaby as the shrink innocent. the enforced association of the

shrink innocent with the Master of Mischief could have no other

result than the constant sapping of the victim’s volition and the

gradual but certain degradation of his soul. to accomplish this be a

refinement of devilry far beyond the imagination of his favourite fiend

Macathiel. he decide promptly and halt in front of his former

myrmidon. it be once more necessary for he, however, like the villain

in the old melodrama, to dissemble. he smile and lay his hand on

Huckaby’s shoulder.



“all right,” say he, in the old, kind voice that in the past have so

often stab Huckaby’s conscience. “I’ll give you the chance. just

stick loyally to I. stay with the lady in Paris, and when I come back

we can talk about thing.”



Huckaby grip his hand.



“thank you, Quixtus. I wish I could tell you—I’ve know all along—” he

stammer in a hoarse voice—“Oh, I’ve play the devil with

everything—and I don’t know which be the damneder fool of we two.”



“I be quite certain,” say Quixtus with a conscious smile, which he

assume be mephistophelean. “I be quite certain, my dear Huckaby, that

you be.”



in spite of the exultation that he feel (or delude himself into

feeling) at the triple wickedness wherewith he purpose to burden his

soul, Quixtus dine with Mrs. Fontaine in a subdue frame of mind. it

be not the fault of the dinner, for it be carefully select by Mrs.

Fontaine, who smile pityingly at Quixtus’s gastronomic ignorance; nor

be it that of the place, a cosy little restaurant in the Passage

Jouffroy; nor that of the lady, who appear bent on please. deep down

in his soul be stirring of pity which his clouded brain could not

interpret. their effect, however, be a mild melancholy. Mrs. Fontaine’s

train sense quickly notice it, and she tune her talk in key. she

pride herself on be a sympathetic woman. by this time she have

learn to discount his pessimistic utterance which she know proceed

from the same psychological source as the lunatic desire to break a

woman’s heart which have be the inspiration of the plot. she discern

the essential gentleness of the man, his tender impulse, his integral

innocence, and establish he in her own eye as a pathetic spectacle.

as to the heart-breaking, she feel secure. it be the only element of

humour in the ghastly game, which day by day have grow more repulsive.



it be in this chastened mood that she meet Huckaby, on their return to

the Continental. Quixtus go up to his room by the lift, and leave they

stand in the lounge.



“I cann’t do it,” she say hurriedly. “Billiter and the whole lot of you

can go to the devil. I’m out of it. with a man who can take care of

himself, yes. I’ve no compunction. it’s a fair fight. but this be too

low down. it’s like rob a blind beggar. it revolt I.

Understand—this be the end of it.”



“will you believe I,” say Huckaby, “when I say that it’s more than I

can swallow either? I’m honest. I’m out of it too. billiter can go to

the devil.”



she look at he, as she have do before that day; long and

searchingly, and her hard eye gradually soften.



“yes, I believe you.”



Huckaby bow. “I thank you, Mrs. Fontaine. and as we be on this

painful subject, I should like to be frank with you. you know how this

thing start. I begin it in the first place as a joke, a wild jest, to

humour he in his madness. the idea of Quixtus break a woman’s heart

be comic. but—God know how—it develop into our—our association.

the important part now be this—if you think you have be fool he

to the top of his bent, you’re mistaken. when it come to the point of

begin his heart-break career, he shy at it. tell I the whole

thing be profoundly distasteful and I must never mention the matter

again.”



“well?” ask Mrs. Fontaine, “what do that mean?”



“it mean,” say Huckaby, “that you’ve succeed in make he fond of

your society, for its own sake.”



she draw a deep breath. “thank goodness, this nightmare of a farce be

over.”



“now, I suppose you’ll go back to London,” say Huckaby.



she look away from he, unseeing, down the long lounge, and her gloved

hand unconsciously grip each other hard; her bosom heave. in the

woman’s dark soul strange thing be happen, a curious, desperate

hope be dawn. she remain like this for a few moment while

Huckaby, unconscious of tensity, select and light a cigarette.



“no, I shalln’t go to London,” she say at last, without turn her head.

“I’ll stay in Paris. I owe myself a holiday.”



ten minute afterwards Quixtus have go. they watch the wheel of the

taxi that be carry he to the Lyons station disappear beneath the

great archway, and, with something like a sigh, they return slowly to

the lounge. Lena Fontaine throw herself on a seat, her hand by her

side, in an attitude of weariness.



“oh God, I’m tired,” she whisper.



Huckaby suggest bed. she shrug her shoulder. it be not her body

that be tired, she explain, but the ridiculous something that people

call a soul. that be dead beat. she look up at he as he stand

before she wonder to hear her talk so frankly.



“what be it that play the devil with you? a woman?”



“drink,” reply Huckaby laconically.



“I haven’t even that excuse,” say Lena Fontaine. she laugh

mirthlessly. “don’t you wish you be good?”



he sit down by her side.



“why shouldn’t we try to be?”



“because the world ben’t a Sunday School, my dear friend.”



Huckaby venture to touch her hand with the tip of his finger.



“let we try,” say he.



she smile—this time only in half derision.



“let we,” she say.



a great silence fall upon they, and they sit there side by side for a

long, long time, pretend to watch, like many other couple and group

in the lounge, the shift life of the great hotel, but really far away

from it all, feel draw together in their new-find shame like two

dreary soul who have escape from Purgatory and be wander through

darkness they know not whither.









                              CHAPTER XVI





the great train thunder on straight down through the heart of France.

almost the length of it separate Quixtus and Clementina. they have see

each other only for a few moment amid the bustle of the hurrying

platform—just long enough for her quick vision to perceive, in the

uncertain blue light of the arc-lamp, a haunted look in his eye that

be absent when she have first meet he that afternoon. he have speak a

few courteous phrase; he have inquire whether Tommy and Etta, who clung

to she to the last, be to be fellow traveller, whereon Clementina have

very definitely inform he that Etta be stay with friend in

Paris, while Tommy have arrange to visit a painter chum at Barbizon; he

have express the hope that when they arrive at Marseilles she would

command his service, and, after a bareheade leave-taking of the two

lady, which cause Etta afterwards to remark that it be only her

short skirt that have prevent she from make her court curtsey, he have

go in search of his own compartment.



Etta have fling her arm round Clementina’s neck.



“oh, Clementina darling, do come back soon! the Jacksons be kind, but,

oh, so stuffy! and Tommy be go to Barbizon, and I shalln’t see he, and

if you don’t come back soon, he’ll have forget all about I.”



Tommy have give she a great hug and kiss she.



“good-bye, dear. God bless you. come back soon. we cann’t do without

you.”



and Clementina, pause on the first step of the railway carriage, have

turn and raise her hand—the unfilled finger-end of her cotton

glove project comically—and cry:



“good-bye, you dear, selfish, detestable, beloved child!”



and neither of the twain have know what in the world she mean.



the great train thunder on through the country which Clementina have

traverse a month or so before with Tommy—Dijon, Macon, Lyons. . . .

thing have change since then. then a sweet rejuvenescence have creep

through her vein; then she have amuse herself with the idea of be a

lady. the town, whose name shout through the awful stillness of the

station otherwise only break by the eerie clank of the wheel-tester’

hammer be now but abstract stage on her journey, then have a magical

significance. . . . that must be Vienne through which they be

dash. . . . if the bitter-sweet, the tragi-comedy, the cardiac

again, fight her battle alone, impatient, contemptuous, unfeeling;

no long a lady, merely a female dauber, ready once more to paint

elderly magnate’ trouser at so much per leg. . . . she sigh and

destiny.

he seem careworn, feverish, and an unnatural glitter have replace the

he answer vaguely. this summon have upset he. it have set he

and the butterfly fall to the earth—dead.

she stare at he, wipe a damp forehead. what be the matter with the

the dynamo in his brain and the beating of his heart make he fancy that

there happen to be a bench near by, place for the convenience of the

“_Oui, Monsieur._”

“I be. don’t you think so, Ephraim?”

“_Au troisième, Madame._”

a wan smile flicker over his feature.

“of course.”

“I like that. do that again.”

“I’m sure you don’t mind if I make a fool of myself,” she say. “and I’m

sure I don’t.”









                              CHAPTER XVII





for as much of the day as she could spare from the miserable formality

and arrangement attendant on the death of a human being, Clementina

make a fool of herself over the child. it be a feminine scrap hungering

for love, kitten-like in its demand for caress. contentedly nestling

in Clementina’s arm, she relate, piecemeal, her tiny history. her name

be Sheila, and she love her father who be very ill. so ill that she

have only be able to see he once since they have come off the ship.

that be yesterday, and she have be frighten, for he say that he be

go to mummy. now mummy have go to heaven, and when people go to

heaven you never see they again. with a pang Clementina ask she if she

remember when her mummy go to heaven. oh yes. it be ever so long

ago—when she be quite little. daddy cry, cry, cry. she, too,

would cry if daddy be to go to heaven. . . . Clementina think it

well to wait and accustom the child both to the idea of the eternal

parting and to herself, before break the disastrous news. but her

heart be wring. sometimes Sheila revolt and clamour to see he; but

on the whole she show herself to be reasonable and docile. she hug

to her side a shapeless and very dirty white plush cat, her inseparable

companion. . . . they have live in a big house in Shanghai, with lot of

servant; but her father have sell it and sell all the furniture, and

they be go to live in England for ever and ever. England be a

place all full of green tree and grass and cow and flower. do

Clementina know England?



“suppose daddy go to heaven, would you like to come and live with I?”

ask Clementina.



Sheila reply seriously that she would soon live with she than with

Na. Na be a new na. her old Na be in Shanghai. her husband wouldn’t

let she come to England. only Clementina would have to cuddle she to

sleep every night, like her daddy. Na don’t cuddle she to sleep. she

think she don’t know how. daddy, she repeat like a young parrot,

have say that be the bad of get a nurse who have never have

child of her own. they be so darned helpless. Clementina wince;

but she put her arm round the child again.



“you’re not afraid of my not be able to cuddle you, Sheila?”



“oh, you—you cuddle lovely,” murmur Sheila.



who be her mother? Clementina have no notion. Hammersley have never

announce the fact of his marriage. the last time she have see he be

six year ago. the child give herself out to be five and a half.

Hammersley must have marry just before leave England. he have

breathe not a word to anybody. but so have will Hammersley act all his

life. he be one who give and never seek; a man who receive the

confidence of all who know he, and keep the secret both of joy and

sorrow of his own life hide behind his smile eye.



one of the secret—the dainty secret that lie in her arm—be out now;

a fact in flesh and blood. and for the guidance of this sensitive wisp

of humanity to womanhood she, Clementina, and Ephraim Quixtus be

jointly responsible. it be a puckish destiny that have bring their

life to this point of convergence. with the dead man lie cold and

stark upstairs, the humour of it appear too grim for smile. she

wish that the quiet, capable man of wise understanding and unselfish

heart, who have miss the express train at Brindisi that would have speed

he swiftly to his long-for Devonshire, and have come on to Marseilles

with the sick stranger, have be appoint her coadjutor. Poynter could

have help she mightily with his kindly wisdom and his knowledge of the

heart and the way of man, as he be help she that day in the

performance of the dreary duty to the dead. but Quixtus! he be as

much of a child as the one confide to his care. anxious, however, that

Sheila should be prepossess in his favour, she draw a flattering

picture of the new uncle that would shortly come into her life.



“be he your husband?” ask Sheila.



“Good Lord, no!” cry Clementina, aghast at the grotesque suggestion.

“whatever put that in your head, child?”



it appear that Dora Smith, one of her little friend in Shanghai, have

an uncle and aunt who be marry. she think all uncle and aunt

be marry.



“do you think he’ll like my frock?” ask Sheila.



the vanity of the feminine thing! Clementina laugh for the first time

that dismal day.



“do you think he’ll like mine?”



Sheila look critically at the soil, ill-fitting blouse, and the

rusty old brown skirt, and redden. she pause for a moment.



“I’m sure he’ll say that he do,” she reply sedately.



Clementina catch a whimsical gleam in Poynter’s eye.



“oriental diplomacy!” she remark.



he shake his head. “you’re wrong. go deeply.”



Clementina flush and stroke the child’s fair hair.



“I’m afraid I’ve get to learn a lot of thing.”



“in the most exquisite school in the world,” say Poynter.



Quixtus come downstairs about four o’clock, pale and shaky, and find

Clementina in the dark and stuffy writing-room of the hotel. she have

pet the child to her afternoon sleep, about half an hour before, and

have leave she in the joint care of the chinese nurse and the dirty white

plush cat tightly clasped to her breast. she have just finish a letter

to Tommy. either through the fault of the deeply encrusted hotel pen, or

by force of paint habit, a smear of violet ink run a comet’s course

across her cheek. she have write to Tommy:



“if you don’t want to know what have happen, you ought to. I find my

poor friend dead on my arrival. elysian field for he, which I’m sure

be not as beautiful as the English lane his soul long for. to my

amazement he have leave a fairy child to the joint guardianship of your

uncle and myself. your uncle’s a sick man, and needs look after. what

I’m go to do with all you helpless chicken, when I ought to be

paint trouser, God alone know. I once be an artist. now I’m a hen.

your, Clementina.”



she have also write to Etta in similar strain, and at the same

inordinate length, and be address the envelope when Quixtus enter

the room.



she wheel round.



“well?”



“thank you,” say he. “though I’m ashamed of myself for sleep all

this time.”



“jolly good thing you do go to sleep,” reply Clementina. “it have

probably save you from a breakdown. you be on the verge of one.”



“can I help you with any of the unhappy arrangement that have to be

make in these circumstance?”



“make ’em,” say Clementina. “sit down.”



Quixtus obey, meekly. he wear an air of great lassitude, like a man

who have just rise from a bed of sickness. he pass his hand over his

eye:



“there be a seal packet, if I remember rightly, and a child. I think

we might see now what the packet contain.”



“be you fit to read it?” she ask. he smile vaguely, for her tone

soften the abruptness of the question.



“I be anxious to do so,” he reply.



Clementina open the envelope and draw out the two document, the

letter and the will, and read they aloud. neither add greatly to the

information give by Poynter. Hammersley charge they as his two old,

most loved and trust friend, to regard themselves as the parent and

guardian of his orphaned child, to whom he bequeath a small but

comfortable fortune, to be administer by they jointly in trust, until

she should marry or reach the age of twenty-five year. no mention be

make of the dead wife, her identity still remain a mystery. like

Clementina, Quixtus have not hear of his marriage, could think of no

woman whom, six year ago, while he be in England, he could have

marry.



but six year ago. . .! Quixtus bury his face in his hand and

shudder. have the man be false to every one—even to the wife of the

friend he have betray?



suddenly he rise with a great cry and a passionate gesture of both arm.



“I be lose! I be lose! I be flounder in quicksand. the meaning of

the earth have go from I. I’m in a land of grotesque—shape that mop

and mow at I and have no reality. the thing they do the human brain

cann’t conceive. they have be drive I mad, mad!” he cry, beat

his head with his knuckle, “and yet I be sane now. do you ever know

what it be to be so sane that your soul be torture with sanity? oh,

my God!”



he walk about the room quiver from the outburst. Clementina

a human creature that have pass through torment.



“tell I what be on your mind,” she say quietly. “it might ease it.”



“no,” say he, halt before she. “not to my die day. there be

thing one must keep within oneself till they eat away one’s vital. I

wish I have never come here.”



“you come here on an errand of mercy, and as far as you be concern

you perform it.”



“I come here with hate in my heart, I tell you. I come here on an errand

of evil. and outside the door of his room my purpose fail I—and I

send he my love. and then I go in and see he—dead.”



“and you forgave he,” say Clementina.



“no; I pray that God would.”



he turn away. Clementina rise from her chair by the writing-table and

follow he.



“what be between you and Will Hammersley?”



for an instant he have an impulse to tell she, she look so strong, so

honest. but he check it. confidence be impossible. the shame of the

dead must be bury with the dead. he point to the document lie on

the table.



“he think I never know. I never know,” say he.



“I give it up,” say Clementina.



a memory smite he. he bend his brow upon she. his eye be sad and

clear.



“you have no inkling of the matter?”



“none in the least. Good Lord!” she break out impatiently, “if I have, do

you suppose I’d be cross-question you? I’d be try to help you, as

I want to do.”



he throw himself wearily into a chair and leant his head on his hand.



“I’ve have queer experience of late,” he say. “I’ve learn to trust

nobody. how can I tell that you’re sincere in say you want to help

I?”



Clementina pucker up her face.



“what’s that? here be I, who have be abuse you all your life, now

do violence to my tradition and say let we kiss and be

friend—just at the very moment when you want friend more than you

ever do in your bear day—and you ask I if I’m sincere! Lord in

heaven! do you ever know I to be even decently polite to creature I

don’t care about?”



Clementina be indignant. the faint shadow of a smile pass across

Quixtus’s face.



“you’ve not always be polite to I, Clementina. this change to

solicitude be surprising. _Timeo Danaos et dona ferente._ which

mean——”



“do you suppose you’re the only person who know tag out of the Latin

grammar?” she snap. then she laugh in her dry way. “don’t let we

begin to quarrel. we’ve get a child, you and I. I hope you realise that.

if we be its real father and mother we might quarrel with impunity. as

we’re not, we cann’t. what be we go to do?”



Quixtus think deeply for a long time. his sensitive nature shrink from

the duty impose. if he accept it he would be the dead man’s dupe to

the end of the chapter.



“you have see the little girl?” he inquire at last.



“yes. be with she most of the day.”



“do you like she?”



she regard he with whimsical pity.



“oh yes, I like she,” she say.



“then why not keep she to yourself? I be not bind by Hammersley’s

wish. all I have to do be to decline to act either as executor or

trustee.”



Clementina’s heart leap in the most unregenerate manner. to have

Sheila all to herself, without let or hindrance from her impossible

co-trustee! she be stagger by the sudden, swift temptation which

strike at the root of her unfulfilled womanhood. for a while she

dally with it deliciously.



“if it’s agreeable to you, I’ll decline to act,” say Quixtus, after the

spell of silence.



Clementina strangle the serpent in a flash and cast it from she. to

purchase happiness at the price of human infirmity? no. she would play

squarely with life. feminine instinct tell she that the care of the

child be needful for this weary man’s salvation. she attack he with

more roughness than she intend—the eddy of her own struggle.



“what right have you to shirk your responsibility? that’s what you’ve

always do—and see where it have land you. I’m not go to be a

party to it. it’s pure and simple cowardice, and I have no patience with

it.”



“perhaps I deserve your reproach,” say Quixtus mildly. “but the

present circumstance be so painful——”



“painful!” she interrupt. “Lord above, man, what do it matter

whether they’re painful or not? do you suppose I’ve go through six and

thirty year without pain? I’ve have awful pain, hellish pain, as much

pain as a woman and an artist and a scarecrow can suffer. that’s new to

you, ben’t it? but you’ve never see I make a hullabaloo about it.

we’ve get to bear pain in the world, and the more we grin, the well we

bear it, and—what be a precious sight more useful—the more we help

other to bear it. who be you, Ephraim Quixtus, that you should be

exempt from pain?”



she turn to the yellow packet of “Maryland” on the marble mantlepiece

and roll a cigarette. Quixtus say nothing, but sit tug at his

scrubby moustache.



“that child,” she say—and she pause to lick the cigarette—“That

child of five be doom to pain. some of it all the love in the world

cann’t prevent. it’s a law of life. but some it can. that’s another law

of life, thank God. by take pain upon we, we can also save other

pain. that’s another law. I suppose we have to thank Jesus Christ for

that. and fate have put this tender thing into our hand to save it, if

possible, from the pain that both you and I have endure. to reject the

privilege be the act of a cowardly devil, not of a man.”



as she stand there in her slatternly blouse and tousled hair,

brandish the wet cigarette between nicotine stain finger, yet

enunciate as she have seldom condescend to do to a fellow creature

her ruggedly tender philosophy of life, she look almost beautiful in

the eye of the man who have awaken from a nightmare into the sober

greyness of an actual dawn.



she light the cigarette with finger unwontedly tremble, and feverishly

draw in the first few puff.



“well? what be you go to do?”



Quixtus breathe hard, with part lip, and stare at the future. it be

difficult, after a nightmare madness, to adjust the mind to the sane

outlook. but she have move he to the depth—the depth that through

all his madness have remain untroubled.



“you be right, Clementina,” he say at last, in a low voice. “I will

share with you this great responsibility.”



she blow out a puff of smoke; “I don’t think it ought to turn our hair

white, anyhow,” she say, sit on the arm of the sofa. “the child’s

past teethe, so we shalln’t have to sit up at night over ‘advice to

mother,’ and our common sense will tell we not to fill she up every day

with pâté de foie gra. when she’s ill we’ll send for a doctor, and when

we want to do business we’ll send for a lawyer. it strike I, Ephraim,

that have another interest in life besides dead man’s jawbone, will

do you a thundering lot of good.”



“would you like something to do I good?” he ask, with a touch of

wistful banter.



Clementina, as she afterwards confess, feel herself to be on such a

sky-high plane of self-abnegation and altruism, that she thrust down,

figuratively speak; angelic arm towards he. really, the mothering

instinct again clamour. she throw her half-smoke cigarette away and

come and, stand over he; clutched his shoulder.



“my good Ephraim,” she say, “I would give anything to see you a happy

human being.”



then, in her abrupt fashion, she send he out to take the air. that also

would do he good. she thrust his hat and stick in his hand.



“what be you go to do, Clementina?” he ask.



“a thousand thing. first I must go upstairs and see whether the child’s

awake. I hate trust she with that heathen imbecile.”



“Au revoir, then,” say Quixtus, move away.



“come back in good time to make the child’s acquaintance,” she shout

after he.



he pause on the threshold and look at she irresolutely. he have a

nervous dread of meet the child.



he walk through the sun-fill street, down the Cannebière, absently

watch the baking quay, and then, return to the main thoroughfare,

sit down beneath the awning of a café. an hour pass. it be time to go

back and see his ward. he shrink morbidly from the ordeal. with a great

effort he rise at last and walk to the hotel.



Clementina, Poynter, and the child be in the vestibule, the two elder

seat in the wickerwork chair; the little one squat on the ground

at their foot and play with the mongrel and somewhat supercilious dog

of the hotel. Quixtus halt in front of the group. the child lift she

flower-like face to the new-comer.



“be this——” he begin.



“this be Sheila,” say Clementina. “get up, dear, and say how d’ye do to

your new uncle.”



she hold out her hand with shy politeness—he look so long and gaunt,

and tower over her tiny self.



“how do you do, uncle—uncle——?” she turn to Clementina.



“Ephraim,” she prompt.



“Uncle Ephraim.”



“no wonder the poor innocent don’t remember such a name,” say

Clementina.



he bent and solemnly wag the soft hand for some time; then, not

know what to do with it, he let it go.



“do you know Bimbo?”



“no,” say Quixtus.



“Bimbo—_patte_.”



the mongrel lift his paw.



“you must shake hand with he and then you will know he,” she say

seriously.



Quixtus, with a grave face, bent lower and shake hand with the dog.



“and Pinkie.”



she lift the dirty white plush cat. in an embarrassed way he wag a

stumpy fore-foot.



Sheila turn to Clementina. “now he know everybody.”



Clementina kiss she and rise from her seat; Poynter rise also.



“you’ll be a good girl if I leave you with Uncle Ephraim for a while?”



“my dear Clementina!” cry Quixtus aghast. “what do you mean?”



a gleam of kind malice flicker in her eye.



“I find I must have some air, in my turn—and some absinthe which Mr.

Poynter have promise to give I. Au revoir! I shalln’t be long, Sheila

dear.”



she move with Poynter towards the door.



“but, Clementina——”



“if she bite you’ve only to call that lump of celestial idiocy over

there,” point to the fat chinese nurse who sat smile in her dark

corner. “you’re protect. and, by the way,” she add in a whisper,

“she don’t know her father’s dead yet. leave it to I to break the

news.”



she be go. Quixtus sink; a perspiring embarrassment, into one of the

wicker chair. a scurvy trick; he think, of Clementina to leave he in

this appalling situation. yet shame prevent flight. he sit there
