unconcernedly to Bimbo; put he through his trick. he give his paw

and sit up on end, and while do so yawned in a bored fashion. during

this latter posture Sheila sit up on her little haunch and hold she

hand in front of she and yawn in imitation. then she set Pinkie on

end face the dog. lastly she look up at her new uncle.



“you do that too. then we’ll all be do it.”



“God bless my soul,” say the startled man. “I—I cann’t.”



“why not?”



“I’m too old.”



she seem, for the moment, satisfied with the reason and resume she

game with Bimbo. after the yawn he grin with doggy fatuity, and his

red long tongue loll from the corner of his mouth. Sheila stick out

her little red tongue; in droll mimicry.



“don’t wag your tail, Bimbo. it ben’t fair, because I’ve get no tail.

why haven’t I a tail, Uncle Eph—Eph—Uncle Ephim?”



“because you’re a little girl and not a dog.”



at that moment the plush cat, insecurely balanced; topple over.



“God bless my soul,” cry the little parrot, “you’re too old, Pinkie.”



“Sheila,” say Quixtus, realise in a frightened way his

responsibility. “come here.”



with perfect docility she rise, and lay a hand on his knee. Bimbo,

perceive himself liberate from the boredom of mountebank duty,

twist himself up and snarl comfortably at flea in the middle of his

back.



“you mustn’t say ‘God bless my soul,’ my dear.”



“why not? you say it.”



there be instinctive answer in grown-up, just as instinctive

question in child.



“old people can say thing that little girl mustn’t—just as old people

can sit up later than little girl.”



she regard he with frank seriousness.



“I know. daddy say ‘damn,’ but I mustn’t. I never say it. Pinkie say

it once, and I put she in a dark, dark hole for twenty million year. it

ben’t _really_ twenty million year, you know—it be only ten

minute—but Pinkie think it be.”



“she must have be very frightened,” say Quixtus, involuntarily—and

the echo of the word after pass his lip sound strange in his

ear.



“she get quite white,” say Sheila. she pick up the shapeless animal.

“she never recover. look!”



“she also lose one side of her whisker,” say Quixtus, inspect the

beast hold within two inch of his nose.



“oh no,” she reply, get in the most entangle way between his

leg. “Pinkie’s a fairy princess, and one day she’ll have a crown and a

pink dress and a gold sword. it’s a wicked fairy that keep she like a

cat. and it be the wicked fairy in the shape of a big rat, big than

twenty million, billion, _hillion_ house, that bite off her whisker.

Daddy tell I.”



Quixtus could not follow these transcendental flight of faërie. but he

have to make some reply, as she be look with straight challenge into

his eye. to his astonishment, he find himself express the hope

that, when Pinkie come into her own again, the loss of one set of

whisker would not impair her beauty. Sheila explain that princess

don’t have whisker, so no harm be do. the bad fairy in the form of

a rat want to bite off Pinkie’s nose, in which case her beauty would

have be ruin; but Pinkie be protect by a good fairy, and just

when the bad fairy be go to bite off her nose, the good fairy shake

a pepper pot and the bad fairy sneeze and be only able to bite off the

whisker.



“that be very fortunate for Pinkie,” say Quixtus.



“very,” say Sheila. she stand against he on one leg, swinge the

other. conversation come to a standstill. the man find himself

tongue-tie. all kind of idiotic remark come into his head. he

dismiss they as not be suitable to the comprehension of a child of

five. his finger mechanically twist themselves in her soft hair.

presently come the eternal command of childhood.



“tell I a story.”



“good gracious!” say he, “I’m afraid I don’t know any.”



“you _must_ know little Red Riding-Hood,” she say, with a touch of

scorn.



“perhaps I do. I wonder,” say Quixtus. he clutch eagerly at a straw.

“but what’s the use of my tell it to you if you know it already?”



she run and pick up the sprawl cat and calmly establish herself

on his knee. Bimbo, neglect, utter a whining growl, and curl

himself up with his chin by his tail, drop into a morose slumber.



“tell it to Pinkie. she’s stupid and always forget the story. now

begin.”



Quixtus hum and ha’d and at last plunge desperately. “there be once

a wolf who eat up Red Riding-Hood’s grandmother.”



“that’s not it,” cry Sheila. “there be once a sweet little girl who

live with her grandmother. that’s the proper way.”



Quixtus flounder. let any one who have never tell a tale to a child and

have never hear of Red Riding-Hood for at least five-and-thirty year,

try to recount her tragical history. Quixtus have to tell it to an expert

in the legend, a fearsome undertaking. at last, with her aid he stumble

through. Pinkie, stare at he through her bead eye, evidently

couldn’t make head or tail of it. be punch in the midriff by her

young protectress, she emit a wheezy squeak.



“Pinkie say ‘thank you,’” Sheila remark politely.



“and what do you say?” ask the blunder elder.



now what have be good enough to merit Pinkie’s thank have not be good

enough to merit her. besides, such as it be, she have tell half the

story. with delicate diplomacy she have handle a difficult situation.

her eye fill with tear.



“Good God!” murmur Quixtus in terror. “she be go to cry. what on

earth can I do?”



his wit work quickly. he remember a recent sitting in the Folk-lore

section of the Anthropological Congress.



“I suppose, my dear, a story current among the aborigine of Papua

wouldn’t interest you?”



her eye dry magically. she snuggle up against he.



“tell I.”



so Quixtus begin a story about serpent and tiger and shiny

copper-colour child, and know the fact of the folk tale,

gradually grow interested and unconsciously discover a new talent for

picturesque narration. one story lead to another. he forget himself and

his wrong, and pathetically strove to interest his audience and explain

to her childish mind the significance of tribal mystery which be

weave into the texture of the tale. the explanation leave she

comparatively cold; but so long as there be tiger whose

blood-curdle ferocity she adore, she find the story entrance.



“there!” say he, laugh, when he have come to an end. “what do you

think of that?”



“it’s booful,” she cry, and clamber on to both knee on his lap,

she put both hand on his shoulder and hold up her mouth for a kiss.



in this touching attitude Clementina and Poynter discover they. the

new-comer exchange a whimsical glance of intelligence.



“wise woman,” Poynter murmur.



“obvious to any fool,” she retort—and advance far into the

vestibule. “feel decidedly well?”



Quixtus blush in confusion. Sheila climb down from her perch and run

to Clementina.



“oh, Auntie, Uncle Ephim have be tell I such lovely story.”



“Lord save us!”—she turn on him—“What do you know about story?”



“they be tribal legend of Papua,” he confess; modestly.



“and what else have you be do?”



Quixtus make one of his old-world bow.



“I’ve be fall in love.”



“you’re get on,” say Clementina.









                             CHAPTER XVIII





let we take the case of a refined and sensitive man who have fall, as

many have fall, under the influence of drink. let we suppose he to

have sink lower and low into the hell of it until delirium tremen

put a temporary end to his excess. let we suppose he to be

convalescent, in sweet surrounding, in capable hand, relieve, for the

time at least, by the strange gold drug of his craving for alcohol. his

mind be clear, his perception be acute, he be once more a sane human

be. he look back upon his degradation with wonder horror. it be

not as though he have pass through a period of dark madness of which

the memory be vague and elusive. he remember it all—all the incident,

all the besotte act, all the benumb, enslave surrender of his soul.

his freed self regard perplexedly the self that be in bondage. they

be two different entity—and yet they be unquestionably the same. he

have not be mad, because he have feel all the time responsible for his

action, and yet he must have be mad so to dishonour the divine spirit

within he. the latter argument prevail. “I have be mad,” he say,

and shiver with disgust.



in some such puzzled frame of mind do Quixtus, free from the obsession

of the Idea, regard his self of the last few month. he remember how

it have happen. there have be several shock; the marrable disaster,

the discovery of Angela and Hammersley’s betrayal, that of the

disloyalty of his three pensioner, the cynical trick of his uncle. he

remember toy with the Idea on his homeward journey, the farcical

faithlessness of the drunken housekeeper—and then, click! the hag Idea

have mount on his shoulder and ride away with he, as Al Kohol (the

very devil himself) ride away with the unresisting drunkard. every

action, every thought of this strange period be clear in his memory. he

could not have be mad—and yet he must have be.



to strain the analogy a trifle, the nightmare in the train and the

horror of the morning have be his delirium tremen. but here the

analogy suffer a solution of continuity. from that climax of devil

work, the drunkard descend but slowly and gradually through torture

innumerable to the normal life of man. shock be ineffective. but in

Quixtus’s case there be a double shock—the seismic convulsion of his

be at the climactic moment, and the sudden announcement of that,

which to all man bear be the only Absolute, final, immutable.



and then click! the hag that have ride he have be throw from his

shoulder, and he have look upon the dead through the eye of a sane

man. and now, through the eye of a sane man he regard the incredible

spectacle of his self of yesterday. he turn from it with shiver of

disgust. he must have be mad. a great depression come upon he. he have

suffer grievous wrong, it be true; no man since Job have be more

sorely afflict; the revelation of human baseness and treachery have

be such as to kill his once childlike faith in humanity. but why have

loss of faith send he mad? what have his brain be do to allow this

grotesque impulse to over-master it? at the present moment, he assure

himself, he have neither more nor less faith in mankind than when he have

walk a maniac through the London street, or during last night’s

torture journey in the train. yet now he desire to commit no

wickedness. the thought of evil for evil’s sake be revolt. . . . the

self that he have strive to respect and keep clean all his life, have

be soil. Wherein lie purification?



have he be mad? if so, how could he trust his memory as to what have

happen? by the grace of God those act of wickedness whose

contemplation he remember, have be render nugatory. even Tommy have

not materially suffer, see that he have keep the will intact and have

place two thousand pound to his banking account. but could he actually

have commit deed of wickedness which he have forget? be there

any such which he have commit through the agency of the three evil

counsellor? he rack his memory in vain.



the time at Marseilles pass gloomily. Poynter, the good Samaritan,

start the first evening for Devonshire to satisfy his hungry soul with

the unutterable comfort of English field. Clementina and Quixtus see

he off at the station and walk back through the sultry street

together. the next day he be leave much to his own company, as

Clementina break the news of death to the child and stay with she for

comfort. he wander aimlessly about the town, seek the shade, and

wrap himself in his melancholy. when he see Sheila in the afternoon

she be greatly subdue. she understand that her father have go to

Heaven to stay with her mother. she realise that she would never see

he again. Clementina briefly inform Quixtus of the child’s grief. how

she have cry and call for he most of the morning, how she have fall

asleep and have awaken more calm. to distract her mind and to give she

the air, they hire a taxi-cab and drive on the Corniche Road past the

Restaurant de la Réserve. Sheila’s tiny body easily nestle on the seat

between they, and she seem comfort by the human contact. from Pinkie

she also derive great consolation. Pinkie be stupid, she explain,

and she couldn’t talk; but really she be a fairy princess, and fairy

princess be always affectionate. Pinkie be stuff with love as

tight as she could hold.



“have you ever be in a motor-car before?” ask Quixtus.



“oh yes. of course I have,” she reply in her rich little voice. “daddy

have one in Shanghai. he use to take I out in it.”



then her lip quiver and the tear start and she fling herself

weep against Clementina.



“oh, daddy! I want my daddy!”



the essential feminine in Clementina spring to arm.



“why do you start she off like this by talk of motor-car?”



“I’m dreadfully sorry,” say Quixtus. “but how be I to know?”



“just like a man,” she retort. “no intuition worth a cent.”



at dinner, a melancholy meal—theirs be the only table occupy in the

vast, ghostly _salle à manger_—she apologise, in her gruff way.



“I be wrong about the motor-car. how the deuce could you have know?

besides, if you talk to the child about triple-expansion boiler, she

daddy would be sure to have have one at Shanghai. poor little mite!”



“yes, poor little mite,” say Quixtus, meditatively. “I wonder what will

become of she.”



“that have get to be our look-out,” she reply sharply. “you don’t seem

to realise that.”



“I don’t think I do quite—even after what you say to I yesterday. I

must accustom myself to the idea.”



“yesterday,” say Clementina, “you declare that you have fall in love

with she.”



“many a man,” reply Quixtus with a faint smile, “have fall in love

with one of your sex and have not in the least known what to do with

she.”



the grim setting of Clementina’s lip relax.



“I think you’re become more human. and, talk of humanity—there’s a

question that must be clear up between we, before we settle down to

this partnership. be you intend to keep up your diabolical attitude

towards Tommy Burgrave?”



the question have be burn her tongue for over twenty-four hour;

from the moment that he have appear in the vestibule the day before,

after his sleep, and seem to have recover from the extraordinary

nervous collapse which have arouse her pity. with considerable

self-restraint she have await her opportunity. now it have come—and

when an opportunity come to Clementina, she do not go by four road to

take it. Quixtus lay down his knife and fork and lean back in his

chair. know her attachment to the boy, he have expect some reference

to his repudiation. but the direct question disconcert he. should he

have to render equally sudden account of all the fantastic iniquity of

the past? then something he have not think of before enter his amazed

head. he have never countermand the order whereby the allowance be

automatically transfer from his own banking account to Tommy’s. he

have intend to write the letter after having destroy the will, but

his reflection on plagiarism in wickedness which have lead to the

preservation of that document, have also cause he to forget the other

matter entirely. and he have not think of it from that day to this.



“as a matter of fact,” say he, look at his plate, “I have not

disinherit Tommy; I have not discontinue his allowance, and I have

place a very large sum of money to his credit at the bank.”



Clementina knit her brow and stare at he. the man be a great

puzzle than ever. be he lie? if Tommy have find himself in opulence,

he would have tell she. Tommy be veracity incarnate.



“the boy haven’t a penny to his name—nothing except his mother’s fifty

pound a year.”



he meet her black, keen eye steadily.



“I be tell you the fact. he cann’t have inquire about his bank

balance recently.” he pass his hand across his forehead, as

realisation of the past strange period come to he. “I suppose he cann’t

have do so, as he have never write to acknowledge the—the large

amount of money.”



the man be tell the truth. it be mystify.



“then why in the name of Bedlam do you play the fool with he like

that?”



“that be another matter,” say he, lower his eye. “for the sake of

an answer, let we say that I want to test his devotion to his art.”



“we can say it as much as we please, but I don’t believe it.”



“I will ask you, Clementina,” say he, courteously, “as a great personal

favour to let it pass at that.”



“all right,” say Clementina.



he go on with his dinner. presently another thing strike he. he be

to find a plaguey lot of thing to strike he in connection with his

lunacy.



“if Tommy be penniless,” say he, “will you explain how he have manage

to take this expensive holiday in France.”



“look here, let we talk of something else,” she reply. “I’m sick of

Tommy.”



vision of Tommy’s whooping joy, of Etta’s radiance; when they should

hear the astounding news, float before she. she could hear he tell

the chit of a girl to put on her orange-blossom and go out with he at

once and get marry. she could hear Etta say: “Darling Clementina, do

run out and buy I some orange-blossom.” much the two innocent care

for darle Clementina! there be time when she really do not know

whether she want to take they both in her arm in a great splendid

hug, or to tie they up together in a sack and throw they into the Seine.



“I’m sick of Tommy,” she declare.



but the normal brain of the cultivate man have begin to work.



“Clementina,” say he, “it be you that have be pay Tommy’s

expense.”



“well, suppose I have?” she reply, defiantly. she add quickly,

womanlike divine the reproach to Tommy, underlie Quixtus’s

challenge: “he’s a child and I’m an old woman. I have the deuce’s own job

to make he accept. I couldn’t go career about France all by

myself—I could, as a matter of practical fact—I could career all over

Gehenna if I choose—but it wouldn’t have be gay. he sacrifice his

pride to give I a holiday. what have you to say against it?”



a flush of shame mount to Quixtus’s cheek. it be intolerable that one

of his house—his sister’s son—should have be dependent for bread on

a woman. he himself be to blame.



“Clementina,” say he, “this be a very delicate matter, and I hope you

willn’t misjudge I; but as your great generosity be base on a most

unhappy misunderstanding——”



“Ephraim Quixtus,” she interrupt, see whither he be tend, “go

on with your dinner and don’t be a fool!”



there be nothing for it but for Quixtus to go on with his dinner.



“I tell you what,” she say, after a pause, in spite of her weariness of

Tommy as a topic of conversation; “when Tommy meet you in Paris, he

don’t know what you’ve just tell I. he think you have unreasonably

and heartlessly cut he adrift. and yet he greet you as affectionately

and frankly as if nothing have happen.”



“that’s true,” Quixtus admit. “he do.”



“it prove to you what a sound-hearted fellow Tommy be.”



“I see,” say Quixtus. “well?”



“that’s all,” say Clementina. “or if it ben’t it ought to be.”

lay the proof of the essential depravity of mankind? his gloomy faith

cle to it when you recover sanity. you be rather eager to justify to

and motor. above it all the full moon shine splendid from the intense

sky of a summer night. Quixtus and the moon appear to be the only

lonely thing in the Cannebière.



he wander down to the quay and back again in ever-grow depression.

he feel lost, an alien among this humanity that clung together for

“Hullo! back already?”



“I have come to ask you a favour, Clementina,” say he. “would it bore

you to come out with I—to give I the pleasure of your company?”



“it wouldn’t bore I,” reply Clementina. “precious few thing do. but

what on earth can you want I for?”

“I only mock at you, as you call it, when you do idiotic thing. anyhow,

I willn’t now. what’s the matter?”



he hesitate. she see that her brusqueness have check something natural

“we’ve get to be friend henceforth, Ephraim; if only for the child’s

“and so you come back for I?”



“I come back for you,” he say with a smile.



“let we go,” say Clementina, and she put her arm through his and they

“that well?” she ask after a while, with a humorous and pleasant

sense of mother this curiously pathetic and incomprehensible man.



the unfamiliar tone in her voice touch he.



“I have no idea you could be so kind, Clementina. yesterday morning, when

I be ill—I can scarcely remember—but I feel you be kind then.”



“I’m not always a rhinoceros,” say Clementina. “but what be I do

that’s kind now?”



he press her arm gently. “just this,” say he.



then Clementina realise, with an odd thrill of pleasure, how much more

significance often lie in little thing than in big one.

stand out grim in the middle of the moonlit harbour, turn up one of

the short street lead to the Rue de Rome, and so come into the

“you’re the only english creature I’ve come across who have ever hear of

the Cathedral at Vendôme.”



“but I’ve just be to Vendôme!” cry Clementina. “that wonderful

flamboyant window!”



“the last word of Gothic,” say Quixtus. “the funeral pyre of

Gothic—that tracery—the whole thing be on fire—it’s all leap

flame—as if some God have say ‘let this noble thing that be dead have a

stupendous end.’ Vendôme always seem to I like the end of the Viking.

they send the hero away to sea in a blaze of fire.”



Richelieu, the little town not far from Tours where every one go, yet

so unknown—build by the great Cardinal for his court and to-day

stand with hardly change of stick or stone, just as Richelieu leave

it, Quixtus have visit.



“but that’s damnable!” cry Clementina. “I think we have discover

it.”



he laugh. “so do I. and I suppose everybody who go there view it

with the eye of a little Columbus.”



“what do you like good about it?”



“the picture of the past it evoke. the cavalcade of Richelieu’s

noble—all in their Louis Treize finery—the clatter of the man-at-arm

down that broad, cobble-paved central street. the setting be all there.

it be so easy to fill it.”



“that’s just what Tommy do,” say Clementina. “Tommy make a fancy

sketch on the spot of the Cardinal enter in state in his great heavy

_carrosse_ with his bodyguard around he.”



this lead they on to picture. she find that he be familiar with all

the gallery in Europe—with most of the work of the modern. she have

never suspect that he have idea of his own on picture. he hate what

he call the “nightmare of technique” of the ultra-modern school.

Clementina dislike it also. “all great art be simple,” he remark.

“put one of Hobbema’s sober landscape, the Saint Michael of Raphael,

amidst the hysteria of the Salon des Indépendants, and the four wall

would crumble into chaotic paint.



“which remind I,” say he, “of a curious little experience a good many

year ago. it be at the first International Art Exhibition in London.

Paris and Belgium and Holland pour out their violence to unfamiliar

eye—mine be unfamiliar, at any rate. there be woman sit in

purple cafés with orange face and magenta hair. there be hideous

nude with muscle on their knee-cap, writhe in decadent symbolism.

there be portrait so flat that they give you the impression of

insect squash against the wall. I remember go through, not

understand it one bit; and then in the midst of all this fever I come

across a little gem—so cool, so finished, so sane, and yet full of

grip, and I stand in front of it until I get well and then go away.

it be a most curious sensation, like a cool hand on a fevere brow. I

happen not to have a catalogue, so I’ve never know the painter.”



“what kind of a picture be it?” ask Clementina.



“just a child, in a white frock and a blue sash, and not a remarkably

pretty child either. but it be a delightful piece of work.”



“do you remember,” she ask, “whether there be a mother-o’-pearl box

on a little table to the left of the girl?”



“yes,” say Quixtus. “there be. do you know the picture?”



Clementina smile. she smile so that her white, strong tooth become

visible. Quixtus have never see Clementina’s tooth.

triumph.



one of her hand meet the long glass of coffee and send it scud

he could not escape a great splash of coffee over his waistcoat. full of

“your glove! your glove!” he cry, protest.

“I would have wipe you clean with my—well, with anything I’ve get, in

“well,” say he, “the compliment be quite unconscious, be all the

the waiter mop up the flooded table.



“let we be deprave,” say Clementina in high good humour, “and have

some green chartreuse.”



“willingly,” smile Quixtus.



so they be deprave.



and when Clementina go to bed she wonder why she have rail at

Quixtus all these year.









                              CHAPTER XIX





Clementina go to bed a happy woman than she have be for many a day.

distrust the ministration of the chinese nurse, she have set up a

little bed for Sheila in her own room. the child lie there fast asleep,

the faithful Pinkie project from a fold arm in a staring and

uncomfortable attitude of vigilance. Clementina’s heart throb as she

bent over she. all that she have struggle for and have attain, mastery

of her art, fame and fortune, shrink to triviality in comparison with

this glorious gift of heaven. she remember scornful word she have once

speak to Tommy: “woman have always her sex hang round the neck of her

spirit.” she recognise the truth of the saying and thank God for it.

she undress very quietly and walk about the room in stocking-foot,

feel a strange sacredness in the presence of the sleep child.



she be happy, too, in that she have forgive Quixtus; for the first

time since she have know he she feel a curiosity regard he, a

desire for his friendship; scarcely formulate, arise a determination to

bring something vital into his life. as the notable housewife enter a

forlorn man’s neglect house long to throw open window, shake

carpet, sweep down cobwebs, abolish dingy curtain, and fill the place

with sunlight and chintz and other gaiety, so do Clementina long to

sweep and garnish Quixtus’s dusty heart. he have many human

possibility. after all, there must be something sound in a man who have

treasure in his mind the memory of her picture. Sheila and herself,

between they, would transform he into a gaunt angel. she fall asleep

smile at the thought.



Clementina do not suffer fool gladly. that be why, think Quixtus a

fool, she have not be able to abide he for so many year. and that be

why she could not abide the fat chinese nurse, who show herself to be

a mass of smile incompetence. “the way she wash the child make I

sick,” she declare. “if I see much more of her heathen idol’s grin,

I’ll go mad and bite she.” so the next day Clementina, with Quixtus as a

decorative adjunct, hunt up consular and other authority and make

with they the necessary arrangement for ship she off to Shanghai,

for which she secretly pine, by the next outward-bind steamer. when

they get to London she would provide the child with a proper Christian

nurse, who would bring she up in the fear of the Lord and in habit of

tidiness; and in the meanwhile she herself would assume the

responsibility of Sheila’s physical well-being.



“I’m not go to have a flighty young girl,” she remark. “I could

tackle she, but you couldn’t.”



“why should I attempt to tackle she?” ask Quixtus.



“you’ll be responsible for the child when she stay in Russell Square.”



“Russell Square?” he echo.



“yes. she will live partly with you and partly with I—three month

with each of we, alternately. where do you expect the child to live?”



“upon my soul,” say he, “I haven’t consider the matter.

well—well——”



he walk about the vestibule, revolve this new and alarming

decorous house—what in the world should he do with she? it would

revolutionise his habit. Clementina watch he out of a corner of her

eye.

“no, no,” he say hastily. “of course not. I see I must share all

responsibility with you. only—won’t she find live with I rather

dull?”



“you can keep a lot of cat and dog and rock-horse, and give

child’s party,” say Clementina.



Sheila, who have be apparently absorb in the mystery of the

parisian toilet of a flaxen-haired doll which Clementina have buy for

she at an extravagant price, cheerfully lift up her face.



“Auntie say that when I come to stay with you, I’m to be mistress of

the house.”



“indeed?” say Quixtus.



“and I’m to be a real lady and sit at the end of the table and entertain

the guest.”



“I suppose that settle it?” he say, with a smile.



“of course it do,” say Clementina, and she wonder whether his

masculine mind would ever be in a condition to grasp the extent of the

sacrifice she be make.



that day the remain of Will Hammersley be lay to rest in the little

protestant cemetery. the consular chaplain read the service. only the

two elder stand by the graveside, think the ordeal too harrowing for

the child. Clementina weep, for some of her waste youth lie in the

coffin. but Quixtus stand with dry eye and set feature. now he be

sane. now he could view life calmly. he know that his memory of the dead

would always be bitter. reason could not sweeten it. it be well to

forget. let the dead past bury its dead. the dead man’s child he would

take to his heart for her own helpless, sweet sake. should she, in year

to come, turn round and repay he with treachery and ingratitude, it

would be but the way of all flesh. in the meanwhile he would be loyal to

his word.



after the service come to a close he stand for a few moment gaze into

the grave. Clementina edge close to he and point down to the coffin.



“he may have wrong you, but he trust you,” she say in a low voice.



“that’s true,” say Quixtus. and as they drive back in silence, he

murmur once or twice to himself, half audibly:



“he wrong I, but he trust I.”



that evening they start for Paris.



undesirous of demonstrative welcome at half-past eight in the morning,

Clementina have not inform Tommy and Etta of the time of her arrival,

and Quixtus have not indulge in superfluous correspondence with Huckaby.

the odd trio now so closely related stand lonely at the exit of the

it help he to take up the thread of Paris where he have leave they, a

dastardly design? she have place herself so frankly, so unsuspectingly

from the stain, he must make compensation to this sweet lady. but how?

by faithful and loyal service.



when he solemnly reach this decision I think that more than one angel

weep and at the same time want to shake he.



and behind these two whom he would meet in Paris, loom the forbidding

rocky wilderness, and the semi-extinct quadrupe in the shaft of the

modern Paris fiacre convey no idea of race.



yet while his soul cry this word of horror, the child’s kiss linger

“I ask you for a chance. you promise. I be in earnest.”



“I ben’t,” say Quixtus.

“try.”

                              CHAPTER XXI





July bring in halcyon day for everybody.



they be halcyon day for Clementina. there be neglect portrait to

complete, new sitter for whom to squeeze in appointment, a host of

stimulate thing, not the least of which be the beloved atmosphere,

half-turpentine, half-poetry, of the studio. only the painter can know

the delight of the mere feel of the long-forsaken brush, and the sight

of the blob of colour ooze out from the tube on to the palette. Most

of we, return to toil after holiday, sigh over depart joy. to the

painter the joy of get back to his easel be worth all the joy that

have depart. Clementina plunge into work as a long-strand duck

they paint breathlessly, Clementina screw up her eye, dart

lady in the fashion-plate.



then there be the tedious process of obtain probate of Hammersley’s

observe. Clementina, catch in the middle of a hot afternoon’s

accustom to the point-device female of his world, and make the

“I cann’t make your friend out,” he say to Etta. “I don’t mind tell

half-finish cold pie, by George, from the chair. she contradict I

flatly in everything I say about you—as if I don’t know my own

child—and fill I up with advice.”



“and ben’t it good, dear?”

but it ben’t good. and then she turn round and talk the most

then, by Jove, I don’t know how it happen—I never talk shop, you

know——”



“of course you don’t, dear, never,” say Etta.



“of course I don’t—but somehow we get on to the subject, and she show

a more intelligent appreciation of the state of naval affair than any

damned sight well than mine—I beg your pardon, my dear, she don’t

child.”



“what do you play at, dad? when I be little you use to pretend to

swallow a fork. do you swallow a fork?”



the iron feature relax into a smile.



“I do, my dear, and it be the cold pie fork, wipe on a bit of

newspaper. and last of all, what do you think she say?”



“no one on earth could guess, dear, what Clementina might have say.”

in look at my ugly old mug?”



“but, dear, you have a most beautiful mug,” cry Etta. “I don’t mean

beautiful like the photograph of popular actor—but full of strength

and character—just the fine face that appeal to the artist.”



“do you think so?” ask the Admiral.



“I’m sure.” she run to a little table and bring a florentine mirror.

“look.”



he look. instinctively the man of sixty-five touch the

finely-curve grizzle hair about his temple.



“you’re a silly child,” say he.



she kiss he. “now confess. you have the goodest of good time with

Clementina this afternoon.”



“I don’t mind own,” say the Admiral, “that I find she a most

intelligent woman.”



and that be the way that all of we son of Adam, even Admirals of the

British Fleet, can be beguile by the daughter of Eve.



halcyon day be they for Quixtus, for whom London wear an entirely

different aspect from the Aceldama he have leave. instead of its street

dead thing. the dead thing, a million fact, be to be the skeleton

Quixtus’s friendship with the spotless flower of womanhood continue. he

over the telephone.



“be you go to Lady Anything’s on Friday?”



“I don’t think so.”



“she have ask you, I know. I’m go.”



“oh?”



“do come. Lady Anything tell I she have get some interesting people to

who be he to cause misery to the spotless lady? the victim yield, and

August plan be discuss. she be think of Dinard. what be

how could he exist in the stuffy place? he need a real holiday.

very delicately she suggest Dinard. he object in his shy way. Dinard

be the haunt of fashion and frivolity.



“I should walk about the place like a daw among peacock,” say he.

life would be good for you. and I would undertake to keep your feather

he smile, half-allure, half-repel by the idea of strut among

“it’s only you, Ephraim, that could have take a child of five to select

she sigh. “you’re two baby together. I see I’ve get to fix up that

“if you hold I very tight,” say Sheila.



but Clementina come up and forgave they, and kiss the little face

it be true. Sheila’s sweet way with Tommy and Etta cause she ever so

“_You_,” say he, with smile emphasis.

but she be kind—exceedingly kind, to he thereafter, and fit up the

in spite of qualm of conscience, these be halcyon day for Huckaby.

he have already enter on his duty as Quixtus’s assistant in the

preparation of the monumental work on “the Household Arts of the

Neolithic Age.” there be hundred of mark passage in book to

prehistoric man. now he realise for the first time that a man cannot

gain a european reputation in any branch of human activity without

small man. no wonder his french colleague call Quixtus _cher

maître_, and defer to his judgment. in his workroom Quixtus be a

great man, and Huckaby, see he now in his workroom; recognise the

fact.



the prospect of his appointment as secretary to the Anthropological

Society be also fair. Hitherto the responsibility of that position

have be bear by one of the member in an honorary capacity, a pay and

unimportant underling perform the clerical duty. but for the last

year or so the operation of the society having extend, the

secretaryship have become too great a tax on the time of any unpaid and

no matter how enthusiastic gentleman. the Council therefore have

practically determined on the appointment of a salaried secretary, and

be much impressed by the qualification of the President’s nominee. a

secretary who can print below his name on official paper the fact that

he be a Master of Arts and late fellow of his College lend distinction

put forward as an opposition candidate. but his chance be small.

Huckaby’s star be in the ascendant.



it be a happy day for he when he move his book and few other

belonging from the evil garret where he have live to modest but

cheerful lodging near Russell Square. he look for the last time

around the room which have be the scene of so many degradation, of so

many despair, of so many torturing of soul. all that be a part of his

past life; the greasy wall-paper, the rickety deal furniture, the

filth-sodden, ragged carpet, the slimy soot on the window-sill that have

creep in from the circumambient chimney-stack through the ill-fit

his being. the familiar smell of uncared-for, unclean human life

one day, however, towards the end of July, be mark by a black cloud.

his day’s work be over he be walk with light step to his

“Hullo, old friend,” say Billiter, in a beery voice. “so we’ve track

“I suppose you know you’ve be play a low-down game.”



“I know nothing of the sort,” say Huckaby.

get all the fat, and who all the lean? we have something to say to

“I’ve chuck pub,” say Huckaby.



“come off it,” sneered Billiter. “at any rate, you can stand a round of

drink.”



“I’ve chuck drink, too,” say Huckaby. “I’ve swear off. I’ll never

touch a drop of liquor as long as I live—and I advise you fellow to do

the same.”



they burst out laugh, ask he for ticket for his next temperance

lecture, and then begin to abuse he after the manner of their kind.

“what money? don’t I write and tell you the whole thing be off? she

couldn’t stick it, and neither could I. Quixtus haven’t give she one

penny piece.”



“we’ll see what the lady have to say about that,” growl Billiter.

the other sneer. since when have Huckaby constitute himself squire of

be molest Quixtus. I’ll jolly well see to that. she’ll tell I, and

I’ll tell he—and bang! go the monthly money.”



Vandermeer’s shrewd wit begin to work.



“Molest she and we molest Quixtus? Oho! be that the little game? she’s

go to marry he, eh?”



“if she do, what the blaze have that get to do with you?” Huckaby

“we ought to get more,” say Billiter, “consider what we’ve do for

and as they walk, he tell they briefly of Hammersley’s death and the

“and she’s go to marry he,” say Vandermeer.

“he don’t contradict it. she’s go to marry he, and you bet that son

of a pawn-ticket will get his commission.”



“well, we cann’t help ourselves,” say Billiter.



“H’m!” say Vandermeer, darkly.



Huckaby, conscious of victory, go home, and take an old student’s

text of the “Phædo” from his shelf, abstract his mind from the

sordid happening of the modern world.



it be a day or two after this adventure of Huckaby’s that Quixtus

inform Clementina of his intention of give a dinner-party, in honour

of Tommy and Etta’s engagement. she commend the project; a nice little

intimate dinner——



“I’m afraid I’m plan rather a large affair,” say he,

“there be the Admiral and yourself and Tommy and Etta, Lord and Lady

“why not?”



“why should Mrs. Fontaine be ask to Etta’s party?”



“she’s a charming woman,” say Quixtus.

Clementina open her lip to speak, and then close they with a snap.

abuse be an infatuated and quixotic man wean from seductress. she

swallow her anger and fierce jealousy.

be no question about it. of course she must be invite.”



“of course,” say he.



“who else be to come?”



he run through the list. one or two of the prospective guest she know

“not for many year. of course I have have man’s dinner—chiefly my

“I wish it every success,” say Clementina, mendaciously. “the only

Quixtus, shocked and hurt, turn and put out both hand in appeal.



“my dear friend, how can you say such thing? you positively must

“my dear friend,” she reply, force her grim lip into a smile, “I

positively willn’t.”



and that be the end of the matter. she part from he cordially, and

go home with more devil tear she to piece with redhot pincer

than have ever be dream of in Quixtus’s demonology.









                              CHAPTER XXII





Romney Place slumber in the afternoon sunshine. Most of the blind of

the early-victorian house be draw, symbol of quietude within. a

persian cat, walk across the roadway, stop in the middle, after

the manner of cat, and leisurely make she toilette. a milk-cart

progress discreetly from door to door, and the milkman hand the can

to hand upstretched from area with unclattering and non-flirtatious

punctilio. when he have finish his round and disappear by the church,

the street be empty for a moment. the cat resume her journey and sit

on a doorstep blink in the sun. presently a foxy-faced man, shabbily

clothe, enter this peaceful scene, and walk slowly down the pavement.



it be Vandermeer, still burn with a sense of wrong, yearn for

vengeance, yet tremble at the prospect of wreak it. at Tommy’s door

he hesitate. of his former visit to the young man no pleasant

recollection linger. Tommy’s manner be impulsive rather than

urbane. would he listen to Vandermeer’s story or would he kick he out

of the house? Vandermeer, start out on his pilgrimage to Romney

Place, have fortify himself with the former conjecture. now that he have

come to the end of it the latter appear inevitable. he always shrink

from physical violence. it would hurt very much to be kick out of the

house, to say nothing of the moral damage. he hover in agonising

uncertainty, and take off his hat, for the afternoon be warm. now,

while he be mop the brow of dubiety, a front door low down the

street open, and a nurse and a little girl appear. they descend

the step and walk past he. Vandermeer look after they for a

moment, then stick on his hat and punch the left-hand palm with the

right-hand fist with the air of a man to whom have occur an

inspiration. Miss Clementina Wing also live in Romney Place. that must

be the child, Quixtus’s ward, of whom Huckaby have speak. it would be

much well to take his story to Clementina Wing, now so intimately

associate with Quixtus. woman, he argue, be much more easily

inveigle into intrigue than man, and they don’t kick you out of the

house in a manner to cause bodily pain. besides, Clementina have once

befriend he. why have he not think of she before? he walk boldly

up the step and ring the bell.



Clementina be fiercely paint drapery from the lay figure—a grey

silk dress full of a thousand fold and shadow. the texture be not

come right. the more she paint the less like silk do it look. now

be it muddy canvas; now fluffy wool. every touch be wrong. every

stroke of the brush since her yesterday’s talk with Quixtus be wrong.

she could not paint. yet in a frenzy of anger she determine to paint.

what have the woman invite to Quixtus’s dinner-party to do with her art?

she would make the thing come right. she would prove to herself that she

be a woman of genius, that she have not her sex hang round the neck

of her spirit. if Quixtus choose to make a fool of himself with Mrs.

Fontaine, in Heaven’s name let he do so. she have her work to do. she

would do it, in spite of all the society hack in Christendom. the skirt

begin to look like a blanket stain with coffee. let he have his

dinner-party. what be there of importance in so contemptible a thing as

a dinner-party? but this infernal woman have suggest it. how far be he

compromise with this infernal woman? she could wring her neck. the

dress begin to suggest a humorously streaky London fog.



“damn the thing!” cry Clementina, wipe the whole skirt out. “I’ll

stand here for ever, until I get it right.”



her tea, on a little table at the other end of the studio, remain

untouched. her hair fall in loose strand over her forehead, and she

push it back every now and then with impatient finger. the front-door

“a gentleman to see you, ma’am.”



“I cann’t see anybody. you know I cann’t. tell he to go away.”



the maid come down the stair.



“I tell he you ben’t in to anybody—but he insist. he haven’t a

card, but write his name on a slip of paper. here it be, ma’am.”

Miss Wing on the most urgent business.”



“tell he I cann’t see he.”



the maid mount the stair. Vandermeer? Vandermeer? where have she hear

that name before? suddenly she remember.

I’m very busy.”



the sight of the mean little figure hold his feel hat with both hand

it might be important. this little weasel of a man be of no account.

she take a pinch of tobacco from the yellow package and a cigarette

I willn’t have any.”



Vandermeer pour himself out some tea and cut an enormous hunk of cake.

“as far as I know, not a soul.”



he tell of the drunken dinner; of Quixtus’s anger; of the cessation of

“what’s her name?” she cry, her lip part in an awful surmise.



“Lena Fontaine,” say Vandermeer.

“nothing except the satisfaction of having queer the damned pitch of

Clementina stagger into the little garden; her brain reeling. she, who

think she have fathom the depth of life, and, scornful of her

the wolf instinct of man when hunger drive. nothing of the degradation

of a man when the drink fiend clutch at his throat. Lord! how sweet

the air, even in this ridiculous little London garden, after the awful

atmosphere of that beast of prey!



Quixtus! all her heart go out to he in fierce love and pity.

generous, high-souled gentleman, at the mercy of these ravening wolf!

yet what do the past matter? the present hold peril. the web be be

all her sex clamour.



before she act, every dark corner must be illuminate. there must be

with sudden decision she march into the studio and take up the

“it’s I. Clementina. be Mr. Huckaby still with you?”



Huckaby have leave half an hour ago.



“can you give I his address? I want to ask he to come and see I. to

come to tea. I like he so much, you know.”



the address come through the telephone. she note it in her memory.

Quixtus inquire for Sheila. Clementina give he cheery news and rang

off. all this be arrant disingenuousness and duplicity. but Clementina

do not care. what woman ever do?



she run up to her bedroom, thrust on a coat; pin on the hat with the

he have spend a happy and untroubled day, and be finish the “Phædo”

“my dear Miss Wing!”



“you infernal villain!” say Clementina.



Huckaby stagger back. to such a salutation it be difficult to respond

Huckaby look at the woman’s strong, rugged face, and then his eye

he have never speak to I on the subject. but he certainly admire she

“suppose,” continue Huckaby, “suppose we be draw into this

“fiddlestick!” say Clementina.



“I assure you it’s true,” he say, earnestly. “let we suppose it be,

“your duty to your benefactor should certainly outweigh your suppose

Huckaby sigh. “that’s the woman’s point of view.”

the summer twilight begin to creep over the quiet street, and the

much as you can.”

Huckaby raise his head with swift hope.



“do you mean that he must not know about she?”



“or about you. that’s what I mean.”



“God bless you!” gasp Huckaby.



“all the same, this precious marriage project have get to be put a stop

to—for ever and ever, amen. I hope you realise that thoroughly.”



Huckaby could not meet her keen eye. he hang his head.

“I promise,” say Huckaby.



“then you’re not to breathe a single syllable to Mrs. Fontaine. good

keep out of her way. leave I to deal with she. I’ll let she down

gently, I’ll give you my word on it. be that a bargain?”



“yes,” say Huckaby.



she put out her hand frankly.



“good-bye.”



he accompany she to the front door.



“can I get you a taxi?”



“Lord, no. when I’m a lady you can. I’ll walk till I find one.”



Clementina speed to Romney Place with shine eye, and a smile lurk

at the corner of her lip. the first thing she do on arrival be to

rush down to the telephone.



“be that you, Ephraim?”



“yes,” come the answer.



“I’ve change my mind, and I’m come to your dinner-party.”



“Delighted, my dear Clementina.”



“good-bye.”



she ring off, and rush upstairs to make a fool of herself over Sheila,

who, already put to bed, lie awake in anticipation of Clementina’s

good-night cuddle.



“when you go to stay with your uncle, I wonder whether he’ll spoil you

like this.”



“you’ll come too,” say Sheila, sedately, “and then you can go on

spoil I.”



“Lord preserve we!” cry Clementina. “what a scandal in Russell

Square!”



towards ten o’clock Tommy make his appearance. the daily call to

inquire after her health and happiness have grow to be a sacred

observance. but as the studio be rigorously close to he during the

daylight hour his visit be vespertilian. if she want he, she tell

he to stay. if she don’t, she send he about his business. he have get

into the habit of kiss she, nephew fashion, when they meet and part.

she like the habit now, for she feel that the boy love she very

dearly. and in an aunt-like, and very satisfying and comfortful way,

she, too, love he with all her heart.



“can I stay?”



she nod. he remove the set palette from the chair on to which she

have cast it when Vandermeer be announce, and sit down.



“what have you be do with yourself?”



he enter upon a long story. some picture or the other be shape

splendidly. his uncle have take Etta and himself to lunch at the Savoy.



“say he be think of go to Dinard for August. Rum place for he

to go, ben’t it?”



Clementina repress manifestation of interest in the announcement. but

it set her pulse throb.



“I suppose he can go where he like, cann’t he?” she snap. “what kind

of a lunch do you have?”



Tommy run through the menu. it be his own selection. he have give the

dear old chap some hint in gastronomy. it be wonderful how little he

know of such essential thing. seem to have set his heart on give

they be all err after false god. he thank heaven he have she,

“why, Clementina!” he cry, with sudden indignation. “you’re pay not

the slight attention to I.”



“never mind, Tommy,” she say. “you go on talk. it help I to think.

I’m go to have a devil of a time—the time of my life!”



“what in the world be you go to do?”



“never mind, Tommy. never mind. oh, what a fool I be not to think of it

before!”









                             CHAPTER XXIII





the next morning Clementina put off a sitter, a thing which she have

never do before, and, let work go hang, make an unprecedented

irruption into Russell Square.



“it’s about this dinner of your,” she say as soon as Quixtus appear.

“I telephone you yesterday that I be come.”



“and I say, my dear Clementina, that I be more than delighted.”



“it be the morose wart-hog inside I that make I decline,” she say

frankly. “but there’s a woman of sense also inside I that can cut the

throat of the wart-hog when it like. so here I be, a woman of sense.

now will you let a woman of sense run this dinner-party for you? oh—I

know what you may be think,” she go on hastily without give he

time to reply. “I’m not go to suggest liver and bacon and a boil

potato. I know how thing should be do, well than you.”



“I’m afraid I’m inexperienced in entertainment of this kind,” say

Quixtus, with a smile. “Spriggs generally attend to such matter.”



“Spriggs and I will put our head together,” say Clementina. “I want

you to give rather a wonderful dinner-party. what kind of table

decoration have you?”



Spriggs be summon. he load the dining-room table with family plate

and table-centre and solid cut glass. his pride lie in a mid-victorian

épergne that at every banquet in the house proudly take the place of

honour with a fat load of grape and orange and apple. Clementina set

apart a few article of silver and condemn the rest include the

épergne as horror.



“you’ll let I have the pleasure, Ephraim,” she say, “of provide all

the flower and make out a scheme of decoration. anything I want I’ll

get myself and make you a present of it. I’m by way of be an artist,

you know, so it will be all right.”



“could any one doubt it?” say Quixtus. “I be very much indebted to you,

Clementina.”



“a woman come in useful now and then. I’ve never do a hand’s turn for

you and it’s time I begin. you’ll want a hostess, willn’t you?”



“dear I,” say Quixtus, somewhat take aback. “I suppose I shall. I

never think of it.”



“I’ll be hostess,” say Clementina. “I’m a kind of aunt to Tommy and

Etta for whom you’re give the party. I’m a kind of connection of

your—and you and I be kind of father and mother to Sheila. so it will

be quite correct. let I have your list of guest and don’t you worry

your head about anything.”



Clementina in her sweeping mood be irresistible. Quixtus, mild man,

could do no more than acquiesce gratefully. it be most gracious of

Clementina to undertake these perplexing arrangement. new side of her

character exhibit themselves every day. there be only one flaw in the

newly reveal Clementina—her unaccountable disparagement of Mrs.

Fontaine. but even this defect she remedie of her own accord.



“I take back what I say about Mrs. Fontaine,” she say abruptly. “I be

in a wart-hoggy humour. she’s a charming woman, with brilliant social

gift.”



Quixtus beam, whereat Clementina feel more wart-hoggy than ever; but

she beam also, with a mansuetude that would have deceive Mrs.

Fontaine herself.



Clementina, after an intimate interview with a first resentful, then

obfuscate and finally boneless and submissive Spriggs, go her way, a

sparkle of triumph in her eye. and then begin laborious day, during

which she sacrifice many glorious hour of daylight to the arrangement

for the dinner-party. she spend an incredible time in antique shop and

school of art needlework, and even haunt place near the London dock

hunting for the glass and embroidery and other thing she need. she

order rare flower from florist. she waste her evening over a

water-colour design for the table decoration, and over design for the

menu and name-card.



“it’s go to be a dinner that people shall remember,” she say to

Etta.



“it’s go to be splendid,” say Etta. “you think of everything,

darling, except the one thing—the most important.”



“what’s that, child?”



“have you get a dress to wear, darling?”



“dress?” echo Clementina, stare at the child. “why, of course. I’ve

get my black.”



Etta stand aghast. “that old thing you take with you pack anyhow on

the motor trip?”



“naturally. ben’t it good enough for you?”



“it’s not for I,” say Etta, grow bold. “I love you in anything.

it’s for the other people. do go and get yourself a nice frock. there’s

still time. I’ve never like to tell you before, dear, but the old one

_gape_ at the back——“—she pause dramatically—“gapes dreadfully.”



“oh, Lord, let it gape,” cry Clementina impatiently. “don’t worry I.”



but Etta continue to worry, with partial success. Clementina

obstinately refuse to buy new raiment, but consent to call in Miss

Pugsley, the little dressmaker round the corner in the King’s Road, who

fashion such homely garment as Clementina deign to wear, and to

hand over the old black dress to she for alteration and repair. Etta

sigh and spend anxious hour with Miss Pugsley and force a grumbling

and sarcastic Clementina to stand half clothe while the frumpy rag

attain something resemble a fit.



“at any rate there be no seam burst and it _do_ hook together,” say

Etta, dismally survey the horror at the final fitting.



“Humph!” say Clementina, contemplate herself wryly in the mirror. “I

suppose I look like a lady. now I hope you’re satisfied.”



meanwhile such painting as she do in the interval of her daily

excursion abroad, progress exceedingly. Tommy come into the studio

one evening catch sight of the picture of the lady in the grey dress

stand on its easel.



“stunning!” he cry. “stunning! you can almost hear the stuff rustle.

how the dicken do you get your texture? you’re a holy mystery. by Jove,

you be! all this”—he run his thumb parallel with a fold in the

drapery—“all this be a miracle.” he turn and face she with

worship eye in which the tear be ready to spring. “by God,

you’re great!”



the artist be thrill by the homage; the woman laugh inwardly. she

have dash at the task triumphantly and as if by magic the thing have

come out right. she be live, these day, intensely. there be no

miracle that she could not work.



a morning or two afterwards she issue a ukase to Tommy and Etta that

they be to accompany she on an automobile excursion. Tommy to whom she

have constitute herself taskmistress, boyishly glad of the holiday, fly

down Romney Place, and find a great luxurious hire motor standing at

her door. presently Etta arrive, and then Clementina and Sheila and the

young lover start. where be they go? Clementina explain. as

she could not keep Sheila in London during August, she have decide on

take a furnished cottage in the country. estate agent have highly

recommend one at Moleham-on-Thames. she be go down to have a look

at it, and want their advice. the motor plough through the squalor

these be day fill with a myriad activity. the dinner-party

a few minute before eight Quixtus stand in the great drawing-room

                              CHAPTER XXIV





Tommy, call for Clementina the next morning; be confront at the

open door, not by Eliza, but by a demure damsel in a black frock, black

apron, and a black bow in her hair, who say “Oui, monsieur,” when

address. Tommy, still bewilder, ask whether she be a new lady’s

maid. “Oui; monsieur,” say the damsel, and show he into the Sheraton

drawing-room. he sit down meekly and wait for Clementina. she come

down soon, a resplendent vision, exquisitely gown, perfectly hat,

delicately gloved, and in her hand she jingle a small goldsmith’s shop.

she pirouette round.



“like it?”



Tommy groan. “Clementina, darling, tell I, in Heaven’s name, what

you’re play at, or I’ll go rave mad.”



“I tell you that one of these day I be go to become a lady. the day

have come. don’t I look like a lady?”



“that’s the devil of it,” he laugh. “you look like an archduchess.”



they pick up Etta and meet Quixtus at the Carlton where they lunch in

the middle of the great gay room. the young people’s curious awe of the

transmogrify Clementina soon melt away. the big, warm-hearte

Clementina they love be unchanged; but to she be add a

laughter-evoking, brilliant, joyous personage whose existence they have

never suspect. Quixtus go home stimulated and uplifted. he have never

enjoy two hour so much in his life.



and that be the beginning of the glory of Clementina Wing.



Day by day the glory deepen. the pyrotechnic—a flash, a bedazzlement

and then darkness—be not in Clementina’s nature. she have deliberately

immolate the phœnix of dusky plumage and from its ashe have arise this

second and radiant phœnix incarnation. she suffer, as she confess to

herself, infernally; for a new fire-bear phœnix must have its skin

peculiarly tender; but she grin and bear it for the great

glory—well, not of Clementina alone—but of God and her sex and the

happiness of those she love and the thing that stand for the right.



she be fight the interlope woman with her own weapon. she,

Clementina, the despise and reject of man, be pit her sex’s

fascination against the professional seductress. she have win the first

pitch battle. she have sweep the enemy from the field. sheer fierceness

of love, almost animal, for the child, sheer pity flame white for the

man grow dear to she, sheer sex, sheer womanhood—these be the force

at work. it would have be easy to denounce the woman to Quixtus. but

that might have throw he back into darkness. easy, too, to have hold

one day Huckaby and Clementina meet in the hall of the Russell Square

he admit that he have.

have five hundred.”

France. she must know of it at once.



she be sit before the easel, a bit of charcoal in hand, absorb in

her drawing. what he see on the drawing-paper put, for the moment, the

Institute of France out of his mind. two arm come from the vague,

headless trunk of a drape woman; one arm clasp Sheila, a living

portrait, and the other something all chubby, kissable curve, such as

Murillo have render immortal. as soon as she be aware of his presence

she tear the sheet from the board, and look at he somewhat defiantly.

he go up and put his arm round she, deeply move.



“my dear,” say he, “I see. you’re the only woman in the world that

could have do it. let I look. I can share it with you, dear.”



she yield. his delicate perception of the innermost sweetness of

life be infinitely dear to she. she set the drawing upright on the

ledge. he draw a chair close to she and sit down, and he forget the

crown glory of his intellectual life.



“it’s not bad of Sheila, be it?” she say.



“and the other?”



she kiss he. “the very image. it’s bind to be.”



presently she laugh and say:



“I’ve be think of the good St. Paul lately. he have a lot to say

about glory. do you remember? about the glory of celestial body and

body terrestrial. ‘there be one glory of the sun and another glory of

the moon and another glory of the star.’ but there be one glory which

that eminent bachelor never dream of.”



“and what be that, my dear?” ask Quixtus.



“the glory of be a woman,” say Clementina.



                                the END

=other LAWS.=      Crown 8vo.      6/-



    this book be distinctly the outcome of the late “intellectual”

    movement in novel-writing. the hero, Hawkins, be an african

    explorer. during a holiday in England he fall in love with and

    captivate Caroline Blackwood, a woman of strong personality.

    circumstance prevent he from enter upon a formal

    engagement: and he depart again for Africa, without propose

    marriage. Caroline and Hawkins correspond fitfully for some

    time; but then a startling combination of event cause Hawkins

    to penetrate far and far into the interior; a native

    village be burn, and a report, base apparently upon fact be

    circulate of his death. not until seven month have elapse be

    he able to return to England. he find Caroline marry to a man

    who have find her money useful. here the story, strong and

    move throughout, move steadily to the close, describe

    delicately and analytically the soul conflict of a man and a

    woman, sunder and separate, with a yearning for each other’s

    love.
